The Times (London): “ A collection of Australian stories,
giving a capital idea of life on the remote out-stations
of the eastern colonies in the last generation.” The
Spectator (London) : “ A brightl.v-written volume of the
trials and humours of colonial iife some thirty years or
more hack.” The World (London): “ They are full of
the-sound of the vagrant’s song of freedom, and are par-
ticularly fresh and unhackneyed.” The Weekly Sun
(London): “There is no more interesting writer about
Australia than Mr. William S. Walker, the well-known
* Coo-ee.’ ”
“from the Land of the Wombat."
The Daily Graphic (London): “ The stories are written
so naturally, and with such an appearance of truth, that
we are alinos!; inclined to believed that the author is
relating his own experiences rather than telling us tales
of fiction. Mr. Walker has great knowledge of his subject,
and is able to convey to bis reader a very realistic im-
pression of Australian life and character. The Stories are
of great variety, and many of them have a quaint humour
about them that is decidedly taking.” the Guardian
(London): “Mr. Walker has done excellent work in
‘ From the Land of the Wombat.,’ and he seems now to be
our finest Australian writer. Pie is always picturesque,
alwavs striking.”

THE RED PAGE.
"Green Fire.”
“There are those of us who would rather be
with Cathal of the woods, and be drunken with
green fire, than gain the paradise of the Holy
Molios who banned him, if in that gain were to
be heard no more the earth-sweet ancient song of
the blood that is in the veins ...
O green fire of life, pulse of the world !
O Love ! O Youth ! O Dream of Dreams ! ”
The Annir Choile.
What a phrase we have here—“ Green Fire”!
The soul of the sap in the trees in spring-time;
the blush in the cheek of the maiden ; the warm
rush of life that makes the birds’sing, and the
grass grow green over graves ! The man who has
this spell laid upon him cares not for Time, nor
Chance, nor black-winged Fate, nor pallid Death.
What can Time or Chance, or Fate, or Death do
to one who lives in his emotions, and who owns
the Future? He is no more free the troubles of
life than other men—less so, indeed, because of
his organisation—but through all he hears the
singing of the birds of Rhiannon, whose song
was so sweet that, as the old Welsh story goes,
warriors remained spell-bound for years together
listening to them.
This is not the type of man that builds bridges
and railways, and starts huge industrial establish-
ments, and, in a word, does, in a masterful way,
the hard, rough work of the world. He is not
even the kind of man who attends strictly to his
own business, and saves money, and ends mag-
nificently by becoming the mayor of his suburb.
Yet the world could ill spare him ; in fact, could
niuch better spare a score of average mayors.
True, the world is not over-anxious to let him
see, while he is alive, that it thinks there is
anything of value in him—anything worth speak-
ing about—fearing, possibly, that the know-
ledge might spoil him and unfit him for his lot in
life. But what the world thinks or does is of
little account in the case of a man of this sort.
He must travel on his own strange path, and dree
his own weird, in spite of everything.
To this rare class of men—even this rare
class of poets, as poets go in these latter days
of thrift and respectability-belonged James
Clarence Mangan. Most Irish readers pro-
bably know as much about him and his poetry
as I can tell them, but there must be many
to whom he is little more than a name, and
I doubt if he is quite that much to the Aus-
tralian native who is not of Irish descent. In-
deed, he was in his own country and generation
the most retiring of poets. But, apart from the
fact that he was a true singer, whose only mission
on earth was to sing, Mangan is particularly in-
teresting, because his genius was essentially Celtic.
He was Irish of the Irish—except in regard to
one quality—supposed to be inseparable from
the typical Irish character—drollery, to wit. He
had a certain amount of grim humour, but scarcely
a trace of the happy-go-lucky, devil-may-care drol-
lery of his countrymen. But he was none the less
a Celt because of this drawback. Even Celtic
Ireland, befoi-H the accursed Saxon came, was a
land peopled by many races. The Milesians were
simply the Normans of an earlier day and con-
quered the existing inhabitants of the island—
Celts of previous migrations—yet did by no means
exterminate them. Their descendants are alive
to-day. There is, in particular, one type of Irish-
man, to be found chiefly in the bogs and waste
places of Connaught, who is the original of the
long-lipped Celt so dear to the English carica-
turists. And, curiously enough, it Is this very
autochthon who is the true Irish wag—the born
humourist.
So that a man may be a Celt through and
through without having much humour or even
appreciation of humour in him. The Bretons are
Celts, the Welsh are Celts, and the Scottish High-
landers are Celts; but who ever accused anv oj
these peoples of a ra ee u f rollicking laughter-
ATr ’ - f J * jave kiiidred in Ireland for all that.
And o— 3 omt , onß Q f t h e se branches of the Celtic
ra ce Mangan may have belonged. So much for
his lack of humour, greatly deplored by some
critics who are on all other accounts his admirers.
But if Mangan was deficient in humour he had
all the other gifts which make the Celt the most
poetic of peoples ; and, within lyric limits, Celtic
poets the most truly poetic of the singers of all
nations. What these gifts are let no less a critic
than Matthew Arnold say:—“The Celt’s quick
feeling for what is noble and distinguished gave
his poetry style; his indomitable personality gave
it pride and passion: his sensibility and nervous
exaltation gave it a better gift still, the gift of
rendering with wonderful felicity the magical
charm of Nature. The forest solitude, the bub-
bling spring, the wild flowers, are everywhere in
romance. They have a mysterious life and grace
there; they are Nature’s own children, and utter
her secret in a way which makes them something
quite different from the woods, waters, and plants
of Greek and Latin poetry. Now of this delicate
magic Celtic romance is so pre-eminent a mistress,
that it seems impossible to believe the power did
not come into romance from the Celts. ” The por-
tion of the foregoing which concerns the magio
of Nature does not indeed greatly concern Man-
gan. He looked too much into his heart, and
wrote, to see the tenderer beauties of Nature ; but
the qualities mentioned in the first part of the
quotation, and above all, what Arnold elsewhere
calls “ the passionate penetrating melancholy of
the Celt,” were Mangan’s in the fullest measure.
But it is time to let him speak for himself—with
a word here and there from the writer by way of
introduction of the passage quoted. His marvel-
lous German translations cannot be dealt with
here. An article all to themselves would be little
enough to do them less than justice. In this place
he shall only speak in his own name and in the
nan.es of his predecessors, the ancient bards of his
country. Let one of the latter speak first—by the
mouth of Mangan, of course. It is O’Hussey, the
Bard of the Clan Maguire, chanting of his chief
who has gone on a dangerous expedition in the
depth of a severe winter. He begins—
Where is my Chief, my Master, this bleak night?
O cold, cold, miserably cold, is this bleak night for Hugh
mavrone !
Its showery, arrowy, speary sleet pieroeth one through
and through,
Pieroeth one to the very bone .
and, after thirteen more stanzas, giving a wildly
vivid description of a stormy night in a bitter
winter, concludes with a stanza, the last two lines
of which are full of fine old Irish ferocity.
Hugh inarched forth to the fight—l grieved to see him ao
depart;
And, 10, to night he wanders frozen, rain-drenohed, sad,
betrayed
lint the memory of the lime-white mansions his right hand
hath laid

The foregoing qiiotations are far too scant to giv
an idea of the spirit of these old Irish singers, bu ;
space is too short for more. So now let Mangai
speak a little in his own person. Three stanza
from “ The Time of the Barmecides ” :
My eyes are filmed, my beard is grey,
I am bowed with r.he weight of vears ;

tar back to the Past, though the thought brings woe,

The times of the Barmecides.
All the poets of all generations have, in their
turn, mourned for the Time of the Barmecides,
but which of them has ever mourned in sweeter,
sadder numbers than Mangan ? “ Twenty Golden
Years Ago” is in the same key of regret. But
the real Celtic Mangan—the Mangan who, as the
typical Celt in literature, seems to walk always in
a land of shadows, a region full of strange
whispers and dreams, between two worlds—is to
be found in the poem entitled “A Vision of Con-
naught in the Nineteenth Century ”
I walked entranced

Mangan was a master of rhyme who delighted in
the display of his skill. The result was that he
sometimes overdid the business in hand. But at
any rate there is no tedium in his measures. Note
here that Matthew Arnold states the weight of
evidence goes to show that rhyme was invented by
the Celts. Another feather in our cap !
Mangan had, curiously enough, much in corn-
mon with our own Kendall. Both were by nature
melancholy, both were masters of many metres,
and both were singularly devoid of humour. Even
in their outward lives the likeness holds good.
They were both unfortunate in a similar fashion,
though, in Mangan’s case; the gvening shadows
were darker, the night blacker, and the end more
tragic. Which brings me to Mangan the Man.
He was a poor lawyer’s clerk, struggling through
most of his life to support a mother and sister.
He was self-educated, yet highly cultured, and
learned in at least three languages beside
his own. He was a victim of drink and
As for his appearance, let John
Mitchell speak: “ Being in the college library
. . . . an acquaintance pointed out to me
a man perched on the top of a ladder, with the
whispered information that the figure was Clarence
Mangan. It was an unearthly and ghostly figure,
m a brown garment; the same garment (to all
appearance) which lasted till the day of his death.
Ihe blanched hair was totally unkempt ; the
corpse-like features still as marble, a large book
was in his arms, and all his soul was in the book.”
Another picture by John Savage: ‘He is about
the middle size, and glides more than walks, yet
at that is but infirm. He stoops and is abstracted.
A threadbare dark coat—is it brown or black?—
buttoned up to the throat, sheathes his attenuated
body. His eye is lustrously mild and beautifully
blue, and his silver-white locks surrounded,
like a tender halo, the once beautiful and now pale
and intellectual face of the prematurely aged
man before us. He glides along and through the
people as if he did not belong to them. His steps
seem as if they were not directed by any thought,
but mechanically wended their way to his
wretched abode.’’ Is this not painfully reminis-
cent of what is told of Daniel Henry Deniehy in
his forlorn latest days ?
. Mangan was found dying—whether of starva-
tion or cholera is not certain—in a garret, and
was taken to the Meath Hospital, where he
lingered for seven days and then died, an old arid
broken man at the age of 4(5. Yet in all the
Ireland of his day there was no other man in
whose bosom burned the Green Fire of life, of
love, of youth, of dreams, as it had burned in the
bosom of James Clarence Mangan,
VICTOR J. DALEY.

Brunton Stephens is busy writing plays, and
the business enchants him—he wishes he had tried
it thirty years ago.. One comic opera was sub-
mitted a while ago to J. C. Williamson, who
thought it “ too literary—readable and unactable ”
—though it is permitted to question the opinion.
A seeonl&K about to bid for London acceptance ;
and the others Time will try. By the way, a
Patchett - Martin preparatory puff in Litera-
ture (of the new collected Brunton which
Angus and Robertson are to issue shortly) de-
clares that “since the death of Gordon and
Kendall, J.B.S. has been recognised as the
leading representative poet of the Australian
Commonwealth "—which is a dictum to be well
shaken before taken. Stephens is a many-sided
man ; and there is a considerable argument to be
dressed showing that (if his brains had not been
stewed in the pan of pedagogy and the saucepan
of Govt, office—though Bunyip forbid we should
speak ill of such a sound bridge for carrying one
over life) his best literary work would have been
done in the prose or—who knows ?—the dramatic
field.

Taranaki (M.L.) Herald says the man who
financed Wellington Critic dead in its sweet
seventeenth number—lost £2OOO in that very-rash
venture. The Critic seemed a machine for the
glorification of its editor, J. S. Evison, and little
beside. The good things in it were some of
Evison’s stones reprinted (without acknowledg-
ment) from The Bulletin and The Australian
Mdguzme; but there is no reason why people
should be asked to read even good stories two or
three times over in different journals.

“It is the old melodiousness and the pleasant
nonchalance of a Lamartine which reappear in
Verlaine. Yet there is nothing in Verlaine to
intellectually recall the sane and noble outlook of
Lamartine, and one is still less reminded of a nr mil
seigneur in considering this poor - Bohemian who
passed his life in garrets and cafes. . . . We
must return, past Alfred de Musset, to the primal
source of modern French poesy, in order to find the
ancestor and the origin of Verlaine. Less fresh,
less healthy and strong, equally sincere, natural
and poetic, but more corrupted, he is lineally
descended from Francois Villon, that great rascal,
true vagabond, and true genius. Verlaine is
Villon in the garb of the 19th century dying.”—
Georges Brandes.

Lond. Bookman —apparently “at unheard-o;
expense,” so far as it is concerned—prints new
poems by Swinburne and Theodore Watts-Duntou
in its last number. Watts-Dunton’s is clever
Eleasant verse, with no poetic thrill to it. Swin
urne has a fine display of phrase-fireworks, in
which the phrase is too keenly felt. These lines
describe the delight of a swimmer who passes
from sunlight “far down through the fathomless
night of the water ” —
Death-dark and delic ous as death in the dream of a lover
and dreamer may be,
It clasps and encompasses body and soul with delight to
be Jiving and free ;
Free utterly now, though the freedom endure but the
space of a perilous breath,
And living, though girdled about with the darkness and
coldness and strangeness of death ;
Each limb and each pulse of the body rejoicing, each
tierve of the spirit at rest,
All sense of the soul’s life rapture, a passionate peace in
its blindness blest.
The title of the rhapsody is “The Lake of
Gaube ” —don’t know where Gaube is—and it
closes:—
As a sea-mew.’s love of the sea-wind breasted and ridden
for rapture’s sake
Is the love of his body and soul for the darkling delight
of the soundless lake ;
As the silent speed of a dream too living to live for a
thought’s space more
Is the flight of his.limbs through the still strong chill of
the darkness from shore to shore.
Might life be as this is and death be as life that casts off
time as a robe,
The iikeiiess of infinite heaven were a symbol revealed of
the lake of Gaube.
This recalls Lanier’s “ Marshes of Glynn

the tide comes in

of Glynn.
and indeed through the whole poem, though it re-
places irregular with regular metre, sings a ghost

many other fine books on a great many other
fine subjects—“We toil, we passion; balder
grows the pate; and Death and Tune in
silence close the pub.” It’s a shame to maltreat
Hebbleth waite so : and “pub.” is vulgar; but
is the best mixture —is there any best ?
“ Hop’s.” is highly recommended —two ounces of
“ Derby ” to two of “ Welcome Nugget ” ; but it
lies heavy on the sedentary brain. One ounce of
“Derby” suits better ; and Quinn says “ Wel-
come Is ugget ” alone. Barrie’s “Arcadia Mix-
ture ” is not sold in Sydney. Ogilvie, who leaned
very sadly over my garden-fence for half-an-hour
after smoking a certain dark cigar, inferentially
recommended “ Capstan ” in “ How We Won the
Ribbon”; but “Capstan'’ palls. What did
Marcus Clarke smoke ? , And are two little pipes
better than one big one ? These things have
troubled
The Bookfellow.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Ballade of the Other Woman.
Earth’s marriages, in Heaven made,
(The knots unblessed are tied in Hell),
Suggest that seraphs tire of trade,
And fling the series out pell-mell.
Results are quaint. Mere men rebel
Or squirm beneath their spouses’ thumbs j
But stand secure (where David fell)
Until the Other Woman comes.
The youth enamored wins the maid ;
The sexton jerks the marriage-bell:
Oh, sweetly is the bride arrayed,
And riohly rolls the organ-swell I
The honeymoon scarce breaks the spell
(O period of sugar-plums !)
He’s good as little Samuel,
Until the Other Woman comes.
So honestly our plans are laid,
We scarcely catch the funeral-knell
Of bridal passion. Prim and staid,
We scarce regret the Paphian dell.
Primordial promptings men may quell,
Count him a craven who succumbs,
Temptations righteously repel . . .
Until the Other Woman comes.
Penelope t we love thee well,
When brisk at eve thy spindle hums ;
Sincere our spousal voids we tell:
But then . . . the Other Woman comes /
T.
FRANK MORTON.
The Old and the New.
Naught is there here that hath halo of glory,
No boast of tyrants withstood ;
No knightly deed writ in legend and story—
Only the Fire, and the Flood !
No holy mem’ries that give the wind voices j
No song of victories won ;
No dear enchantment the Dreamer rejoices—
Only the Earth and the Sun !
Yonder the dark sombre forest is frowning,
Gumtrees, all gumtrees again !
Deep, breathless silence the senses is drowning :
Life seemeth empty and vain.
Oh ! this new land with its heart-haunting same-
ness,
Still in the Dawning it lies ;
Oh ! this new land with its dull, craven tameness—
Joy veils its sun-stricken eyes.
I love the land that is hallowed and hoary—
Dear is the ivy-olad pile ;
Proud is the vision—the pageant of glory—
Seen in the dim, ghostly aisle ;
Sweet are the fancies that come in the gloaming
Down in the dreamy old dell,
Down in the hollows where fairies go roaming.
What is there hero that hath spell ?
But ... it is Spring, and I waka from that mad-
ness.
Soothed by the rivulet’s flow;
Here in the deeps of the forest is gladness,
Here, where the fairy-ferns grow.
Never, oh ! never, was Life richer, sweeter
Here there are birds that have song !
Never, oh ! never, was Love truer, deeper—
Here there are joys that live long !
Naught that the heart of the Dreamer rejoice*,
Only the Earth and the Sun 1
Ah ! in the beat of the Hours there are voices,
Out of the Dawning they come ;
Not from the tombs of the Dead, but the Liv.eg,
Not from the Old, but the New ;
Singing, I hear them, exultantly singing,
Not of the False, but the True 1
Here is the past, with its joy, and its sorrow—
All that is worthy is ours,
Flowers that will garland the brow' of the Morrow
Lie in the lap of the Hours.
All that 1 found writ in legend and story,
Buried in ivy-clad pile,
All that 1 found in that vision of glory
Lives in this, fairy-fern isle.
y.
ANDREW SARD.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 25, 1899.
To Maoriland in GeneraL
Maoriland has a general election early next
month. The present democratic Govern-
ment, after nine years of continuous office,
applies for an extension which will ex-
pand its term of power to twelve years.
So far as The Bulletin can see, there is
nothing for any wise or sane community to
do save grant the extension by a big and en-
thusiastic majority. It is quite possible that
Maoriland would be the better for new blood
—new democratic blood that is—in its coun-
cils, for the jaded warriors who occupy the
Treasury bench have been there a long
time ; but that isn’t the question just at
present. If the country had an Elective
Executive—if it could displace the Seddon
Government without thereby putting the old
Tory hacks into office—if it could send in a
powerful democratic majority without neces-
sarily sending Hall-Jones and Thomas
Thompson and the other small weak fry of
the Ministry back to portfolios which are too
big for them—then the situation might be
different. In a better-ordered community it
would be different. It is an absurdity that,
if an alleged self-governing community wants
a democratic policy without Seddon, or
with some one else, it shouldn’t be able to
have it. It is also a parody on free institu-
tions that tlie country, if it is tired of
Seddon, has absolutely no means of indicat-
ing who, in the Progressive Party, it would
prefer to follow. But it is necessary to con-
sider things as they are ; not as they should
be. The country is asked to choose between
the Progressive Party, plus Seddon, and the
Unprogressive Party, plus Russell. There
is no new blood in the question. It is a case
of selecting the old successful veteran in
office, or the much older unsuccessful
veteran out of office - between the old Demo-
crat and the very aged Tory. A question
such as that should require little considera-
tion.
* * * *
Premier Seddon deserves ejection—
prompt and vigorous and permanent ejec-
tion—because of his steady drifting back to
the old, wretched, thriftless borrowing
policy, because of his ridiculous grovel in
the matter of the Transvaal Contingent, his
apparent tendency to fish for a title, and his
recently-developed weakness for singing the
National Anthem at times when that de-
pressed publication is wholly uncalled for.
That is, he would deserve ejection if there
was any man fitted to take his place who
wasn’t mentally afflicted the same way. But
there is no one. The Tory leader in particu-
lar is vastly more hopeless in the financial
sense than Seddon ; is quite as Jingo ; quite
as anxious for gauds and decorations ; and
his top-note in the National Anthem would
probably be even more awful to listen to
than his opponent’s. There is very little use
in ejecting one Imperialist if it is only done
to confer his billet on another. It is impos-
sible to make old Henry Scotland Premier,
in the absence of all other qualifications,
merely because he lifted up his voice against
the Government’s military folly. These
matters which ought to disqualify Seddon
from office for the rest of his natural life
must needs be overlooked, in the absence of
any available leader who doesn’t likewise
borrow and take fits of Jingo frenzy.
• * *
Otherwise Richard of Kumara deserves
well of the people. If they drop him they
will drop such a pilot as no other of the
Australias possesses. It is the fashion to
say in Seddon’s disparagement that he has
no constructive power of his own, and
merely carried out the ideas bequeathed to
him by his predecessor, Ballance. It may
be so, or it may not—at all events, from
whatever source Seddon got his ideas, he
had great ideas, and he carried them out
with rare vigor and intelligence and success.
If Ballance was his ghost then Ballance
was a remarkably good ghost, and the man
who has a ghost like Ballance should not
be recklessly exchanged for Russell, who
has no such spectral mentor to guide him.
And if all Seddon’s ideas were borrowed
from Ballance it is a rare gift to know how
to borrow intelligently, and how to make a
good use of the ideas thus dishonestly come
by. Judging by Russell’s past re-
cord he never comes by a good idea
dishonestly or otherwise, and if he did
he wouldn’t know what to do with it.
It is also the fashion to say that Digger
Dick’s day of inspiration is over ; that he has
done all the things he knows, and has
emptied his basket ; and therefore the coun-
try has no further use for him. That also
may or may not be true, but if it is true it
helps Russell’s case not one iota. If Sed-
don, because he has done great things, must
therefore be assumed to have exhausted his
stock of ideas and to be finished at the age
of 54, that is no proof that Russell, having
done nothing, is just about to begin at the
age of 61. Of course Seddon must have
fewer original ideas in stock than he had in
’93 for he has used up so many in the interval.
And Russell, having used up none of his
new political ideas in the interval, must have
as many now as he had then. But that
doesn’t prove that he has any valuable ideas
at all.
* * * *
The Seddon Ministry, either by its own
ideas or the ideas it borrowed, has made
Maoriland the most politically-progressive
country in the world. It has reduced sweat-
ing to a lower minimum than in any other
country that this writer knows—and he
knows a good many. It has established the
only national Old Age Pension system on
earth. It has constructed the most complete
and successful and democratic Factories Act
in the world. It has practically abolished
strikes—it can fairly claim to be the only
country existing which has constructed a
system of compulsory arbitration sufficiently
complete to make strikes obsolete. It has
gone to work systematically to discourage
absenteeism, and to break up large land
monopolies, and substitute for them small
independent proprietors or State lease-
holders. It has found out how to supply
cheap loans to settlers, and to do it on pay-
ing principles. It has given Maoriland One
Man One Vote and Woman Suffrage. It has
limited the hours of labor in shops and fac-
tories ; practically abolished child labor;
abolished the attachment of wages under
£2 a week ; ensured better accommodation
for shearers and farm laborers ; and done
other reforms too numerous to mention. In
such provinces as Queensland, N.S. Wales,
Tasmania and Westralia, where many even
of the smallest of these reforms appear still
an immense distance away, the Seddon
Ministry looks like a marvel of intelligent
progress. Not one of the four provinces
named ever possessed a Government which
did a tenth as much for the masses. But
Maoriland has grown accustomed to its good
fortune and takes it easily, and is half in-
clined also to throw it away easily from the
mere monotonv of possession.
* '* * *
The Seddon programme for the coming
election contains little that is new. The
Russell programme contains also little that
is new, and most of what is new in it is
wholly objectionable. The Russell party
proposes to wreck the State-leasehold idea
in its inception by allowing State lease-
holders to convert their holdings into free-
hold ; in other words, it proposes to set the
aggregation of big estates going again. It
proposes to take a Federal Referendum,
but it doesn’t pledge itself to carry out the
will of the people as expressed thereat if the
popular verdict doesn’t happen to suit its
views. It promises to reform the Council
by making it elective, but doesn’t state how
it is to be made elective. If it is made
elective on the narrow property basis which
has rendered the Victorian and Tasmanian
Councils such insuperable bars to all pro-
gress, then the reformed Council will be
forty times worse than the unreformed one.
And there is no semblance of a promise that
the present temporary Old Age Pensions
Act will be renewed when it expires. The
Russell party has assured the country over
and over again that it won’t repeal the pro-
gressive legislation of past years—the legis-
lation every item of which it declared to be
sinful and disastrous—though it already
proposes to partially repeal some of it by
allowing State leaseholds to be converted
into freeholds. But the Old Age Pensions
system will perish automatically, without
any repeal, unless a friendly Government is
in power to renew it. The Russell crowd
tries hard to look like such a Government,
but the effort is none too successful.
* • * *
And while there are few definite new ideas
in the Seddon programme, there is one
point to be considered which is above and
beyond mere programmes. Maoriland has
acquired a mass of valuable new legislation
in recent years, and it has panned out well
so far, but the new institutions have hardly
taken such deep root yet as to work auto-
matically. A great deal depends on the
mode in which they are administered during
the next, twenty years. The country values
these institutions ; it values them so much
that even their bitter opponents see their
only hope of climbing into office in assuring
the country that they won’t undo what has
been done. But they don’t promise—and if
they did the promise would be worth nothing
that they will administer the much-
denounced policy which they promise to
borrow from their opponents in a friendly
spirit—that they will administer it with the
single purpose of making it a success, and
thereby proving that the Seddon Government
was thegreatest reforming Ministry of theage,
and that they themselves, as its opponents,
were short sighted and narrow-minded
bigots. The interests of the Seddon Govern-
ment lie in making their progressive legisla-
tion a great success, and thereby gaining
credit for its initiation. The interests of
Russell and his followers lie in making the
Factory Acts and the State Land Bank and
the Old Age Pension system, and all the
other valuable measures that the province
has gained in recent years, break down by
insidious and subtle bad management, so
that they may put this epitaph on their joint
and several tomb r “We denounced these
things at the first and said they would not
work. They made a little preliminary
splash, but despite our best and most gener-
ous efforts to make them last, the inherent
rottenness of the principle tells in the long
run. We were true prophets—the only reli-
able, unquestionable, all-wool prophets in
this land of charlatans.” The theory that
any Russell Government would do its best
to prove its opponent to be a great, far-see-
ing statesman, and itself a tenth-rate incap-
able by making its opponent’s legislation—
the legislation it had cursed from A to Z and
back again—as great a success as possible, is
something past hoping for.
The Hour—and No Man.
}o ftll the public men of Australia, to all the
politicians ’ (abominable word ! smelling
of the abominable things it represents—
fools, liars, shufflers and tricksters, gulling
the people for a living—or worse, gulling
themselves), to all the men whom Austra-
lians have been taught to rely upon and loot
up to for counsel and leadership, there came
the other day a Great Opportunity. If it
had come after waiting and expectation, if it
had been known and canvassed beforehand,
if there had been three months, or six, to
discuss and consider it, then, mayhap, the
Hour would have found a Man. But it
came suddenly, like a thief in the night,
and in all this broad country there was not
one man of pre-eminent standing who em-
braced it, and rose to the level of it, and
made the Great Opportunity his own. The
Hour came, and passed, and there was no
Man in Australia—none !
* * * »
YV ar was announced between Britain and
the Lransvaal. The local spirit of militarism,
simmering in idleness, boiled at once to
fury. Every Australian province has its
militia- ostensibly and theoretically for the
defence of the province and of Australia.
I here is here, indeed, no other ground for
the existence of the military spirit and of
military armaments. For hundreds of years
to come Australia’s prayer to the world must
be merely that of Diogenes to Alexander :
“ Stand out of my sunshine ! ” We have a
vast territory, with the scantiest population,
the barest fringe of settlement. The obvious
thing to do, the only ideal of human well-
being and human happiness which is offered
to us, is to develop our territory, extend our
settlement, and create a prosperous, self-
centred, self-sufficient community. War will
not pay us. Bloodshed will not profit us. All
the military “ glory ” on earth is not worth to
us the content on the brow of one successful
pioneer, the smile in the eyes of one
Madonna of the Bush bending over her
candle-box cradle. We cannot covet our
neighbors possessions while our own posses-
sions are still too vast for our grasp. We
cannot afford, heavily in debt as we are, to
pay the mere money-tribute exacted by the
harpy of War, the modern woman of Babylon,
in whose worship strong men and strong
nations are made drunken. Still less can we
afford the loss of the brain and muscle for
which our waste lands cry out, upon which
our Present and our Future rely. So plain,
so urgent, is the need of Australia for peace-
ful development, that to squander money
and men in far-away lands for the aggrandise-
ment of Britain—that blood-stained Titan
of the nations—is the foulest treachery, the
grossest crime which could be perpetrated
against a young community. It is the rape
of infancy, the murder of Hope just-born.
* * * *
But the curse of militarism and of the
military spirit is that it can hardly ever be
held within the bounds of law and right,
even within the bounds of expediency. Give
a gun to the average man, and he wants to
shoot. Give him a sword, and he is never
satisfied till he has fleshed it in another
man. The hereditary tiger craves for blood ;
and though military convention makes a
pretence of agreeing that blood should only
be shed in a just cause, for defence of home
and liberty, the savage in us is always ready
to strip off his veneer of civilisation and
fight, burn, slay, devour for the mere
pleasure of it—for the satisfaction of the
ancient appetite to slay and devour. When
Chaka had drilled his corps of Zulus to
the utmost perfection he could attain, he led
them out to fight, and they clamored to be
led out. When Major-General French
has drilled his N.S.W. corps to a dim
perception of skirmishing drill and a
knowledge that the left foot goes first when
you can remember it, he sends them out
to fight, and they clamor to be sent out.
The one set of savages is as bad, or as good,
as the other.
* * * »
Since the military spirit persists, then, in
defiance of justice and right, all the more
reason for opposing it—not for crushing out
the brute and letting the tiger die, but for
keeping the brute in leash and training the
tiger to fight for his mate and his cubs and his
den rather than for the excitement of chewing
another tiger who has not attacked him, and
only asks to be left alone on his own hunting-
ground. All the more mason for men of pro-
bity and patriotism, citizens of Australia, to
plant firm feet on the welfare of theircountry,
and oppose firm voices to the insane fury for
shooting and killing, for war at any price, in
any rotten cause. All the more reason for
the men honored as leaders to stand firm
against the hysterical military tumult, and
to declare with tongue, and pen, and deed,
that Australia supports military forces for
her own safety and protection, not for wan-
ton aggression and the slaughter of men to
whom we are not hostile, who have never
harmed us and cannot harm us, and against
whom we have no need or reason to fight.
♦ * 9 m
But when Australian militarism rose in
tumult and demanded that Australia make
war upon the Transvaal, who was there
found to stand against it ? There were
scattered voices here and there, but in all
the Parliaments of the Australias there was
found not one man with more than a local
reputation to protest against the outrage
upon the Transvaal, the disgrace and crime
against Australia. The so-called leaders
approved silently or applauded shamelessly.
The Bulletin wishes it could believe
that some of them did not consider private
motives and personal ends. But this one,
democrat grown cynical, saw the title which
his wife longed for within her grasp, and
yielded to the military cry to send the men
away. This one, brain and heart sunk in
stomach, reckoned with his future, perchance,
and judged that, did he oppose the imperialist
clamor, preferment in his profession would
not come to him. That one, of whom an
astonished London paper, marking the
exaltation, notes wonderingly that he mea-
sures six feet and inches high, and weighs
18 stone (of jelly), went with the tide as a
jellyfish should. Yonder one, the typical
Thin Man turned Fat in the process of years,
welcomed the opportunity of realising the
Fat Man’s ideal—to see fat shibboleths,
fought for by somebody else at somebody
else’s expense ; and out-shrieked the shriek
ing “soldiers” themselves. And the crea .
tures of domestic politics, accidents of acci
dents, completed the pitiful tale.
* • * *
So Australia looks round for a leader—a.
Man. This Hour indeed is gone; but
another is coming—is here—the Hour is ;
always here. There is always place and
room for him who will realise the character
and destiny of this continent, and sacri-
fice himself willingly for the sake of
the Nation that we look for and long for.
Liberally endowed he must be—no ascetic of.'
the cloister, but a man of blood and passions-
and humanity, a man to tower above the
multitude and force their applause event
when opposing them—a man superior to the
chatter of club cliques and small political
coteries—careless ot reputation, careless of
profit, so that he do Australia’s good as far
as he sees it, attain Australia’s ideal as far
as he knows it. Of obloquy and detestation
he is certain ; he may easily fail, and die
with the derisive laughter of smug selfishness
ringing in his ears for passing-bell. But he
may succeed ; if he is great enough he ivill
succeed—for the heart of Australia is sound ;
and of him also some historian may write
what the American historian, Motley, wrote
of William the Silent —surely the proudest
epitaph ever given to mortal man—“ As
long as he lived he was the guiding-star of a
whole nation, and when he died the little
children cried in the streets.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Wanted—Another Contingent.
Now that the military madness is on these
provinces, and many usually intelligent indivi-
duals are sleeping in their uniforms, and Aus-
tralia is talking largely about “mobilisation”
and “ troopships” and such things, The Bul-
letin desires to point out the urgent necessity
for raising another contingent. For there is
another grievous wrong to be righted ; the fair
fame of the Empire is being smirched by tyranny
and oppression, and an ignorant, large-booted,
narrow-minded oligarchy is wiping its feet oil
the freedom of British subjects ; therefore it
is obviously time to interfere. What this paper
desires to point out is the urgent necessity for
raising 2000 men for the immediate invasion of
Westralia, and its chief reasons for so doing
are as follows :
(1) The Westralian and the Boer both inherited a coun-
try rich with mineral wealth, and they both mooned;
away the sleepy years raising- cattle, and wasting their
great opportunities. This shows they are unfit to possess-
that country any longer.
(2) Then the Outlanders came and made these two;
States the two great gold countries of the world, andi
created Johannesburg and Kalgoorlie.
(3) The Outlanders, in each case, outnumber the oltf
slow cattle-raisers by about two to one.
(4) The inrush of these strangers has been accompanied
by a great increase of rev enue. The Transvaal revenue
has risen inside 25 years from £BO,OOO to (on one occasion!
as much as £4,800,000. The W. A. revenue rose in the
same period from about £105,000 to close on £3,000,000.
(5) The taxation is alleged to be oppressive in both
States. In the Transvaal it has got as high as £l9 12s
per white inhabitant per annum. Westralia has extracted
from its miserable population as much as £2O or £2l per
white inhabitant per annum. But as Westralia produces
only one-fourth as much gold per annum as the Trans-
vaal, the load of taxation imposed by an ignorant and
narrow-minded Boer oligarchy in Westralia is really
heavier by comparison than it looks. }
(6) In each State the political power is carefully re-
tained in the hands of the handful of old settlers—the
dull bull-raising crowd which shows no capacity
for improvement and speculation, and wild-cattism
and share-jobbing, and such like soul-improving arts
and which even now moulders along in the old
fashion, and learns little, and forgets not much. But in
Westralia the case is worse than in the Transvaal The
Boers refuse political power to unnaturalised foreigners of
alien speech. The Westralian ’Gropers refuse it to people
of their own language and descent, their fellow-subjects
of the Empire. The Boers were driven to refuse it by the
knowledge that if they gave the franchise freely to the
new- foreigners the latter would use their political
supremacy sooner or later to bring about the annexation
of the Transvaal by an alien Power. The Westralians
know that they run no risk of being annexed by Germany
or France or any foreign power if they give equal political


rights to the Outlanders. The Boers were aggravated by
the fact that it was the British residents in their country
who incited the infamous British annexation of 1877, and
by the recollections of the Jameson Raid, the seizure of
the Diamond Fields, the high-handed annexation of the
Orange State in the '4o’s, the reward of £2OOO which
Britain put on the head of President Pretorius, and
many other wrongs. The Westralians have no such
excuse.
(7) In Westralia, one free British subject from the
Eastern States has only about one-fifteenth of the politi-
cal power of one’Groper. The ’Gropers inW.A.,bya
fraudulent manipulation of the franchise, and by their
fraudulent method of distributing the representation,
leave the great majority—the “ t’othersiders ” as they
are scornfully termed—wholly at the mercy of the hand-
ful of old settlers in political matters.
(8) There have been more t’othersiders murdered with
impunity in Westralia than there have Outlanders in the
Transva d. There has been no serious crime against
Outlanders in either place—still, the fact remains as
above.
(9) The ’Gropers, in addition to being unprogressive
(like the Boers), are cruel to the natives (like the Boers)
and practise a hideous slavery under the name of an
aboriginal “ indenture ” system. And they haven’t the
excuse that the Boers have. The ’Gropers never had to
fight for their lives against overwhelming native armies ;
their passions were never inflamed by war ; and they are
not living on the edge of a volcano with warlike black
men in overwhelming numbers around them. The Boer
might claim that any man was liable to grow' callous in
the years of terrible warfare during which he humped
the white man’s burden against stupendous odds. The
’Groper grew callous apparently because he was built
that way.
(10) Therefore in both countries a crowd of miners and
speculators brought civilisation and frauds and wild-cats
into a slow, peaceful agricultural community ; brought
about a huge increase of revenue ; and while forming the
bulk of the population, see practically all the political
power in the hands of the old slow minority. The Boer
hangs on to minority rule because otherwise he would
give away the independence of his country to a foreign
race that has always been his enemy. The ’Groper does
it because Oom Forrest has all the bad qualities of Oom
Kruqer without his justification.
Therefore, The Bulletin proposes that the
people of Kalgoorlie start a Reform Committee
as those of Johannesburg did, and lay in arms,
and practically take control of the town ; and
that they invite Kingston (who may pass for
Cecil Rhodes at the moment) to send an armed
force to their relief; and that there be an
invasion, and much murder of the ’Gropers;
and that the eastern provinces thereafter raise
2000 men to invade Westralia and redress the
grievances of the Outlanders. British citizens
are being trodden on in W.A. and taxed with-
out representation, and oppressed by a small
minority of farmers and pastoralists, and on the
Transvaal Contingent idea it is time to make
gory war on W.A. If the British flag is to
afford shelter and secure justice to all British
subjects, and isn’t to be a howling mockery,
then Redvers Buller is wanted at Hannan’s
Find, and the principles of justice need to be
upheld by force of arms at White Feather, and
the bombardment of Bunbury is so essential
that the essentialness of it is visible to the
naked eye of anybody and everybody.
The Disappearance of “ Gentleman One.”
Even in Victoria, where people of wealth and
standing are treated with intense respect, it is
impossible to any longer disguise the fact that
Joseph Henry Grey, of Geelong, solicitor and
ex-M.L.C., hasn’t been seen of late about his
usual haunts. As a matter of fact, the good
and eminent man isn’t; there. Considering that
his want of thereness began nearly six months
ago, the reticence which has been preserved
about the whole business is something almost
incredible. Grey was a self-made man, an ex-
Vlayor and one-time magnate of Victoria’s
House of 48 Rich Landlords, a man of many
schemes and enterprises, stupendous hospitality
and huge expenditure, and a solicitor of such
high standing that the trust-funds in his charge
ran to hundreds of thousands. Rumor states
the amount of the trust-funds that can’t be
found (possibly because the persons left in
charge don’t know where to look for them) at
£250,000. If more energetic looking fails to
bring these missing funds to light, the misery
that will fall on very many widows and orphans
will be something almost too dreadful to think
of. Grey either died suddenly and carefully
concealed his body, or was murdered,
or kidnapped—-or else he left ; and there
was either an unfortunate coincidence or a
dreadful swindle. Rumor has it that Grey is in
Europe somewhere, but in spite of the size of
the disaster that has followed on his disappear-
ance the Government has, up to now, made no
attempt to follow him. It is just thinking
of beginning to attempt something. Mean-
while this episode should prevent many hun-
dreds of aged and affluent citizens from
following in the footsteps of the aged and
alflueut fools who have preceded them, by
leaving the fortunes of their widows and
children in the hands of any solicitor or other
personal trustee, however noble his character.
It is only the trustee of noble character who
goes away with large sums, for he is the only
kind who gets large sums to go away with. In
the absence of a Public Trustee guaranteed bv
the State—an institution which only Maon-
land has been sensible enough to establish—
every aged and affluent citizen should remem-
ber that the poorest Trustee Co. in Australasia
is better security for the cash of the widow
than the best solicitor or other private person
in this hemisphere. The co. has at least some
paid-up capital, and from .£20,000 to ,£975,000
of callable capital; the private trustee
often hasn’t the former and never has
the latter. Its accounts are regularly
audited, and at least once a year the
securities have to be there—with the private
trustee they may be gone for years before any-
body is aware of the fact. If the manager dis-
appears, and the securities disappear at the
same time, it is dead sure that an excited chase
after that manager will begin inside a fort-
night. The directors, knowing that they are
liable for calls to the last penny of their shares
to make good the deficit would be very much
less supine than the Turner-Isaacs Minis-
try or the Vic. police. The manager of a
trustee co. can only do a great fraud by hood-
winking the directors and auditors ; the private
trustee only needs to hoodwink himself. Under
these circumstances the citizen who, at his death,
leaves the portion of his helpless dependents at
the mercy of any solicitor deserves to be sen-
tenced to carry bricks in the next world until
he has earn'd enough to make good the short-
age if there is one. If there isn’t one he should
carry brichs all the same to make good the
shortage there might have been.
Lying- Transvaal Cables.
Let Australians make no mistake—the cable-
grams published about the Transvaal war come
from a tainted source—the Rhodesian press-
agents in South Africa. Yet papers like S.M.
Herald persistently refer, in leading articles,
to the Edgar case and others as justifying the
war—ignorant or reckless that their supposed
facts are unworthy of credence. Edgar was a
drunken boiler-maker, a very powerful man,
who had struck down another Englishman and
almost killed him on the spot, finishing up by
battering his fallen foe against a wall. A mate
of the fallen man (also English) brought a
policeman. The case looked like one of murder
(it proved to be murder, for Edgar’s victim
died afterwards), and the policeman attempted
to arrest Edgar. The latter—a much more
powerful man than the officer—not only refused
to surrender, but attacked the policeman with
a stick; the latter fired in self-defence and
Edgar was killed. Almost any policeman
would have done the same. If the Dutch
officer hadn’t acted as he did the complaint
would probably have been that a British sub-
ject was killed (by Edgar) with impunity, and
that the brutal Dutch law made no sufficient
attempt to interfere for the protection of
British subjects when Edgar attacked them.
If the episode had happened in France or
Germany, or anywhere but in the Transvaal,
the Herald’s verdict would have been
“Served him right.” The burglars, Mont-
gomery and Williams, resisted arrest with
an iron bar in Sydney, maiming a police-
man, and were hanged for it, with the com-
plete approval of the Herald and the rest of
the Jingo press ; and George Dibbs, who de-
nounces the Boers, straightway armed the
Sydney police with revolvers, that they might
shoot men resisting arrest as Edgar was shot.
Amiable, consistent George Dibbs ! And be
it noted that the Transvaal law did punish the
Boer policeman —quite as heavily as the case
called for ; and it compensated the widow of the
man who was killed while resisting arrest after
hideouslymaltreatingaßritishsubject. Another
“ Boer outrage” was cabled as committed on
an Englishman named Lanham, who was said
to have been kicked to death with spurred
boots. Then Lanham turned up alive and
well; and few of those who shuddered at the
blazoned “outrage” ever saw the tiny con-
tradiction. The Australian Jingoes are
more gullible and more biassed than the
rank-and-file of the British themselves.
Boers are a rough race, but manly; and their
“outrages” exist mostly in the imagination of
the lying Rhodesian press agents, busily fan-
ning the flames of war. The same tactics were
pursued in 1881, when Sir George Colley—
who was a gallant man, if an unfortunate
general—wrote to his Govt. —“I hate this
atrocity-manufacturing, and its effects on the
men, tending to make them either cowards or
butchers.” Let Australians make no mistake
—the cablegrams about the Transvaal come
from a tainted source.

Public Duty v. Private Profit.
Melbourne Tramway Co., which is fighting
Fitzroy municipality over the rating of its lines,
and proposes to carry its appeal to the Judicial
Committee of the House of Lords, has taken
the masterly step of feeing Vic. Attorney-Gen.
Isaacs to argue its case" there. From the
Irani way Co.’s point of view the nobbling,—or
at least muzzling—of a Minister is an adroit
and dexterous move, but the people who
pay Isaac A. Isaacs to do their work and
look after their interests will probably fail to
perceive the beauties of the proceeding. The
point on which the appeal lies—the rating of
the lines—is one of the three concessions
recently demanded by the unspeakable co. as
the price of a reduction in the hours of labor
of its employes from GO per week to 56. The
Tramway Bill is still before Vic. Assembly,
and the Tramway Co. is still fighting all
it knows for an extension of its mono-
poly a monopoly which sweats and
directly plunders, not only its employes,
but the general public, whose paid servant
Attorney-General Isaacs is, and also the muni-
cipalities, against whom the said Isaacs is
engaged to appear. These facts brand the
action of Isaac A. Isaacs with even more of
disgrace and venality than if an ordinary public
servant had accepted gratuities for work out-
side the department. The public sees one of
its most necessary servants temporarily dele-
gating his duties to an assistant or to one of
his colleagues, while he takes service for six
months or more in the employ of a private co.
against the public which pays him. And while
this public servant takes private pay for
appearing against the public he will presum-
ably, after the manner of privileged lawyers in
such positions, still draw the public’s wages.
The Vic. Government, of which Isaacs is a
moving spirit, has persistently blocked the
way of those members who were trying to
force the Tramway monopoly to keep the law,
and the public has greatly wondered why.
The public, which is a brutally-direct animal,
lacking in the niceties of gentlemanly expres-
sion, and utterly incapable of understand-
ing legal etiquette, will probably leave off
wondering now. It will assume, in its
coarse, pig-headed fashion, that Isaacs’
profitable job of representing the Co. in its
Privy Council appeal, is the payment —the
gross, material consideration—for which the
Turner-Isaacs Government allowed the Co. to
shamefully break the law as against its em-
ployes, and for which they refused to assist
those who were trying to make the Co. keep
faith. Of course the assumption will be totally
unwarranted, but it is the utter shamelessness
of Attorney-General Isaacs and the Turner
Ministry which will be responsible for the pub
lie’s conclusion. If Minister Best had eor-
ruptly put 25 of his relatives over the heads <>i
their seniors into the public service, it woid
have been a smaller scandal than this.
Maoriland’s Midland Railway Curse.
Premier Seddon of Maoriland grows somewhat
disappointing in his old age. Witness these
remarks on the Midland railway :
As to future developments, it had been suggested thiu
the Government should guarantee the interest, and that
a company should he formed to take the line off the
hands of the colony. With the legal difficulty out of the
way, and the matter placed before the share and deben-
ture-holders, he was sure that capital could be raised for
the purpose indicated. In his opinion the Slate would
shortly he cleared by the highest tribunal in the empire,
and it would then be for the share and debenture-holders
to decide whether they would lose all or submit a scheme
for continuing the line. He concluded by expressing the
opinion that the enterprise would be a good thing for a
company.
After all the trouble Maoriland has gone
through with one Midland railway syn-
dicate any man who seriously proposes
another one should be suppressed. The co.
spent .£1,200,000 and had only £650,000 worth
of work to show for it, and the State paid
£250,000 towards that in land grants. So the
co.’s line really cost, through excessive office
and other expenses, nearly three times what
the State could have built it for. At this rate,
if a syndicate builds the line and the State
guarantees the interest, it will simply
have to pay three times as much inter-
est as it would if it built and owned
the line itself. It will be practically a
case of being responsible for 9 per cent, interest
instead of for 3 per cent. And the province
will owe the money just the same either way.
If a Maoriland railway is built with English
capital borrowed by a syndicate it is just as
much the country’s debt as if it was built with
English capital borrowed by the State. Maori-
land has to find the interest in fares and
freights and otherwise, either way, and that is
the test question. The people who have to pay
the interest owe the money—in fact, if not in
name.

Minister Best and His Brother-in-Law.
The contemptible “ Jackson Case ” was due
for final discussion in Vic. Assembly when
The Bulletin went to press. Many things
have happened since Minister Bob Best de-
manded an inquiry touching his brother-in-
law’s career in the Lands Department, and a
brief re-statement of the case will not seem
superfluous. Jackson, it appears, was trans-
ferred from the Postal Department to the
Lands Department, in 1896, and after six
months of supernumerary service under his
brolher-in-law’s banner, he was “ put into a
position of responsibility over officers who were
his seniors.” General discontent arose from
the alleged unfair advancement of Jackson,
and naturally enough Ministe.r Best was held
to be the real cause of the grievance. Also,
naturally, Graves, M.L.A., the “foxiest”
member of the Assembly, made it his business
to collect evidence, and make a fuss over
the matter. Whereupon Minister Best com-
mitted himself to an extravagant burst of
virtuous indignation, which left him no alter-
native between proving his “complete inno-
cence” of the charge of nepotism or retiring
from the Ministry. Hence the appointment of
a select committee to consider all the Jack-
sonian facts. The question as to whether
Minister Best had committed an unpardonable
offence in thus furthering the interests of his
relative (if he did further them) was not raised
at all. Had it been put to the select committee-
men they would have been bound to admit that
paid bosses in all walks of business life are per-
mitted a little license to favor a particular
friend or relative if the favored one be a capable
and respectable servant of the “ firm,” and
although the Civil Service is supposed to
be run on an almost entirely mechanical
system without regard for individual friend-
ships and relationships, the committee in this
Jackson inquiry would doubtless have found
Minister Best guiltless of any iniquity in favor-
ing his brother-in-law, presuming that the
nepotism were conspicuously plain. But, inas-
much as Mr. Best professed to abhor the very
thought of a boss making a distinction between
his own brother-in-law and other people’s, it
was necessary that the committee should declare
him to be wonderfully innocent or shockingly
otherwise. In a vague, negative sort of way,
the committee found him innocent. Its re-
port to Parliament is the one unmistakeably
scandalous feature of the Jackson case up'
to time of writing. Mr. Graves’s indictment
of the Minister was divided into clauses, or
charges, to vVhich the committee has furnished
curt and inconsistent replies. It states that
clerk Jackson’s sudden promotion caused dis-
satisfaction among the older officers in the
Lands Department; it acknowledges that
when the Minister’s confidential representative
(Secretary Morkham) was appealed tore Jack-
son’s undue advancement, Morkham said, “It
is so, you have my sympathy ” ; but, all the
same, those select committeemen advance no
reason or suggestion for the job which gave rise
to Morkham’s remark. Also they ignore cer-
tain evidence given by chief clerk Skene,
which, if true, practically convicted Secretary
Morkham of having put Jackson on the promo-
tion list at the request of his brother-in-law.
Evidence strongly corroborating Gfaves’s main
charge came out unexpectedly during the en-
quiry, but the report of the committee ignores
these vital points. Looking at the disingenu-
ous document in the light of an extra draft
report drawn up by the chairman of this select
tribunal, the public—such as take any interest
in the matter—will come to the conclusion that
a very small matter has been distended into a
rather large scandal. The committee’s attempt
to cling to Best without pretending to despise
Graves is an unmitigated failure.

The “Patriots” Who Won’t Pay up.
'• Now is the time for every British citizen to
rally round the flag ! ” Half-a-dozen Aus:
tralian Premiers, Chief-Justice Darley, Chief-
Justice Madden, Chief-Justice Griffith —all
men bedizened with Imperial titles—and a
score of minor persons in authority assure Aus-
tralians that it is their duty and privilege to
whoop up the Dear Old Mother-in-law Country
in her campaign to grab the Transvaal mines
and crush the liberty of the little Boer repub-
lics. Then why don’t the rampant “ Patriots”
pay up? There was a splendid “ Meeting of
Citizens” on Nov. 2at Sydney—a “Signal
Demonstration,” said S.M. Herald— “ Magni-
ficent Enthusiasm ” —yet after nearly three
week’s hard battling, with half-a-dozen “ lead-
ing newspapers ” howling behind it, theN.S. W.
“ Patriotic Fund ” only amounts at Nov. 20 to
.£3933, a sum which invested safely at interest
would yield at the most £l6O a year—just
about enough to keep in comfort one
wooden - legged ex - soldier. And in the
last few weeks the local Presbyterian body
has collected .£B4OO for a purely local
object—the extension of the Presbyterian
church ! “ When the Empire calls ” these
patriots ” and meets with such a beggarly re-
sponse, what on earth does the Emnire think
about them ? They open their mouths wide ;
but how tightly they shut their pockets ! Look
on this picture and on that ! (The Transvaal
figures are those advertised in S.M. Herald to
Nov. 20 ; but these may be misleading, because
some of the “ distinguished ” citizens may have
chosen to give their modest £5O anonymously,
blushing to find i; fame. As there are practi-
cally no anonymous donors so far, however,
The Bulletin assumes the Herald figures,
with their significant omissions, may be taken
as correct; and if they are not it will be proud
to correct them.) Here are a few significant
comparisons :

And so on, down a long, long list. The wealthy
Sydney Daily Telegraph gave £26 ss. to
its own Lancers’ Insurance Fund, which closed
with a miserable total of £6so—only half the
amount asked for ; but gave nothing to the
Patriotic Fund. Nothing has so far been seen
of the Sunday Times subscription, though the
Sunday Times weeps on the neck of the Empire
every week with shocking regularity. The
military firm of LASSETTERand Co., which raised
£55 for the Soudan fund, is represented only
by £lO 10s. contribution from Col. Lassetter to
the Insurance Fund—nothing to the Patriotic
Fund. Yet the Soudan business was mere
play - acting compared with the Trans-
vaal war. Like the King of Spain with all
his men, the Soudan contingent marched up
a hill and down again—or something to that
effect. The Transvaal war is real war —against
men with guns, who can shoot ; not a mere
skirmish with a few hundreds of miserable
Arabs. For the Soudan fund, when the Empire
didn’t call for it, between £30,000 and £40,000
was raised in a few weeks. But—that was the
nasty part of it—the “patriots” who promised
their thousands and hundreds, really had to
pay up ; though some of them tried hard to
dodge payment, and were greatly relieved when
in the long run they got most of their money
back again. The bitter memory of that
paying-up has been with a lot of them for 14
years—and now the Patriotic Fund is down to
collecting the miserable pence of the Sydney
“working and factory girls”—the poor little
wretches with wages of ss. and 10s. a-week.
Bah ! Booh ! Come, Messieurs the Patriots,
this Transvaal contingent is going to cost
N.S. W. no end of money ; and some
of you are making nice little pickings
in the way of shipping contracts, fodder sup-
plies, and food deals. Don’t shove it on to the
taxpayer ! Don’t shove it on to the wretclmd
little factory girls ! If your “ patriotism ” is not
sounding brass, if your words are not empty
wind, pay up ! You have abetted and en-
couraged Australians in going to shed their
blood in a disgraceful foreign war ; can’t you
—who stay at home in safety and shriek for the
Empire—can’t you shed a little of your money?
Come on ! ye foaming “ patriots ” and frothing
orators, ye warlike auctioneers, importers and
shipping agents—come on ! The Empire calls,
and the treasurers of the “ Patriotic Fund ” are
wondering where the devil they are going to
get subscriptions.
A Simple Little Outrage.
“ Cockney ” champions cash-coupons :
A proposal to abolish the “ trade coupon ” system in
Victoria by Act of Parliament has thusfar attracted very
little notice from the non-shopkeeping: public. The Lev.
Council seems to have started an enquiry into the Aboli-
tion Bill, and barrister Coldham is appearing- before a
select committee of Fatmen to show cause why the pre-
cious Bill should be squelched, and but for this enquiry
the Vic. community in general would not have thought
twice about the matter. As soon as the average citizen
possessed of the average small intellect brings hunself to
think twice concerning the coupon system he can’t help
perceiving that he is being treated as a slave, worm and
nincompoop by certain of the Powers that Be. The mere
mention of a Bill to suppress coupon-trading sounds like
a Tory joke, yet this legislative outrage is likely to be
perpetrated with the consent of an alleged democratic
body —viz., the Trades Hall Council. Wherefore
one would like to know whether the Trades Hall
had sold its immortal soul to a ring of bi«- ‘hop-
keepers, or is too tired to understand where the
virtue of the coupon system comes in. It is essen-
tially a cash system, calculated to suit the business nf the
smaller traders. They buy bits of paper outright from
the coupon co., and offer them as an inducement for
their customers to pay cash for goods, instead of “tun-
ning on account,” When a customer has acquired
coupons to a certain amount (on face value) he or she—
it is usually she—exchanges them for goods of the same
face value at the coupon co.’s store. In short, this system
is an extension of the old tea-gift shop system the gift
part of the business being conducted by the co ’ whereas
the tea shops used to manage the gift department then-
selves. Into the question whether the public gain or lose
by this system, or the shopkeepers who adopt it gain an
advantage over their rivals who don’t adopt it Parlia-
mentarians have positively no excuse for.’ pokin«-
their mean provincial noses. They have no more
right to interfere with the sale of goods plus bits of
paper to an ordinary retail purchaser who knows what
he, or B he—particularly she-is about, than they have
to pass a Bill for the suppression of “ Clearing' Sales ” '
and Alarming Sacrifices.” The coupon transaction as
between company and shopkeeper, and shopkeeper and
cash customer, is quite clean and respectable. It is even
admirable as far as it encourages prompt cash dealing at.
the expense of “ tick.” And even if it didn’t run on cash
lines it would be none the less the public’s own pai ticular
funeral, and none the less a matter in which the Legisla-
ture has no sort of right to interfere. But it is a ca-li
system, and because it does not suit the book 0 -

About the last act of the M.L. Government

Ttie second reading of a Bill to legalise the

thau any other province—only illegally, and
by the corrupt connivance of the police the
principal difference will be that the thing will
be honest and public, instead of up a back lane
and dishonest. The present illegal tote lias to
be dishonest, for if it didn’t swindle its patrons
it wouldn’t have enough money to bribe the
police with. The saddest voice raised against
the machiue was that of C. G. Hkydon, who
held that the £50,000 a-year which Govern-
ment would probably make out of the business
would be the price of iniquity—though why
N.S.W. shouldn’t grasp at the price of iniquity
with both hands, it is difficult to see. Iniquity
isn’t so beautiful a thing that it should be let
off paying its share of the public burdens. Be-
sides a country which lives so largely upon
drink duties, upon fines levied as the price of
drunkenness, assault, obscene language, theft,
wife-beating, and cruelty to animals, has got
past being so particular.
Persistent Murray Smith is just now hoping,
or pretending that he hopes, to block the \ io.
Factories Act Amendment Bill with a proposed
Parliamentary enquiry into the working of the
previous Act. Mr. Smith wants to abolish
the minimum-wage clause in order that the
local manufacturer may be able to hold his own
on the Australian market. Also, he desires to
secure the local worker against the temptation
to tell falsehoods re the wage he actually gets.
The situation, as depicted by Murray Smith,
is truly sad. Firstly, the manufacturer stands
to be damaged because he pays the minimum
wage, and, secondly, the worker is compelled
to perjure himself because the manufacturer
doesn't pay the minimum wage—he has to say
that the minimum wage is paid when it.isn t.
Hence the anxiety of Murray Smith to injure
the trade by ensuring truthfulness in
the worker, in which case the worker
must suffer likewise. As long as the
manufacturer is “ handicapped,” says Smith,
by the compulsion to pay a higher wage than
the wage-earner receives, says Smith, so long
will the export trade be in danger of “ ultimate
extinction,” whereas if the worker were not
bound to perjure himself things would remain
exactly as they are now. In short the present
evasion of the Act, according to Smith, is so
satisfactory that it ought to be enquired into
on behalf of the worker whose occupation would
be mostly gone if lie didn’t tell a lie and stick
to it. “ For if a product is not worth what is
paid for it in wages, how,” asks Smith, “can a
manufacturer go on paying the minimum wage
which —says Smith— is not paid to the men
who are not able to earn it? ”

The above vague remarks were suggested
to some extent by the graveyard scene in “ Buried
Alive,” at Sydney Criterion. The Cri. is under
new management, Douglas Ancelon being the
sub lessee, and the drama is by a local author,
whose name The Bulletin will give next week, if
he thinks lie can stand it. The local man’s melo-
drama is very, very awful in places, but it must be
admitted that it is better than sundry im-
ported articles that the suffering city has been
struck with. Its principal fault is also the prin-
cipal fault of most other melodramas ; there is so
much crime'to be crowded into three hours that
the perpetrators haven’t time to have motives.
An adequate motive takes some little space to
develop"; and when there is so much to do, and so
little time to do it in, it is impossible to seriously
develop anything. It is a case of Macbeth, with-
out leisure to have a reason, throwing a brick at
Duncan on general principles as soon as the cur-
tain goes up. The medical gentleman in “ Buried
Alive ” wants his girl-ward out of the way because
he has got through most of her money, which is
intelligible enough. But the girl who pours
poison into the other girl’s glass does it on the
spur of the moment, for no reason in particular ;
and the balloonist is quite friendly with his
partner one moment and sticks a dinner-knife
in him immediately after merely because he
asks for .£2O. Also his conduct in tying the girl
hand and foot and taking her up in the balloon
lacks adequate cause, and the conduct of the doc-
tor in strangling the other doctor and putting his
remains in the box: - sofa is painfully sudden.
Sweeney’s object in getting up as Napoleon Bona-
parte, too, and being robbed of his money by five
girls with bare legs, is wrapped in mystery—in
which respect it differs from the five girls, who
are wrapped in hardly anything.
* * * *
There is nothing particularly gruesome
about the tomb scene after all, except that the
door of the tomb refuses to keep shut. In this
respect it resembles the box with the dead doctor
in it, said box also refusing to keep shut. The
worst member of the co. is the yacht on which the
hero has staked all his money, and which loses
the race because the man in the balloon has hired
a despicable character to cut the main-sheet and
somebody has poured vitriol on the main-sail.
Said yacht bounds over the intermittent wave in a
fashion which calls for severe scorn. The best
character is undoubtedly Harry Diver, whose
rendering of the murderous doctor is a very fine,
strong, and wholly commendable piece of work.
Very few artists could save that incoherent
strangler from ridicule, but Diver hauls him out
of the abyss of derision by sheer talent and
energy. Miss Roxy Barton, Percy Walshe, Cross,
J. 11. Nunn, Miss Fanny Wiseman (who makes
an admirable old woman) and Miss Hope Mayne
all call for respectful comment. Miss Mayne is
an especially promising young actress who
deserves better luck than this tomb-yarn will
ever give her.

The one-week revival of “La at
Iler Majesty’s (Sydney), will come to an end to-
morrow night. One-week revivals are merely an
aggravation to a weekly paper which doesn’t come
out to illuminate the dark public intellect till
Thursday, therefore it is pleasing to record that
“Robin Hood,” a new opera which intends to
make a longer stay and give more time for con-
sideration, is due on Saturday. During its very
temporary re appearance, however, the doll-opera
has given large and general satisfaction. Looking
back into the dim recesses of time, this eminent
dramatic authority has an impression that Miss
Dorothy Vane has put a few finer touches on her
character as the big doll than she did aforetime.
Anyhow, it is Miss Dorothy’s best character, and
it is done so admirably in every detail that it
would be difficult to suggest improvement any-
where. I’anil's holy but impecunious father
is also about his best character—what there is of
it ; there isn't much of it, but while l’aull
is on the stage he is a gladsome father.
Ketmingham is admirable ; and Lauri toils
manfully at tbe thin streak of humor allotted
to him as llilarius, the toy-maker. There is much
pleasant music in the dull-opera, and the fact that
Caron's bald spot glows cheerfully at the con-
ductor’s desk is a sufficient guarantee that the
music receives full justico. The gleam of Caron’s
bald spot is a lamp on the path of art and a
radiance on the highway of melody. Also, in case
The Bulletin should do him an injustice, it
hastens to explain that the spot isn’t really halo.
The talented musician is merely becoming visible
through his hair, so to speak.

“ Christopher Columbas” still does paying

# * * *
Out of respect for the scenery and fixing of
Holloway’s new drama at Melbourne Royal, The
Bulletin would like to think “ The Power and
the Glory” were not a deplorably foolish thing to
spend so much money on. Scenic artists Clint and
Vaughan have done some fine work for this penny-
dreadful drama. Each act contains exceptionally
effective “sets.” The midnight view of London
as seen from the top of the monument is about the
most striking picture ever produced at the Royal.
Then a nice little girl angel breaks through the
smoky heavens and beams upon the principal
villain until lie drops senseless instead of throwing
the angel’s mother off the monument aforesaid.
The end of “The Power and the Glory” is an
orthodox transformation scene, showing how the
heroine earns eighteen pence a night by posing as a
central figure of Rule Britannia, whilst a blood-
thirsty demi-mohdaiue poses near her in the red
tights of sin.
* * * •
It is a drama with no sane moments in its
career. People meet together, and follow one
another, and form friendships and hatreds and
tableaux, under absolutely preposterous circum-
stances. The angel in the sky-hole is, perhaps, the
least unconvincing item in the plot ; otherwise the
drama runs on lunatical lines from first to last.
The low-comedy mother, who keeps a coffee-stall
near the Mansion House, has an improper daugh-
ter in gorgeous array' and a virtuous daughter who
sells floral buttonholes for a living, and talks of
having gone to school with two wearisome idiots
apparently 20 years older than herself. Also,
there is a virtuous brother, just back from sea,
who loves the wife of a burglar, and this burglar
is led into the housebreaking business by the
wicked sister’s “ mash.” The wicked sister
and her evil lover waste much time trying
to destroy the other burglar’s wife, body
and soul. 'They pop up casually in her
humble garret, and hope that her blind
child will die, but after the child deceases the
villain is haunted by visions of her blue eyes and
golden hair, simply because he had given her a
shock by robbing a church in the second act when
her mother somehow got mistaken for a graven
image. Intermingled with the prevailing crime
and misery are a proud countess and her gussy
offspring who loves the smudgy', but virtuous,
daughter of the coffee-stall proprietress. T’or a
long time Lady Gwendoline reckons that the gulf
between the classes and the masses is too broad to
be bridged by a coffee-stall, and when she finds
her gilded heir offering marriage to the Hower-gal
in the garret where the blind infant is about to
die, she threatens to disown him. Subsequently
the proud aristocrat relents in the “Hies” of the
Burlington Theatre, and the Hower-gal, who is now
in tights, and the mother from the korfee stall,
who has been engaged to play the hind legs of an
elephant, or something, are embraced by tier nibs
because their kind hearts are more than coronets.
Meanwhile one burglar has gone mad and murdered
the other burglar, and the wicked sister's scheme
for destroying the blind child’s mother has fallen
through. Everything ends as happily as could be
expected.
* • • *
“The Power and tho Glory ” is well acted
in some places. John Saunders, Miss Dora NVin-
ton, Frank Hawthorne, little Miss Holloway, and
Miss Rose Conroy deserve a much better drama.
Miss Watts-l’hillips, W. J. Montgomery, and
Stanford are moro satisfactory than somo other
members of the co. ; Miss Alice Deorwyn makes a
pretty' little figure in tights ; and nobody is a
downright inHiction except J. I*. O’Neill. Tho
present scribe has a notion that O’Neill is a useful
Irish comedian when he gets the chance, but as a
noisy sham cockney, in humorless parts, he merits
scorn and derision. The time has now arrived for
requesting O’Neill to “cheese it,” whatever that
may mean.

A matinee benefit to J. F. Cathcart will be
given at the Princess’s on Thursday, the 30th inst.
“ Old Jimmy ” Cathcart’s right to be benefitted in
the 72nd year of his reign is quite beyond dispute.
He is one of the comparatively few actors who
ever ought to have taken to the stage, and he has
been a long standing credit to the profession in
Australia, where salaries for the local “ all-round ”
man don’t run extravagantly high. Managers
lately have not wanted a Henry the Sixth, Sir
Peter Teazle, Frar Lawrence, or Old English
comedy father, therefore the public have lost sight
of J. F. Cathcart.
* * * *
As Brough’s season atMelb. Princess’s lasts
only a few weeks, “Gay Lord Quex”must pack
up his wicked past and retire into private life after
Friday. Meanwhile the fame of Pinero’s bold,
unflinching play is loud in the land, and a desire
to go and blush at its candour appears to be pretty
general. All the same “ Quex ” can be absorbed
without a blush by any practical person. It isn’t
gratuitously spicy, like “The Sign of the Cross,”
for instance. The grev-haired marquis, captured
by the “usual creamy English girl,” and reflecting
that it is very pleasant to be a good boy when one
has arrived at 48, is a cleaner moral lesson than
the Roman Prefect in an abbreviated tunic. The
religion of common sense sticks out of “ Gay Lord
Quex”at all points. Everybody’s folly finds him (or
her) out, more or less. The Duchess gets a shock
which probably upsets her digestion, and the buck
from West Africa suffers awful headaches as the
result of looking in at the club. Also the Captain
who hugs Sophy in the wisdom of his own conceit
loses a rich wife, and a pair of solitaires which are
duly exchanged for a dog’s collar. After “ Quex”
come six nights and a Wednesday matinee of
“The Physician,” commencing on Saturday (25th)
