rue,
The owl who sings his carol on the mournful willow tree,
The ashes in the urn, and the ivy and the yew,
And the crow that ponders wisely where the dead men be;
And the corpse so gay and free, and the cheery gallows
tree,
And the ghoul that blooms in spring-time to gladden you
and me.
Oh! the green ghoul in the spring-time that gladdens
you and me.
The lightsome skull and pleasant, with eyeholes large
and bright,
The Thing that crawleth lightly as he crawleth in and
out.,
The pine-tree and the toad, and the broken column
white,
The slimy, sodden bank and the grey gra=s round about.
The long worm that hath no turning as he hasteth to his
meat,
And the pleasant caterpillar with his soft green feet.
Oh! the happy caterpillar with his damp and noiseless
feet.

The London patriotic crowd, which never fights itself,
but is always hollering for the other fellow to wade in
gore, was made a fine ass of by a music hall manager
who dressed a “canary ” in the uniform of the N.S.W.
Lancers and instructed him to sing “Soldiers of the
Queen ” from a private box. The dodge succeeded. The
Jingoes thought that it was really one of the Lancers who
was suffering very badly from their brand of patriotism,
and even when the same man arose nieht after night to
sing the same ditty, the audience did not “ drop.”

Charles Arnold from Johannesburg, as reported in
Melbourne Taiu.k Talk: -“Mr. Charles Arnold, whilst
keeping neutral on the subject of Boers and British,
sticks up for the Boers. He says that, the Boer is not so
black as he is painted. He and his company receded a
great deal of kindness from the Boers during their
theatrical tour. The lion. Mr. Menzies was particularly
thoughtful regarding their welfare. He promised free
passage and protection to the coast in case of war; his
wife, Mrs. Menzies, called on Mrs. Arnold with a basket
of roses and 50 eggs, and when little Edna Arnold was
ill some Boers in the vicinity sent fresh milk twice a day.
There was not the slightest ill-feeling shown, although
all the company were English. Kruger never goes to the
theatre, but his son paid two visits to see ‘What Hap-
pened to Jones.’ Mr. Arnold contrasts the condition of
Johannesburg and Pretoria, the former hysterical and
wildly excited, the latter calm and unemotional, as if
there was no thought of war.’’
Recently-deceased Charley Burford was for some years
coach to Sydney University Dramatic Society'. He was
great in heavy parts, and used to explain with gusto that
for this reason he had been known as “ Blood and Guts
Charley.” Reverence for stage tradition was his chief
characteristic. Writer remembers how he withered the
secretary of the society, who proposed to “cut” a long-
winged epilogue to one of Column's comedies. “My
God, sir,” groaned Charley, “ cut the epilogue ! Do you
realise the enormity of what you propose, sir?’’

jetted dress, jet ted at a thousand points, clinging closely

The Engineering Works of Mephan Ferguson
arc at present fully occupied, giving employment to a
large number of hands, in the different colonies. In
Western Australia, the patent rivetless locking-bar pipes
for the Coolgardie Wa'cr-supply are now being delivered.
The South Australian works are busy manufacturing and
supplying pipes for the Hundaleer Water Scheme, and at
the General Engineering Works, Melb. orders are being
received for bridge work, pipes, fluming, earth scoops,
forgings, refrigerators, machinery and ironwork. A large
number of patent Nelyambo scoops or tank-cleaners are
being manufactured to meet the increasing demand.*
* * * *
The Western Mine, Zeehan, Tasmania, has cut
six inches of first class ore at No. 7 level in the south
drive on the main lode. A complete Ltthrig concentrat-
ing plant on the gradual reduction principle is erected at
this mine with a capacity of 100 tons per day, and consists
of stone breakers, trommels, rolls, jigs, classifiers, Luhrig
Van tiers, etc. Since the erection of this mill the company
have paid about £106,000 in dividends and the mine still
promises to continue its prosperous career. Luhrig
machinery may be obtained from the General Agents, W.
and J. Lempriere, Melbourne and Broken Hill. A two-to-
five ton experimental plant is erected near Melbourne.*

The Great Boulder Co., Kalgoorlie, have just
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Co.,
Ltd, South Melbourne, to build a pair of compound con-
densing engines of 700 h.p. to drive their extensive mil-
ling plant. These engines will be of the latest type, with
Proeil valve gear. The flywheel is 20ft. in diameter,
grooved for 16 ropes, and weighs upwards of 22 tons, the
total weight of engine will be 85 tons. The engines are to
be completed by the end of the year and will be the finest
and largest plant on the field. Three engines under con-
struction at the Otis Works for the Kalgoorlie goldfields
have an aggregate of 1800 horse power.*

COLUMN
At 30th June last tho Victorian State Land
Bank hail lent £700,945. The total amount of interest in
arrear was only £49 ami the repayments of principal in
arrear "'ere only £35. Though the Stale Land Bank lends
on mortgage at only 4$ per cent, the year’s profits were
£5547, which goes to reserve fund. Working expenses
the first year of the hank’s existence were £1 ‘3s. 7d. per
cent, on all moneys lent. Last year, the business being
larger, and therefore more economical, they were only
12s. W. per cent. Next year it is probable they won't
exceed Os. per cent. If Lite State Land Bank continues
growing at the present rale it is probable that expenses
will very shortly be down to 4s. or ss. per cent., and then
it will be possible to lend at 4 per cent, and still have a
safe margin. Even the bitterest Tory in Victoria has now
given up saying that the State Land Bank is a failure.
What lie says now is that it was solely owing to his
advice tiiat Turner started it.
• • • •
Westralia has decided to enlarge the capital of
its State-owned Agricultural Bank from £lOO,OOO to
£200,000, the original capital having been all lent.
Westralia’s State Land Bank is so utterly unlike the
eminently successful ones of S.A., Victoria and Maoriland
that it is impossible to say yet how it will work out. In
these other three provinces repayment of principal starts
from the date of the loan, so even in those rare cases
where the amount lent left perilously little margin the
amount of the loan started to diminish and the margin to
grow larger and safer almost immediately. In W.A., by
way of making things easy for the agriculturist, repay-
ment of the principal doesn’t begin forfive years—in fact,
in the very earliest loans it hasn’t begun yet—so the
Agricultural Bank doesn't know for certain so far whether
it has been lending on dangerously-inflated valuations or
not. If the repayments come in very badly, and it is left
with stacks of foreclosed properties on its hands, then it
will know all about it.
* * • •
Some of the items included in the temporary
Loan Estimates which Premier Lyne submitted to N.S.W.
Parliament last month, just to go on with, are most in-
teresting. The whole amount was £298,500, and of it
£122,000 was thus accounted for—
Forest thinning .. .. .. .. £4500
Law costs in the McSharry case .. ~ 37,500
Improving railway grades and curves .. 40,000
Converting steam trains into electric trams
in Sydney’s western suburbs and rolling-
stock .. ... .. .. 40,000
£122,000
Forest-thinning is not a permanent and reproductive
work, and any Government which calls law costs a per-
manent and reproductive public work is a disgrace to
book-keeping and arithmetic, and civilisation. Yet all
the rest of the expenses of the McSharry case were
charged to loans. Charging the conversion of steam
trams to loan funds simply means this—The old steam
trams were all paid for with borrowed money, and ail the
money is owing yet, and now the unpaid-for asset will be
taken to pieces, and a new system, constructed with
borrowed money, be built on its ruins, so the country
will owe for two systems, and only possess one. Charging
the new rolling-stock to loans is an equally bad fraud.
The old cars were paid for out of borrowed money to
begin with, but it was always understood that, as they
wore out, they were to be replaced out of revenue by new
cars. But because the ancient stock is to be replaced by
Dew cars of a different pattern, the new cars are to be
paid for, not out of revenue, but out of loans. And the
country will have to pay interest on the cost of two sets
of cars, and yet only own one. The old motors will also
go out of use. But years and years after the}' have fled
tnto the scrap heap the country will still owe the money
for them, and pay interest on it. The new railway grades
and curves come into the same category ; the country
hasn’t paid for the old grades yet. The short and
simple creed of almost all Australian politicians is to pay
nothing out of revenue that can possibly be paid out of
borrowed_ money, anil there aren’t half-a-dozen men in
politics with sufficient courage to condemn the swindle.

The Bank of Australasia’s balance-sheet for the
half-year ended 10th April, 1899, is to hand—the ’Asia
issues its balance-sheet in England and it comes out here
very gradually. The profits are £56,312, which is the
best return this institution has shown for 5£ years past.
The list of half-yearly profits since ’92 is as follows :
October, 1592 ... £80,346 April, 1896 ... £40,568
April, 1893 ... 60,742 October, 1896 ... 40,400
October, 1593 ... 60,607 April, 1897 ... 45,376
April, 1894 ... 48,571 October, 1897 ... 40,278
October, 1894 ... 45,735 April, 1898 ... 48,547
April, 1895 ... 40,854 October, 1898 ... 54,148
October, 1595 ... 41,159 April, 1899 ... 56,312
The profits are equal to £4 13s. lOd. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds, so the ’Asia is doing very well
indeed as average rates go nowadays. In 1895-6-7 the
dividend was at the rate of 5 percent. In 1898 the share-
holders received 6 per cent., ami now the management,
with very doubtful policy, makes a rush to raise the div.
to 7 per cent. This absorbs £56,000, and the other ' 312,
added to £9970 brought forward from last year, leaves
£10,282 of accumulated profit to carry forward. The
Bank of Australasia has a paid-up capital of £1,600,000
and a reserve-fuml of £BOO,OOO, and it lias apparently no
intention of growing any larger—at all events, its
reserve has remained stationary for about a dozen years,
and its profit-balance is only £641 more than it was iri
1891. Deposits have increased by nearly £BOO,OOO during
the half-year, and the reserve of cash and Government
securities is £.3.677,446—equal to about 23 percent, on all
liabilities to the public. Altogether, the'Asia is a very
solid and steady-going institution, which paid a reliable
6 per cent, even in the worst days, and which seems to
have good prospects in front of it. This apparent tauto-
logy is resorted to in onler to distinguish it from certain
institutions, which have splendid prospects behind them.

In its issue of .Tune 10, this year, The Bulletin
drew attention to a strange concern, calling itself the
Industrial Medical Benefit Assurance Society, which had
Started to rake in the premiums of the poor and ignorant
and lowly in a small office at 88 I’itt-street, Sydney. It
W'ent in for industrial life assurance, sick pay, and other
of tiie small and secluded hack alleys of the assurance
business. Its prospectus didn’t give the name of any
manager, secretary, director, medical officer, banker—it
didn t contain the name of anybody, in fact. For all
the evidence therein to the contrary, the institution was
uni by the office cat. A Bi'l.lktix man calling at Lite
very obscure little place of business found a stout man
there who might, or might not, he the whole society, huh
he didn't even learn the stout man's name. So far
M the writer can find out, this society has disap-
peared from its original haunts. The stout man is
said to be running an office in a different line of
business in a different part of the town, and this
paper can’t find out that any other stout man has taken
his place. Therefore it asks the N.SAV. Government if it
will take any rational steps to protect the ignorant
policy-holder (who doesn’t know one industrial office from
another or all of them from Adam) against the disap-
pearing insurance office. A country where any man has
only to take a room, and get a time-payment chair and
desk in it, and ink the decayed spots on his bell-topper,
and wash his celluloid collar at the sink, to be an in-
dustrial fife assurance society and take the hard-earned
uppers of the poor, isn’t run on even the most elemen-
tary business principles. And that is how N.S.W. is run
—in practice, if not in theory.
* * * *
For the first time in its long career the Western
Australian Bank publishes an audited balance-sheet. A
notable feature about it is that the profits (though still
enormously above the average) show a heavy falling away
as compared with those of the last two years. There is
nothing to show that the auditing is in any way the cause
of this, or that in previous balance sheets the manage-
ment, in the absence of any audit, took an over-sanguine
view of what constitutes profits; still, whatever the
cause, the fact remains. The earnings for the past few
years have been:
June, 1894 .. £8,387 June, 1897.. ..£19,685
December, 1894 £9,623 December, 1897 ..£13,251
June, 1895 ..£10,315 June, 1898.. ..£18,542
December, 1895 £10,972 March, L 899 (9 mos.) £29,763
June, 1896 .. £12,284 September, 1599 .. £13,736
December, 1896 £16,703
The profits are equivalent to £9 3s. 2d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds (capital £lOO,OOO,
reserve fund £200,000). There is a large increase in
deposits and in note circulation, but the advances have
diminished (which possibly accounts in some degree for
the diminution of profits). Also coin and bullion on hand
have increased from £544,723 in March last to £680,750.
Cash and Government securities put together are equal to
over 45 per cent, of all liabilities to the public, which is,
proportionately, the strongest cash reserve in Australasia.
With a paid-up capital of £lOO,OOO and a reserve of
£200,000, the Westralian Bank has also the strongest
reserve fund in proportion to capital in Australasia. In
addition to this it pays the largest banking dividend in
Australasia—at the rate of 17£ per cent, per annum,
which absorbs £8750; and the remaining £4986, added to
the previous balance of £9292, leaves £14,27S at credit of
profit and loss. In one way and another the Westralian
Bank's balance-sheet puts up a lot of records.

The fourth annual report of the Australian
Metropolitan Life Assurance Co., of Brisbane—that for
the period of a year and 13 days to 31st August, 1899—is
to hand. The first report, that to 18f.h August, 1896, never
reached this office, so most of the figures for that year
can’t he given, but the following is some account of the
co.’s doings;—
Expenses
including Added to
Total Shareholders’ Assurance
Revenue. Claims. Dividend. Fund.
IS9O .. .. .. £250
1897 £4540 £133 £2754 £1653
1898 £6420 £751 £4450 £1219
1599 £7267 £lOO7 £5102 £1153
According to all ordinary arithmetic, therefore, the Aus-
tralian Metropolitan should have an accumulated assur-
ance fund of £4280, that being the amount it has actually
saved in its four years’ career. On paper, however, it
claims to have a fund of £14,375. It arrived at this pro-
cess by purchasing an office building—the Queensland
Deposit Bank Buildings, Brisbane—for about £14,000 and
putting it down in the balance-sheet as an asset worth
£24,000. It said that this was “according to the valua-
tion of the asset,” hut the report didn’t say who made the
beautiful and useful valuation. In this case, as in that
of two other life assurance institutions with which the
same thing happened, the writer can only say that the
co. whose office property increases handsomely in value
at a time when such increase comes in very handy
possesses a much more courteous and obliging building
than he ever possessed. Whatever buildings he lias
owned have always deteriorated with a thud just when
they were most wanted.
In 1897 the Australian Metropolitan spent <£fio
13s. out of every £lOO of income in working expenses and
dividends to shareholders. In 1898 it spent £69 6s. out
of every £lOO ; in 1899 expenses and dividend absorbed
£7O 4s. out of. every £lOO of gross income. There is
a general idea that as an assurance institution grows
larger t.he proportion of working expenses should
diminish, but things are not going that way with the
Australian Metropolitan. If the business was wholly
industrial the expenses might not seem exorbitant, but,
considering that a considerable proportion is ordinary
life insurance, they are very high. The sum saved and
added to the assurance-fund out of revenue (the obliging
conduct of the building being for the moment left out of
the reckoning) is growing smaller each year. Under
these circumstances, the conduct of the shareholders in
helping themselves to the very handsome dividend of 10
percent.—which they have now done for three years in
succession—looks like a most reckless and unjustifiable
grab. To all intents and purposes, every copper the co.
has is represented by its big building. The paid-up
capital and assurance-fund amount to £20,375 on paper,
and the building is put down at £24,500, so all the com-
pany’s loose assets wouldn’t pay off the mortgage and
leave it with its structure clear of debt. Though the co.
is now four years old, there is no mention of any actuarial
valuation, which may reveal whether the assurance-fund
would have been solvent if the building hadn’t increaseil
in value so much above the purchase price, or what would
have happened. Probably the A.M. proposes to have its
valuation every five years, as several other institutions
do.

Zachariah Gray is proceeding against L.
Stevenson and Sons,.'Ltd. (Melb.), on a question of the
forfeiture of 500 shares in that luckless softgoods concern.
The shares were declared forfeited last year for non-pay-
ment of a call of Is. Plaintiff disputes the act of annexa-
tion on the ground that “ the Is. call was not made bona-
fide in the interests of the co., but in order to benefit the
directors and their friends.” Furthermore, he alleges
“ breaches of trust by the directors, whereby the plaintiff
was left in a state of ignorance as to the real position of
the co., and the real value of his shares, and so refrained
from paying the calls made.” Zac.hariah’s story of how
he was left in the dark should be worth hearing.

lb is an oft-repeated gag of the Ooflander brigade
that the foreign population were wholly responsible for
the gold industry of the Rand. The fact is that the dis-
covery of the Rand was made by two burghers of Johan-
nesburg—a Boer and a Hollander—who had a 10-head
battery erected on the field long before ever the Ooflander
came along.

Girilambone Copper Co., N.S.W., has passed
another interesting stage. It has audaciously refused
£45,000 casli and £55,000 in paid-up shares in a £250,000
co. with 150,000 working capital. “ The tone of the dis-
cussion at the meeting quickly made it evident that the
shareholders were not inclined to accept the offer made.”
The chairman thought a fair alternative would be £20,000
more cash and one-fourih interest in the new co., and a
shareholder thought that 10 per cent, per 6hare in cash
and £1 fully-paid share for every two held now would he a
square thing. The market price of the shares on this
day was, sellers Bs. for paids and os. Od. for the oilier
kind. Shareholders have waited three years in vain for
the mine to come along, and now they peremptorily
refuse a hig offer. Of course the mine is going to do
great things immediately, but that story has been heard ’
before. One nhase of the question has been entirely
overlooked—that the £60,000 working capital, which
there is no chance whatever of obtaining locally, would
give the shares received in part-payment a higher market-
value than the mine has to-day, and shareholders would
then have the £45,000 cash to the good. The usual story
is, “ they know their own business best.” In this in-
stance, it is doubtful.

The law of the politician is that whenever a
new Treasurer takes office, his first duty is to announce
that liis predecessors have left thing's in an awful mess ;
which is generally true. Now, the 8.11. Junction
board consists mainly of politicians, the result of the
kick-out of the old crowd at the last half-yearly meeting ;
and in the first report since the change was made, it is
quite natural that the new political crowd should say—
“ Look what a horrible mess they left us to get out of! ”
And, though things might certainly have been made to
look a good deal better, the mess is there all right—all
along of the greedy policy of new-chum directors three or
four years ago in hack ingout every available pound of good
ore, and hang the consequences 1 This particularhalf-year's
figures arc made to look worse than they might be because
a trifle of l!)0i)0 is charged to wear-and-tear. For a
couple of years the smelting-plant at I’ort. Adelaide (the
co. used those worksonce fora few months, but it is many
years ago) stood on the books at £12,000; this line is now
written down to £BOOO. By one or two drastic actions
like that the profit and loss account comes out. nearly
£2OOO to the bad ; but making a fair charge for deprecia-
tion, and relieving this unfortunate six months of the
sins of its predecessors so cruelly thrown upon it, we get
at a profit of about £4OOO. But even that with respect to
a co. which three years ago made a profit of about t 76,000
in a twelve-month, is surely awful enough. There is an
atom of consolation in the fact that the directors show a
small balance of liquid assets over liabilities for the first-
time since the cave-in of the mine, and the discovery that
the estimates of ore were all wrong, and that more plant
was needed, drove the old board to borrow £lO,OOO. The
loan was “ for 12 months." The co. owes it still.

The B.JT. Junction milled for the half-year
40.720 tons ore, which averaged only 9'3oz. silver and
15‘2 per cent, lead, as against 10'720z. silver and 18 per
cent, lead for the previous six mouths ; 0570 tons of con-
centrates were produced, averaging 23‘40z. silver and
61’0 percent, load. (Zinc contents not stated, though they
should be known in order to judge of the quality of the
work done and of the value of the concentrates.) The re-
covery is therefore 35‘4 per cent, of the silver and 57 per
cent, of the lead. The.directors do not think this can be
considered satisfactory; and this paper does not feel called
upon to contradict them. In fact if it had a pocket
which let two-thirds of its silver and nearly one-lmlf of its
copper slide down its trouser-ieg, it would think that
the pocket deserved stronger language than that. At
present metal prices, it seems, about 355. per ton of ore,
goes flown the Junction’s trouser-leg, and 365. stays in
its pocket—until it has to come out to pay expenses. And
in those figures no account is taken of the zinc, of which
there is, say, 20 per cent, in the ore, and which, in metallic
form, is worth al>out>£22 per ton. “ The question of treat-
ment of the tailings (the ‘refuse’ where the 355. and
the zinc go), is being very carefully considered,” the di-
rectors say ; and the matter is worth all the considera-
tion that the board can give it. Some improvement in
the ore-treating is all the more necessary, because the
stopes at the 775 ft. level—worked for the first time this
last half-year—show ore of a disappointingly low grade ;
though 100 much importance can easily be attached to the
lowness of the grade of the ore at any one level in this
patchy mine.
• • • •
The 8.11. Junction’s more recent catastrophes
thunder a couple of texts into the ears of people who
nurse mining shares : (1) When you have a board consist-
ing of new-cliums in mining and a manager with an only
moderately stilt back, watch them in the days of pros-
perity. Don’t let ’em think (as they're likelv to) that all
they’ve got to do in order to earn their fees and your
blessing is to pile up divs. promptly. Ask them what
they’re doing with the hole where the ore came from.
(N.B.—This foregoing is specially recommended to share-
holders in some Westralian mines which are busy putting
up div. records. Already there have been a few collapses
in workings there, and the butcher’s bill is heavy already.)
(2) The businesses of ore-raising and ore-smelting are not
the same. The Junction’s Port Adelaide works cost it
about £20,000. They would have been dear at fewer
pence. (This is specially recommended to the considera-
tion of “ investors" in copper-shows just now.)

B.H. North, thanks to the rise in the price of
lead, has paid a div.—a little one. And at last, after
waiting 13 years. Broken Hill can say that every one of
the mines on the main line of lode has paid a div.
B.H. British Co. owns two blocks, but has so
far almost confined work to Block 15—the one nearest
Block 14. Block 16 is partly in a flat; the lode twists
there into the Junction. Thus there are points of re-
seinhlanee between Block 10 and Block 16. Still, the
latter has been neglected, chiefly because, after the great
“ creep ” eight or nine years ago, the co. has never had
money to spend in prospecting. The tide has turned now
though ; and with it the directors are going to probe
this half of their property a little more.

Of all the booms that have failed that wliich
had Worturpa for a peg to hang upon was surely the
most abject failure. A minute.quantity of t.ellurid'e was
found there; and a wild rush to the Far North of S.A.
began. Prospectors toiled over the most God-forsaken
country on the continent to reach a district which the
most experienced of them condemned at once—all broken
country and quite unlikely to carry permanent lodes or
reefs. The “ field ” was deserted almost as rapidly as it
was rushed.

large scale arc being carried on by London companies,

lOdwt. of gold per ton, it is very doubtful whether the

work of developing the mines.

The strongest argument against the pro-
posal to hold a Sydney Exhibition is the fact that
Melbourne, with a fine Exhibition building in its
midst, never talks of spending another quarter
of a million on another dreary international show.
Australia is much too large, and its people are
much too scattered for any capital city to run an
important exhibition, except at a big loss,
yisitors frem Europe and the adjoining Austra-
lian provinces were found to be a very small matter
of support for the Melbourne enterprise of ’BB.
The spec, had to rely mainly on local patronage,
and Melbourne, which even then contained con-
siderably more than the present population of
Sydney, proved quite unequal to the manage-
ment’s demand for shilling admission fees. The
big show was a nine-days’ wonder—then it became
a gloomy farce. _ Phil. May, shaking hands with
one of the exhibitors a few weeks after the open-
ing, said—“ I don’t think you’ll see me here again,
old man—it’s too much like a cemetery.” All was
cold and dead.

The Select Committee appointed by N.S.
Wales Assembly to inquire into the case of lunatic
Cresswell and his alleged identity with Roger
Tichborne should be able to settle the question at
one sitting. The exact height, physical peculiari-
ties, and undoubted tattoo-marks of the real Sir
Roger are matters of history, and several doctors,
AS UNDERSTOOD IN AUSTRALIA.
O'PHIDDIAN: “ Phwat’s the maniny of Pat-riot-
ism?”
MACLAURLIAN : “Love o’ country."
O’P.: “ Phwat country?”
MACL.: “ Tother feller’s! ”
including two of Sydney’s most expert and
reputable surgeons, have sworn that Cresswell
doesn’t answer to the description in any important
particular. The inquiry need not go further than
a careful measurement of Cresswell and a close
inspection of his limbs and body. If he bears the
essentia' marks and signs, and is not, in his old
age, more than an inch shorter than the long-lost
baronet’s recorded height, he is apparently the
real Sir Roger, and the doctors stand convicted
of conspiracy to conceal the truth. On the other
hand, if Cresswell lacks the Rogerian marks,
inches, and characteristics, any other inquiry were
a scandalous farce.

Regulation Bill becomes law in N.S.W., and it is
illegal to employ any female domestic servant
under 14, or to work any female domestic more
than eight hours a day (say from 8 a.m. to 4 p.m.
—“ the hours when the work is to be done are to
be as arranged between mistress and servant ”),
and she musn’t be disturbed to attend to children
m the night—what will happen? The writer
dimly foresees seven possibilities. (1) Many
families will employ the male white Sairey Hann
instead of the female white ditto, and there will
be employment in abundance for every man in the
cximmunity. (2) There will be a run on Chinese,
Kanakas, and Africans—but happily the supply of
these is very limited. (3) Ingenious families won’t
keep a servant at all; they will keep an adopted
daughter instead. There are enormous possi-
bilities about an adopted daughter. The Bill says
nothing about an adopted daughter only working
eight hours. (4) In a great many families where
there are daughters who now batter “Daisy
Bell” and “ Harfter-r-r, the B orl” on the
piano, these young ladies will attack the house-
work themselves, which will he beneficial both to
them and to the community at large. (5) There
will be a run on governesses, and the governess
will tackle the housework in a purely voluntary
spirit, and solely because she likes housework, and
yearns after it, and can’t live without it, and won’t
be happy till she gets it. There will be no com-
pulsion, and she can leave off the housework at
any time. But if she leaves it off she will be
sacked, because of the imperfections of her French
accent and alleged deficiencies in her Sanscrit and
h er -"- ra bian trigonometry. (6) There will be a
rush to invent domestic labor-saving machines,
and the automatic iron Biddy will partly fill the
void. Also (7) people who can afford it will keep
two servants in place of one. There are so many
interesting possibilities in view that this scribe
hopes to see the measure pass. If it doesn’t, a
great contribution to the gaiety of nations will
be lost.
» « * *
The only suicide directly due to this year’s
raping carnival was that of Jas. Anderson, a
Vic. saveloy-vendor. James jumped into the
Yarra, and the rash act was due to depression,
consequent on a ruinous speculation in saveloys.
Anderson expected a tremendous run on hot
saveloys on the Flat, but the rain spoilt his
market, and he was left with a woful waste of
sausages on hand, from which the poor man could
discover no refuge but the grave.

“ The Colonial ” has a personal grievance
Over the departure of the contingent:
I have kept up for years, in spite of syndicated cables,
a flickering interest in English cricket. From the cables
I have slowly deduced the conclusion that the principal
performers on English wickets are Albert Trott and
Roche. I know, in a general way, that there are fiftv or a
hundred other men of equal interest, but the cable-man
never seems to see them. From the Cape cables I have
already begun to realise that two Australian doctors have
hitherto been running the entire South African campaign
between them, and that the arrival of 105 N.S. W. lancers
at Capetown is the central feature of recent develop-
nients. About the most thrilling incident of the whole
affair to date has been the appointment of Trooper Tom-
kins (as duly chronicled per special war correspondent)
to clean up manure ’tween decks on the Aberdeen, while
the one touch of pathos necessary to complete the swel-
scene is imparted by the melancholy sacking of a
Westralian barmaid for the sake of a parti-colored blouse.
I am Australian all over, I hope, but the participation of
Australians in this war doesn’t add one scintilla of in-
terest to it for me. This is a stand-up fight with good
hard men on both sides. As a spectator with a sporting
turn of mind, I ask to be allowed to back m 3 fancy and
get a clear view. The Australian contingent is snoiling
that clear view. I am in the stalls at this performance
and the matinee hats of those 105 N.S.W. Lancers are in
the way. They have no business in that gallery. They
are but mere amateurs, anyhow. If a big battle takes
place I want to read, per cable, about the dreadful
slaughter, about British pluck and Boer perfidy, to
learn what were the incidents, and which regiments dis-
tmguished themselves. What we shall learn is that
Troop-sergeant Jones of the onetieth N.S.W. regiment
performed prodigies'of dexterity in the stopping of some
runaway horse, and that Corporal Higgins had the lobe of
his left ear shot away by a spent bullet. I have be-
grudged the daily copper for the paper for years. I was
just beginning to get the value of my money over this
fight, and the contingent business has spoilt it again.

THE BOER INVASION.
OLD SYDNEY LADY (reading Transvaal cables):
The Boers are marching on Newcastle.
Oh, dear me! and my poor son went up there
last night by the Namoi!"
There is some superstition left yet. When
i 6 instalment of N.S.W. troops left for
the Transvaal, one “soldier of the Queen”
wrapped himself up in the Union Jack and
climbed up the main rigging, whereupon a man in
the crowd was heard to say, “ Well, / wouldn’t
do that fora fiver ; he’ll be tipped overboard with
a flag round him before they get to South Africa.”

A young Melbourne lady, from Abbots-
ford, was recently in London, and asked Father
Kussell S.J. (brother of England’s Lord Chief
Justice), who is a bit of a bard, to write some
verses m her album, about the Yarra. “But”
said the priest, “I have not seen it.” “Never
mind, said she, “ write as Wordsworth wrote his
borrow Unvisited. ’ ” Father Russell then wrote
Ne’er have I ramified on its marge
Nor angled ’mid its willows— ’
I ne’er have sailed in skiff or bar«-e
Upon its languid billows.
Yet will I sing as Cullinan
Once sung of Gougaune Barra—
Yet will I sing, as best I can—
“ The Lazy Flowing Yarra.’’
It creeps between high winding sides,
And e’er it sees the city ;
Past Holy Abbotsford it glides,
To which it owes this ditty.
For ah Australian Album, why
Waste praise on Connemara?
Thy heart’s in Abbotsford, and I
Will praise its Yarra Yarra.
They tell me that the sky above
Is bluer far and brighter,
Than that which spans the soil we love :
The air is warmer, lighter,
Gay flowers along the margin float,
And many an Avis Rara,
Of brilliant bloom, but tuneless throat,
Skims o'er the Sparkling Yarra.
And now at last we must give o’er
With our Wordsworthy Sapphic,
Though sundry rhymes remain in store,
Historic, Topographic.
Besides the few we have imprest,
As Lara and Bokhara,
Carrara, Marat, and the rest,
To chime in with you r Yarra.
“K.8.J.” sends along some disrespectful
language
Saw N.S.W. contingent* march through Melbourne on
Cup-day. Have always been under the impression that
one of the first essentials of the military aspirant was to
look like a soldier. These men looked, to me—trained
under an Imperial instructor like a troop of uniformed
anthropoids, making at a jog trot for the nearest forest,
they bored along with stooping shoulders and arms
swinging like animated pendulums, seeming, beyond a
vague idea of keeping line, to be unacquainted with even
the goose-step. How the well-drilled Tommy Atkins will
grin and set them down, from the jump, as amateius
and what ineffable contempt he has for amateurs in his
own line! Surely if it was at all necessary to send
troops, it would have been better to send some of the
trained Permanent men, and not a lot of raw recruits.
Now that the war-drum throbs in Kruger-
land it may be as well to remember that there is a
correct and an incorrect way of pronouncing the
word “ Uitlander.” Chamberlain has been in the
habit of calling it “Ootlander,” and Campbell-
Bannerman says “ Outlander. ” Journalist Lifoy
comes nearest to the right thing with “ Ait-
lander.” An undoubted authority has determined
that the correct pronunciation is “ Oitlander. ’’
You must now say: “ Regarding the matter of
President Kreere and the Oitlanders,” or your
education will be considered to have been neg-
lected in your youth. Another undoubted au-
thority says it is “ Oitlandter,” and that President
“Kreere” is really “Krujer.” He is also
“ Kruzher.”
* * * *
From Sydney D.T.’s special correspondent
with the N.S. W. South African contingent
In the last Australian (Soudan) contingent, there were
a few officers who worried the hearts out of the men by
vexatious severity and stupidly' inconsistent punishment.
Such was the bitterness bred by this convict gang con-
ception of discipline that there were three officers and a
sergeant blacklisted for their bullet if their men got a
chance; certain officers were publicly groaned at and
hissed by the men on the transport returning to Sydney.
On this occasion I don’t find the officers blundering in
the direction of excessive strictness; while the men
themselves are quietly sliding into better form.
* * * *
There is confusion in Heaven. Moderator
Murdoch, the hatchet-faced and amiable head of
the Victorian Kurruk at large, issued an order
that the Lorrud must be besocht for the success
of the Australians in Boer-sticking. The chorus
was accordingly wafted upward, when, 10, a dis-
cordant note ! Dr. Rentoul rushed with bulgy
forehead to the pulpit of the little church in Park-
ville, from whence there forthwith shot up a peti-
tion for all tyrants to be squelched who tried to
crush the Independence of two Little Republics.
Dr. Rentoul, admired by the laity, is regarded
by his brother meenisters as the enfant terrible
of the Kirk. The doctor, whose chief business is
teaching about 17 raw, half-fledged parsons at
Ormond College, is a small, big-headed, pert man,
never happy unless engaged in hot newspaper
war. He is most catholic in his disputes, which
range from a controversy with R.C. Archbishop
Carr, in Age and Argus, down to a shindy over
the Boers with a dour person named Hendry, in
the Southern Cross —Parson Fitchett’s paper.
He never enters a controversy without “keenly
deprecating strife,” and then he hits as hard as he
can—and that is very hard indeed. He scored a
neat point in the Boer dispute—in which he is on
the, at present, unpopular side —by contrasting his
authorities—Olive Schreiner and Bryce, with
poor Hendry’s Rider Haggard and Anthony
Trollope.

Will some member rise in each of the seven
Legislatures of Australasia and demand a return
of the number of warriors originally selected to go
to Africa who didn’t go, with the reasons, so far
as known, for their not going? There are per-
sistent rumors about many unaccountable disap-
pearances among the chosen, and it is even
alleged that one man who rashly offered himself
was so knocked of a heap, from excessive joy or
otherwise, when accepted that he promptly cut his
throat. The information is wanted in order to
throw some light on the Australian’s military
sentiments. It may be put as a sum in proportion,
thus : If out of so many men who volunteer for a
well-paid military service that looks more like a
picnic than anything else, the proportion of
absentees is per cent., how many per cent.

Natal natives will be surprised when they see a
a powerful lamp to light up the various bridges. It burns
kerosene, and converts it into gas, giving a white light,
equal to 3500 candle power, and brighter than any elec-
tric light yet invented. Messrs. Brandt Bros., lamp
manufacturers, of Sydney and Melbourne, are ahout to
submit them to the Defence Department. Their Primus
Kerosene Das Cooking stoves, specially constructed for
travellers, ought to be of great service, as they can be
packed in such a small space.*
The Bulletin acknowledges the receipt
from the publisher, Mr. 0. F. Maxwell, of Syd-
ney, of a small, concise volume entitled The
Liquor Act, 1898, No. 18. The work is by H. M.
Cockshott and S. E. Lamb, L.L.B.’s and is a
complete encyclopaedia of all that one may do,
and also everything one may not do, when out on
the drunk. The Liquor Act is a most compre-
hensive instrument and with the aid of this valu-
able work one can see at a glance exactly the sort
of language one may address to the barmaid and
what sort of language must never be addressed to
the landlady. Also it can be immediately dis-
covered what sort of blouse the girl in the side
parlor should wear, and in every way the book is
a valuable mine of information on the great thirst
question.

When each instalment of N.S.W. South
African Contingent sailed there appeared an
official list of the men. And each time it was
promptly followed by a list of “corrections”
something like this :
FIRST AUSTRALIAN HORSE.
For Trooper “ M. A. Cummins ” read M. A. Commins.
For Trooper “ J. J. Lewis ” read J. Louis.
MOUNTED RIFLES.
For Sergeant-Farrier “ J. Hawkey” read J. M. Hawkey
For Corporal “A. Bateup ” read A. H. Bateup.
For Private “F. Oxam ” read F. W. Axam.
For Private “ M. Burke” read M. J. Burke.
For Private “ A. Butler” read A. A. Butler.
For Private “ E. Cleary ” read E. T. Cleary.
For Private “ R. Nethery” read R. J. Nethery.
For Private “ P. Reilly” read P. J. Reilly.
For Private “ P. Sharp” read R. Sharpe.
For Private “ B. Sparing ” read B. C. Sparing.
For Private “ W. Wintle” read W. E. Wintle.
Westralia went one better than this ; it published
a list to the effect that Edgar Waterman was to
be read as George Augustus Morris, and Thomas
Edward o‘Elaherty was to be regarded as Carl
Alfred Eorsberg, and William Schultze was really
Jacques Farquharson Messer, and so on. Appar-
ently about half the Contingenteers hadn’t de-
cided who they were till the very last moment,
and in the W. A. case, it really seems as if eight
men had evaporated at the last moment, and the
urgent necessity for regarding Jones as Thompson
arose through Thompson being non est.

The departure of the N.S.W. Laneeahs
from London was an interesting function. They
marched through the streets of the misty city on
foot, each man carrying his pitchfork with its
ribbons hanging loose. In front went a band
playing rousing music, also women, dogs, and
small boys. All along the line of march people
hung Union Jacks from the windows and bal-
conies, and kept the front windows and doors
closed in case the hats and cloaks should escape off
the rack in the hall. Passing a warehouse in
Fenehurch-street a panic was nearly produced by
an individual who suddenly appeared at a window
and heaved a heavy-looking parcel at the Corn-
stalks. For a terrible moment it was suspected
that Dr. Leyds was aiming lyddite at the men,
but it turned out to be a parcel of small Union
Jacks, silk ones at that, and each man seized one
as a memento. The makers had thoughtfully
made them the size of pockethandkerchiefs. At
the station the men were harangued by Carring-
ton and Hampden. Altogether it was a great
show, and said by some of the papers to be un-
equalled by anything since the great Jubilee pro-
cession. . . .„ And now 33 per cent of those
warriors aren’t going to fight after all.

vVhen the Lyne Ministry has
time to consider the N.S.W. free-news-
paper-postage swindle it might fix its
baleful eye on a fine sample in *the
Southern Cross. The Cross is a
kind of alleged monthly magazine,
though the suffering taxpayer of
N.S.W. pays for the free transit
of the awful thing through the
post as a newspaper. Its chief
business is to spread information
about two advertising doctors.
The writer has before him a copy
of the statutory declaration of
somebody or other to the .effect
that he runs one of the adver-
tising doctors, and that he has
bought 156,000 copies of the
magazine for free distribution—
which apparently means that the
N.S.W. taxpayer pays for the
con veyance all over the province
of 156,000 advts. about the man
who is prepared to treat weak
back, giddiness, prickly sensa-
tion and specks before the
eyes. There is not a vestige
of ordinary news in this
medical organ to justify it
in calling itself a news-
paper and loafing on the
taxpayer in that capa-
city; but it contains a
few scraps of verse; a
photo, and puff of a
person w ho is descri bed
as “the eminent soli-
citor, “and whose six-
roomed office, it
seems, is “ hand-
somely furnished in
exquisite taste ” (it is
even mentioned that
the eminent one has
travelled as far as Fiji);
also a crude yarn which says,
“One wild shriek and Hilda
Harding lay lifeless at the Gold
King’s feet.” This is the organ which is
now blessing the N.S.W. taxpayer with such
a lot of thrilling information about his inside.
The average taxpayer, by the way, has a vague
idea that he is hollow, that he is filled about
half way up with blood and other fluids, and that,
when he eats, his food falls with a splash into the
general receptacle and floats about there. Adver-
tisements about his inside shouldn’t be thrown at
this kind of person too recklessly.

A young man named Davis
was recently crushed to death at a
Maclean (N.S.W.) mill, and it is very
curious that the night before the acci-
dent deceased’s mother dreamt that
another son, employed in another
mill, had been crushed in the same
way by machinery. Was the por-
tent switched on to the wrong number ?
* • * »
The recent fatal shooting-accident at St.
Kilda is a prize object-lesson in the theory of
chances. An eccentric octogenarian was cleaning
his revolver on his back verandah when one charge
exploded. The bullet sped across his yard, and
passed through a paling-fence oft. 6in. above the
ground. On the ether side of the fence was a
narrow recess, enclosed on three sides, and used
simply as a light-area for the windows of the
house, 4ft. distant—a spot which is probably
visited by the.residents of the place about once a
year. But, at the time the charge exploded, a
young woman, who had moved into the house
only a fortnight before, was collecting some
creepers. Under ordinary circumstances, the
shot would have passed harmlessly over her head
she stood but sft. 3in.—but, to reach the
creepers, she had clambered on to the lower rail
of the fence. The bullet passed through the
paling, severed the carotid artery, and she was
dead when her relations found her, 15min. later—
a succession of circumstances which would not
occur once in a million years.
• • * •
A Y.M.C.A. secretary in an Australian
capital has more good nature and less Stigginsism
in him than most of his employers. At the time
of the late Melbourne Cup meeting he had been
out seeing some friends, and as his rooms in
town are close to a newspaper office he promised
to ring them up on his return and let them know
the name of the winner. Unfortunately, how-
ever, a committee meeting was being held in the
building, and the assembled Holy Joes were
horrified to hear the secretary ’phoning “Hello!
Melbourne Cup—Merriwee, Voyou, Dewey ! ” It
all but cost him his billet, and he’ll be more care-
ful in future.
* * • »
A simple little tale of Kerristian indiscre-
tion was told in Melbourne Divorce Court last
week by a deserted husband. Petitioner stated
that on the night of his marriage, 12 years ago,
the bride gave him warning of a domestic event
in store for him, and further admitted that the
author of her trouble was the Bible Christian
minister who had performed the ceremony. Next
morning he (petitioner) went round to the house
of his bride’s mother, where the officiating minis-
ter was a lodger, and asked the good. man why he
had joined him in holy matrimony to the erring
spinster, to which the good shepherd replied, in a
meek Bible Christian tone of voice, that he
“ thought it best, to let things take their course.”
Eour months later the penitent bride gave birth
to her spiritual adviser’s baby, and when the un-
welcome stranger had been registered as the off-
spring of her lawful husband she went away and
left him forevermore, saying it was impossible for
her to carry on any more married life after what
had happened—meaning the baby. This story is
intended to put unsuspicious citizens on their
guard when meditating matrimony with any
young woman whose mother lets lodgings to very
pious personages.

It goes without saying that J. H. Grey,
the missing Geelong solicitor, was a shining
churcblight, and an ardent supporter of charitable
movements. No man can figure as a inuch-
respected inhabitant of Geelong unless he travels
around on the religious ticket. Grey’s deficit
is one of the occasional pills that all canting com-
munities must swallow, and if it purges Geelong
of a little dull respectability, the ex-M.L.C. and
entertainer of Governor Brassey will not have
lived and prayed in vain.

S.A. has a smellful police enquiry on just
now. Certain officials are accused of being in
league with, and sharing the profits made by,
prominent confidence-tricksters, and the fishy
stories became so circumstantial that S.A. Govt.

The man who made so gross and savage an
attempt upon plucky little Miss Huie, teacher of
the lonely Wellesley (N.S.W.) State-school, has
been captured and has fully confessed his crime.
Writing tb The Bulletin a man on the spot says:
The N.S.W. police score again heavily. They always
seem to have the luck in catching their man. In the
Wellesley case it was sheer luck, and the man almost got
clear. He was within six miles of Moruya, and if he had
only been cautious enough to camp back off the road
Constable Branch would have passed him, and he would
probably have escaped ; a small coasting steamer was
leaving in a few hours, he intended travelling by it, and
could by 10 following morning have been swallowed up in
the Sydney crowd. His capture was entirely due to an
unusually clear-headed constable at Bermagui, who, on
reading the man’s description in the PoliC® Gazette, re-
membered that such a man had passed through the
town, making north, a few days previously ; and he at
once wired the fact to the head office of his district.
But a force which allows its constables to obtain
descriptions of individuals who are “wanted” from a
Gazette published 10 days after the event, does not
deserve any such luck, and is, if anything, very little
superior to the Queensland how-not-to-do-it. The N.S.W.
force were entirely at fault as to the line Bertsch had
taken, and followed unhesitatingly the blind lead he gave
them from Craigie to the Vic. border. It never seems to
have struck any of them that it uas a blind, and that the
man had doubled on his tracks. Still, the fact that he
ostentatiously asked in Craigie the distance from there
to the border should have given them a clue—a criminal
does not usually deliberately leave finger-posts along the
road he is taking. Only 32 years of age, Bertsch has,
during the last 16 years of his life, spent 11 in gaol. He
seemed a cool, determined, powerful man, possessing
clear-cut, open features, with nothing of the hang-dog
criminal in them, and he wrote a good fist. Still, the
fact that, when arrested, he was wearing a portion of a
woman’s underclothing round his neck, points to the fact
that he was probably a victim to satyriasis. He pays
Miss Huie a high tribute as being a “ real game one."

Complained that the women awaiting con-
finement in Sydney Benevolent Society’s Mater-
nity Hospital have to sleep two in a bed. The
sad truth of the whole business is that the
Benevolent Asylum is not a State institution with
a bottomless purse, but a struggling organisation
which depends on public subscriptions and pro-
portionate subsidies from Govt, and which does
the best it can with the limited means at its dis-
posal.

Queensland Govt, analyst:—
I examined 43 samples of tea; 24 of them failed to
reach the standard, and shipments were stopped. Nine
samples of carbolic disinfectant were also examined,
eight of which were absolutely useless for disinfecting
purposes. All the samples marked salad oil, and some
marked olive oil were not olive oil, but cotton-seed oil.
Poor Queensland ! Its whisky is mostly sulphuric
acid and aniline ; its youngsters are largely hybrid
and mottled ; it is fast becoming a great land of
Hidden Crime and Open Adulteration.

A high-toned lady, said to be in the jour-
nalistic line, was interviewed at a Melb. hotel the
other week re her experiences of the nasty Boers.
Her attempt to show reason for wiping out the
barbaric creachaws was painfully thin. Said the
fair authority to her interviewer—
At Volksrust we were searched. I was taken into a
room where a woman, employed by the Transvaal cus-
toms, proceeded to pass her hands all over me. I suppose
she thought I might have firearms concealed about my
person. Even the contents of my pocket-book were in-
spected. I came to the conclusion that I must look
rather a suspicious character.
How shocking! Transvaal customs officers are
evidently no better than the savages who repre-
sent any ordinary European Govt, on the frontier.
A foreigner landing at Dover isn’t less sus-
piciously explored.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
. _ Sydney, November 14, 1899.
My Dear Myee.—
From gossip dropped by various frier.ds jnsfr
back from the Cup I gather that Lord Beauchamp,
scored quite a sensational popularity in Mel-
bourne. His little speeches were considered!
arrowy and humorous over there, which rather-
puts Sydney to the blush for not having made that
discovery over the prophet in his own country.
There the fashion was rapidly growing of taking-
him thusly : “ The Governor's speech ! What has<
he been saying now?” Also, Melbourne worn era
could never get enough of him, and crowded!
round in adorration wherever he appeared.
I have just been shown a letter from a Mel-
bourne girl to a Sydney friend. “Do ask your-
Governor not to stand on one leg. If lie didn't do.
that, the angel would be quite too perfect for any-
thing.” Didn’t she mean wing instead of leg?
The Grand Commonwealth Fair opened on.
Saturday afternoon at the Town Hall in the.
presence of a large, somewhat Hibernian” crowd.
An ad. in the daily papers that morning said there,
were to be 850 women assisting, so that I was not,
surprised to see only a comparatively few men in.
the hall, and those chiefly reverend fathers. It,
was a mistako all the same, as the stall holders,
didn’t seem any more numerous and übiquitous,
than at any other bazaar of the season. A great
many little stalls ran up each side of the hall” and!
many different-colored draperies are arrayed along,
the tops of the stalls. The Burwood and Strath-
field counter broke out rather prettily in mauve-
and white draperies, the interior draped cleverly
to simulate a cool check pattern, and the stall-
holders all ringing the changes on mauve and!
heliotrope gowns. The ecclesiastical stall of Mrs.
Freehill anil Miss O’Brien showed some marvellous;
needlework vestments, all done by the nuns’
clever fingers. Heaps of needlework of a superior-
kind is on sale at many of- the stalls. The stall-
holders’ frocks are quite a sort of go-as-you-please.
One lot sported white silk skirts, and white serge
sailor jackets with blue sailor collars and silver-
fringed epaulettes. 'I ho Glebe stall was in white,
muslin with pink roses round the knees and pink
and white hats. Among the Saturday afternoon’s,
crowd I noticed Miss Oxenham in a red cloth,
frock braided in black, with a grey velvet hat and;
pink rosettes, Mrs. Mort in black and purple and
white bonnet, and Mrs. Slattery in a black niuslim
scattered with violets.
As the Fair has the appearanceof being entirely
an R.C. affair, opened by the Cardinal, and in aid
of the Sisters of the Good Samaritans Charitable
Institutions, I was somewhat taken aback to come-
across, in the midst of innumerable Misses O F.’s
and McC.’s, and soon, one little lonely “Jewish
Stall,” supported by Mrs. and the Misses
Montague Marks, and dubbed “Los Anglos”'
out of compliment to the stallholders’ pure white-
raiment.
You will be glad to hear that everyone left in;
the city at this time of the year has been listening
to 1 rebelli at the Centenary Hall. Her opening
night saw a big fashionable house, but Saturday
night drew a crowd that was far too large for the
hall. Ever so many people were turned away. In
the dressy front seats I noticed at least two-
oui-dircft, a modern widow with pink roses in her
hair whose engagement to a medical man is.
whispered abroad very softly, and an artist,
chaperoning his latest study.
As usual, Trebelli’s frocks were a large part of
the attraction. In addition to singing like a.
highly-veneered angel she always dresses like a
Frenchwoman of the world. And when. I say-
dresses, I don’t mean only her frocks, for the arc
applies to every detail of her toilette, from the
hair parted in the middle and dressed low on the-
neck in defiance of fashion’s top-knot, clown to her
carefully-slippered feet. She wore on the first
night a lovely frock, cut en princesse, of white-
brocade, patterned in apple-blossoms. The yoke
and sleeves were of transparent lace, and down the
right side, from the yoke to the bottom skirt, were
round lace appliques, embroidered with iridescent
paillettes. Diamonds in the hair and on the
corsage, and a trailing skirt finished a costume
into which the plump TrebeHi fitted without a
crease. At Saturday’s warble she was in glittering
blue, with transparent sleeves and corsage and
pink roses at the neck and waist.
The art of going without gloves, that started in
the back scats in prehistoric times, has reached up
through the intermediate rows, through the highly-
manicured front seats, even unto the platform,
where it is quite the thing now for the artist to
warble with baro white fingers. Men still sing
in new white kidsof the tight old order, diamonds
being forbidden them, but a woman is allowed to
be gloveless pven if her sleeve is a more shoulder-
strap and her arm like a projxisition of Euclid.
Trebelli veils her round arms with transparent
lace; always the prettiest way of showing off a
pretty arm, and accompanies herself with her
head over her shoulder several times during the
evening.
What still strikes one most about Trebclli’s
singing is that she always gives you the idea of
revelling in her vocalisation. She passes through
bewildering passages of trills and roulades and
shakes, and invariably emerges with an easy,
delighted smile that lights up her whole face, and
the entire house into the bargain. On fjaturi lay
night she sang 12 times, winding up with the
Laughing Song, when she produced a wonderful
imitation of that silvery laugh we used to hear so
much aljout before the New Woman came in with
a guffaw to match her bloomers. Trebelli'* tones
are sweet and limpid, although not largo, but even
with half her voice she would be worth listening
to for the sake of her artful way of making so very
much of what she has. Mr. John Prouse, a bari-
tone with a beard, from Maoriland. was the only
•other contributor to the programme, which may
sound monotonous, but was nothing of the sort in
reality.
“La Poupeo” is following the “Geisha” at
Her Majesty’s this week, tilling up time till
“Robin Hood ” goes on next Saturday night. To
my mind, Kenningham is now imparting a too-
•elaborate girly-girlyness to the part of Launcc-
lot, blurring the line between Launcelot and
lunatic. Dorothy Vane tills La Poupeo to per-
fection, especially in her white satin wedding-
dress and roundest stare. You get the meaning
of all her absurd pretty little mannerisms in other
parts when you see her play the doll. She never
gets rid of them—in fact, the toddle of the doll
seems to have become part of her. Miss Joey
Cassilis replaces Carrie Moore as Henri with a
high-pitched voice and golden hair.
The other night, when “ Ma Mie Rolette” was
•on. good old Howard Vernon forgot all about his
(leaf right ear that had been chopped off, and
when a pretty chorus-girl whispered into the right
ear at an off time in the play, heard all she said
and whispered back with interest. A voice from
the gods called out immediately, “the other sido!”
and Mr. Vernon looked dazed.
“ Buried Alive ” is the name of a melodrama at
the Cri., understood to bo the work of a local
playwright, though nobody has had the courage to
own ui» to it yet in public. A large and friendly
house looked in on the opening night. Rignold
sat alone in one of the boxes. He and Mrs.
Rignold are leaving us shortly, going to try their
luck in London. Pretty Lilian Wheeler watched
from the front row her second lead, Miss Roxy
Barton, play leading lady in junk cambric and a
big black hat. From the title of the play you
would scarcely expect much dressing, and this is
one of the few things in which you will not be dis-
appointed. Hope Mayne’s black locks support a
rod rose effectively, and Mr. Diver airs some well-
creased suits and waistcoats, but the rest of the
performance hits rather the head than the eye.
Yon will be sorry to hear the Yietoria Salon is
now no more—you never went to the Salon, did
you ? Yes, Sydney’s latest attempt at capturing
■culchaw in a afternoon tea cup has floundered
hopelessly, and all that remains is the Ladies’
Club, where a woman can go and brush her hair
and look in the glass at her ease.
Everybody has a different reason for the shut-
ting-up of the Salon. Financial reasons are the
real ones. Culchaw didn’t pay its way. But,
then well. Ihirty women left in a bunch once
when Lady Mary Lygon was expected and a
.'Salon official asked them to move off the bannis-
ters, as only the few naicest were to receive. The
.Salonites said that wasn’t what they had come to
•a Salon for ; and all the other reasons are like
unto that one.
The sudden death of Mr. W. B. Mitchell, of
Carthona, Darling Point, has cast quite a gloom
•over a larsje circle. Although less widely known
than his wife, the dashing “Mrs. Willie,” Mr.
Mitchell was much liked and respected. He had
been in indifferent health for years, and awhile
ago the doctors reported that his death mi Hit
happen at any time. On Friday night the
Mitchells had a theatre-party to the “Geisha,”
mid on Saturday morning Mr. Mitchell was seized
■with paralysis, after which he was never con-
scious.

W rites my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Tho mismanagement of vice regal ‘ hospi-
tality’ during this last Cup time surpassed all
understanding. Lord Richard and his noble col-
league, the callow Hindlip, did the things they
ought not to have done, and left undone the
things they ought to have done, for all the world
as though they were a pair of wicked practical
jokers, rather than two guileless creatures suffer-
ing from severe mental strain. The cruellest
blunder of the week fell upon pretty tough vic-
tims, who. perhaps, found some consolation in the
thought that a good, solid grievance is often a
useful thing to have about the house. A V.R.C.
steward and his wife —so the story goes—were
invited to lunch in the Governor's room on Cup-
day. 'I hey hadn’t angled for the awful distinc-
tion —in fact., they would have preferred to feed
with their own private party, but out of respect
for the Rritish Constitution and all that sort of
thing they toddled off at the appointed time
to the elegant sanctum under the Grand Stand,
'i hey were met by the wan, worried aide-in-chief,
who stammered something to the effect that the
invite was an—er—awfully vexing mistake, dnn-
cherknow, whereupon the shunted “guests” fled
hack to their friends. On Oaks Day the Vice-
regal invite was repeated, Lord Dick waiting on
the steward in person to wequest the pleashaw of
making atonement for the previous mistake. The
steward accepted with a calm, matter-of-fact
smile, and took his wife along to the Vice-regal
eating-place as before. Tins time they found
nobody outside the loom, or in it. The dazed
aido-de-kong must have forgotten that a guber-
natorial luncheon wasn't ordered. His Ex. had
gone down the Bay with the Admiral, and the
rest of the exalted ones, including Lord Neville,
were taking their turkey and champagne in vari-
ous parts of the carriage-paddock, no doubt.
Anyway, there was no Vice-regal party, so the
twice-disappointed couple fell back once more on
their own refreshments.

“Talking of braving wedding trips? How’s
this for a bridal beginning? A rich Mellwurno
girl and a mailboat officer had the knot tied
securely a week or two ago, but when the deck

maiden name a week back,’ explained the

passage.’ At the failure of their ruse the pair
were distraught. The bride had friends in the
Adelaide 1\ and O. office. She arranged to go by
train to tho City of Churches, and sec if tho
matter could be arranged. No, P. and O. law is

now Mrs. Second Officer —and she could not sail
with hubby. Bathed in tears, the bride waved
farewell to the ‘happy man,’ and took passage in

retired Major-General Downes, who will hold

badly ; but their anger knows no bounds.

llousono more. Some say he died of small-pox

—it strengthens the system to resist, oppose and
overcome the attacks of disease. You’ll hear
someone say, “So-and-so has "a strong constitu-
tion ” ; follow that up and you’ll find that So-and-
so follows the golden rule of being temperate in all
things, and pays attention to diet and exercise.
Does he or she keep up this strong constitution by
taking medicine or swallowing pills ? No indeed 1
They have discovered that prevention is better
than cure. Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa places a means
in the hands of everyone to build up amd maintain
a sound constitution, which enables its possessor
to travel his life’s journey without the aches and
pains which are in many cases preventible. Thus
we come round again to sound common sense
based on experience.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *

future as “ Early Closing Bill.”
A reference by Emily Soldene to Nellie
Stewart as “Mrs. Musgrove” reads strangely.
Have the nuptials been announced ?
Parallel portraits in the Sketch show a
truly remarkable resemblance between Queen
Victoria and Oom Paul, especially about the eyes
and mouth.
There are' over 40 ex-Ministers of the
Crown now knocking about N.S.W., and every
man of them tells you confidentially that the
country is going to the dogs.
November 14 was the anniversary birth-
day of N.S.W. ex-Treasurer John See, 1851 (Hun-
tingdon) and N.S.W. ex-Treasurer William
M'Millan, 1850 (North of Ireland).
Melb. Table Talk announces that “ Mr.
Reid is laying low.” Well! he has not been
“cackling” so much as usual; but is it certain
he is not “sitting?” Possibly G.H.R. is
“brooding.”
The next Victorian Judge must be selected
from three men —J. B. Box, H. B. Higgins, and
I. A. Isaacs. There has never been such a scar-
city of eligible men for the bench since the pro-
vince was proclaimed.
All three of the Wardens and P.M.’s who
had the tough job of keeping order in the
humming days of the Barrier are now dead, tho’
none of them was much more than middle-aged—
Wyman Brown, G. H. Gower, and C. L. Badham.
Mrs. Search, recently deceased at Rich-
mond (N.S.W.) and alleged to be in her 100th
year, was really in her 93rd or 94th. She was
an Australian native, as also is Thomas John, of
Kurrajong, certainly now in his hundredth year.
The proprietor of Sydney’s most prosper-
ous tote —it is said on authority that the profits
often reach .£2OO a week—was at one time a
policeman and in that capacity was noted as a
most assiduous hounder-down of the illicit betting-
shop.
Melbourne Age alleges that the Earl of
Caithness has just died penniless in Wairoa
hospital. His other name was Murray McGregor,
and he had enjoyed his landless title for a number
of years at Clive (M.L.) where he made a humble
living at odd jobs. Whitaker’s Almanac says

The case of punster Zox, late M.P. for
East Melbourne, is another proof that there is no
money in politics. Zox died quite penniless. At
one time he claimed to be worth a few thousands,
but, if the money ever existed except on paper,
he lost it all. He lived in cheap lodgings, and
had no means but his Parliamentary “screw.”
If N.S.W. Speaker Abbott can hold his
post for three months more he will have sat in the
Chair longer than any of his predecessors. He
first presided in the Assembly 22nd Oct., 1890.
W. M. Arnold was elected Ist Nov., 1865, and
died Ist March, ’75. With the exception of
Wigram Allen, all the other Speakers passed
rapidly.
Percy Claye, aged 47, telegraph-master at
West Maitland, N.S.W., shot himself dead, last
week, while temporarily insane. Like many an-
other good man, he had damaged his nerves by
high-pressure sound-receiving in the head-offices
at Melbourne and Sydney. He was, long ago,
one of Victoria’s crack operators, and the N.S.W.
service contained no more efficient or better-
respected man.

Parliament in 1871, when he
was the youngest member of
the House. After the pass-
ing of 28 years he finds him-

Chamber.

A well-known figure about Sydney was
the recently-deceased Emile Doublet. Besides
having become, during nearly a quarter-century’s
residence, a favorite with all who knew him, the
kindly old bachelor had been constituted a sort
of godfather-in-ordinary to the children of the
French community in Sydney. “ That is all ver’
nice,” he said, not long ago to the writer; “ very
plea-saunt; but”—with his characteristic twinkle
—“ the ai-rre of Sydney is so good for the ladies
that it corns rathair expen-seef 1 ”
Adelaide holds a curiosity. When C. M.
Bagot, sec. of S.A. Agricultural and Horticultural
Society, died, his billet (worth .£4OO a year and a
liberal commission on subscriptions) • was tem-
porarily taken by W. J. Ashton. The latter was
offered the plum permanently, but declined, on
the sole ground that, as a member of the Rechabite
Order, he could not consistently be secretary of a
society which held a wine-show ! Ashton belongs
to an old Adelaide legal and clerical family,
attached to the same little tabernacle as Chief-
Justioe Way.

“ Mrs. Gladstone,” says an
English writer, “is as much
entitled to be called ‘ The Grand
Old Lady ’ as her husband was to
be named ‘ The Grand Old
Man.’ It is admitted on all hands
that the deceased statesman owed
much to her constant help and
oare. The great Parliamentarian
was fond of deluding himself into
the belief that he had little love-
affairs with different girls. When
their billets-doux used to drop out
of his pocket, Mrs. Gladstone
would secretly return them re-
marking : ‘lt keeps my husband
in a good humor, you know.’
How many other wives have
shown such sense and tact ! ”
J. J. Miller, lately defeated at the Hume,
challenges Lyne to resign N.S.W. Premiership and
re-contest the seatwithhim. Thesix-times-defeated
Miller runs largely to cheek—a common failing
with men who have stalked a community as lay-
preachers. Miller, in his itinerant Gospel days,
was known around the bush as the “ Ironbark
Bishop. ”
Northcroft, who proposes contesting
Kumara (Maoriland) against Premier Seddon,
learned something about the relative sizes of
people during his canvass. Among other electors
he found a long-bearded hatter in a gully, and
after gossiping for awhile, Northcroft started in
to talk politics. “ I’m going to stand for Kumara,
Jack,” he said at last. Whereupon, staring at
the candidate with bulging eyes and open mouth,
the ancient exclaimed “ Good God ! is Dick,
dead?” That was the whole philosophy of the
West Coast in a sentence. Dick and the Almighty
are the two unanswerable propositions in West-
land.

=£2ooo a year—to spend the rest of his days among

Buller were in the 60th Rifles together in the

Vic. Parliamentary law dealing with con-
tempt of Parliament did not always find publishers
as tractable as Ebbsworth, of the Australasian,
and Packer, of the Age. Hugh George, after-
wards manager of S.j\J. Herald, when managing
the Argus declined point-blank to pay any fine or
fee and went doggedly to prison for having
printed the assertion that a speech of Chief Sec.
McCulloch “bristled with falsehood.” Parliament
prorogued, however, a few days later and thus
terminated the trouble. George Dill, another
Argus publisher, was similarly adjudged guilty
of contempt—his paper had alluded to Fraser,
M.P. for Creswick, as “ a privileged ruffian
but like the publishers of to-day he paid the fees
and was released.
An interesting incident in Anglo-Boer re-
lationships. At the conclusion of the negotiations
after Majuba Hill (1881), Major Stewart, who had
been Chief of Staff to the unlucky Colley, and had
taken a prominent part in the diplomatic work
after his chiefs’ death, presented Joubert with a
sword. This was a Stewart family heirloom,
having been worn by the grandfather of the
Major when serving with Wellington in the
Peninsula. Stewart became Genera], and led
the ill-fated flying column during the Khartoum
expedition, when he was killed. Joubert there-
upon returned the sword to his family, with a
graceful letter, duly treasured by them, expres-
sive of regard for a gallant foe. Abuse of the
Boer rarely comes, it is worth noting, from those
who have had anything to do with him.
A newsboys’ fete was given at Hobart the
other day, when the following speech was made
by newspaper-boss Rex : “ I once knew a bo.y as
poor as any of you here. He got 6s. a week in
our (Mercury) office, and used to light fires and
take out papers, but he conducted himself well
and gave all his wages to his mother, who was a
widow. Well, he learned to set type, and he got
a “ease,” and then he began to write reports of
things, and he went to Sydney, where he got
good situations and became a member of Parlia-
ment. Now he is a Minister of the Crown ! His
name is O’Sullivan. Now, if you boys mind your
work, and are good to your mothers, you may be
aldermen or members of Parliament, or perhaps
even Ministers of the Crown.”
Poor old Roper, who lies in Merriwa
(N.S.W.) cemetery, used to look back with
marked pleasure on but one incident of his
long life (he died at 72) —his discovery of
the Roper River. He set out with Leichhardt
from Brisbane to Port Essington in 1844, and
although the whole journey was successful, and
one to be very proud of, John Roper recalled
ecstatically only the one bit to which he was per-
mitted to attach his name. Roper was not, as is
sometimes made to appear, wholly neglected on
his return. He was made C.P.S. at Albury, and
afterwards Inspector of Stock. Besides, he was
personally pushing, and capable of taking care of
himself. He was a Norfolk (Eng.) man, and
arrived in Australia in his 19th 3 7 ear.
W. J. Lambie, the Age war-correspon-
dent, is one of the few pressmen who does his
work with solemn seriousness and “feels ” abuses
with ten times the resentment of the one who
suffers them. Lambie is also a military expert,
or considers himself one, and his criticisms of
the tactics of the British officers, when he returns
and has no censor over his pen, should be most
vigorous. On one occasion Lambie took the local
military authorities to task, and with charac-
teristic fury referred to the imported swash-
bucklers as “ Imperial carpet-baggers.” The par
went in proof to editor Windsor, who promptly
scored it through for its violence and tossed it
into the opening of the copy-lift leading to the
composing-room on the top floor. But at that
moment it happened the lift was overhead, and
the slip of paper dropped unnoticed to the ground-
floor, allowing Lambie’s paragraph to appear next
day in all its pristine glory. Before 10 o’clook
there was a procession of infuriated imported
officers to demand from David Syme the meaning
of this outrage. Lambie in private life is a
small, wiry man of uncertain age, who cultivates
daffodils m his spare time and startles Melb.
Block by turning out in a bell-topper, a khakee
coat and a pair of tweed trousers.
Rev. Taylor, the China missioner who left
Sydney for Melbourne the other day, is head of
a great Taylor family, all of whom seem on fire
to save John’s soul. For instance, he has four
sons, two daughters, and eight nephews and
nieces at evangelical work in that vineyard.
There are 300 single white women connected with
the mission residing in inland China.
“ O. T.” : The Melbourne papers are treat-
ing the late Ephraim Zox badly. In life and
white waistcoat Zox passed for a wit—perhaps his
genial squint had some share in the illusion. But
the poor man is no sooner dead than the journals
proceed to burst up the fable by quoting his good
sayings. Such stuff as any level-headed circus
down would scorn to utter they are giving forth
as poor Zox’s side-splitters. So that when they
go on to write about his sterling qualities of
character, the reader realises that he needed all
the virtues he gets credit for to compensate for
the tortures he must have inflicted upon society
as a professional purveyor of good sayings. It i 3
a lamentable fact that most current Hebrew
humor consists of puns of the hopelessly-Cockney
order. See how poor Salomons, Q.C., is thus
afflicted ! However, the Chosen race can’t give
us Heines all the time.
Dr. Buntine, Transvaal surgeon, who may
be the first Australian Y.C., was one of the best-
known figures of Melb. University seven or eight
years back. He was one of the crack footballers
of the Scotch College when he entered the medical
school—a long, lanky lad, with very fair hair and
a pink-and-white baby face, that made him look
a mere boy even when he had graduated, and
earned for him the sobriquet of “ Baby” Buntine.
Both at school and at the “ shop” he was a promi-
nent athlete, footballed with the defunct. Uni-
versity Club, and once carried off the University
championship in all-round athletics. As one of
the resident surgeons of the Melb. hospital, he
became a popular and jolly figure in youthful
Melbourne, but his juvenile appearance was much
against his chances of success, and he decided at
last to light out to a country where greybeards
were scarce.
The whirligig of time ! The other day
one or two legal applecarts were severely shaken
by a rumor that N.S.W. Govt, had decided to
offer the vacant District-Court judgeship not,
in the ordinary way, to one of the Crown
prosecutors, but to that chilly and deliberate law-
yer, Mr. C. G. Heydon, who is understood to
have long had his steely accusing eye fixed on a
Supreme Court seat. It seems that, as a matter of
fact, the D.C. appointment was actually offered to
Mr. Heydon, who expressed his willingness to ao-
cept it. (A N.S.W. minor judgeship carries, with
£l5OO a year salary, substantial travelling ex-
penses and a retiring pension of £750 a
year, and is a very comfortable post not
HE NEVER THOUGHT OF IT.
HIS LARGER HALF: “ And who’ll mind me when
you are gone ?”
to be despised in these hard times even by
a high-up lawyer.) Then Minister Crick burst up
the arrangement by abruptly telling his col-
leagues that Mr. Heydon should never be made a
judge with his consent, and that if such an ap-
pointment was carried out they could take the
consequences. Crick has a substantial personal
grievance against Heydon dating from Dean-trial
time, but, apart from thet, he is quite right on
the general question. Mr. Heydon is a good
lawyer and a most “correct” person, but he
would make as objectionable a Judge as Justice
Windeyer.
