Aboriginalities.
“Dominie”: We hear such distressing
tales concerning bush schoolmasters that it’s time
someone gave the other side. The bush teacher (I
am one) is not the unfortunate we read about.
He has a decent salary, and is virtually his own
master 364 days per year (the Inspector comes once).
Reckoning in Saturdays, he spends 14 weeks in
holidays yearly on full pay, is looked up to as a
sort of social king among the bucolics, gets his
milk, butter and firewood practically on the nod,
while his groceries and other parcels are brought
from town to him by neighbor Spinks, who also
gives him a owt. of seed spuds for the garden. In
return, he is sociable; he measures tanks, calcu-
lates road-batters, areas of ploughing, illuminates
the address for the departing postmaster, gives the
local parsons a bob or two, takes a ticket in the
local sweep, and lends The Bulletin. He rides,
fishes, bikes, or shoots, morning and evening,
visits the station where he gets good tennis and
meets a white circle, and then when Easter, Mid-
winter, or Xmas fly round, he’s off to the Smoke.
If the nights hang long he can chum in with Tod-
hunter, Conway, Julius Caesar and other family
pets and fag for the higher exam. The bush-
teacher only growls because there is really nothing
to growl about, and he’s gob suoh a lot of spare
time to growl in.

Sir,—l want to tell you wot i think o you in black
and white. You hammered Jimmy fur usin bad lanwidge
he ses he ony sed bloody an tole you to go to ell. Now as
is father usis that langwidge an hes better man nor you
an shud no wot is propper fur is owne chile to do an say
i therefur worn you not to be after itting Jimmy agen or
is father will soon be ome from rousaboutin an if i tell
im o your conduo he wud praps go down an pul your
hear an i doant want eny trubble threw the children. I
wud like you to pay moar atension to the speliin of the
children as 1 am pertickler o this part o the lernin insted
nockin them about fur usin a fue simppel wurda that is
armless.

N.S.W. sclloolboy’s essay on “ Flowers ” :
Our native rose is a glorious blossom. It’s proper
name is Borneo. The Botanical Gardens gives pleasure
to thousands, 30me of which may not have much pleasure;
they may not have any food or money, but are more
pleased than if they had plenty of food, hut no flowers to
look at. Some men, if you ask them have they got a
flower garden, they will tell you they would not be
bothered with flowers, well they ought to be dead. A
home without flowers is like the home of a savage beast.
The place were flowers is sold is called a Forest shop.
Some flowers shut up at night as merry goes. Even
schoolmasters are fond of flowers.

“Parcel ”: The fact that the crows in
Booligal district have taken to building their nests
on the telegraph-poles shows that this interesting
bird is a stickler for business before pleasure
when it comes to a choice between superintending
the lambing in the Riverina district and travelling
in search of a sufficiently tall nesting-tree.

N.S.W., once ploughed up three eggs, which were
kept for a while to see what the product would be.
When a month had passed without result, the
box containing the eggs was stowed away in a
shed and forgotten for over a year, when it was
found to contain one fair-sized carpet snake and
two tails 1

The sort of thing that may still happen in
way-back N.S.W. It is a message from Wanaar-
mg:—
. Dr °ver M‘Donald had a lively time when near Wanaar-
mg with the Keroongooloo cattle. Some of his bullocks
went mad and charged the camp. The men took refuge
in the vehicle, which the animals repeatedly charged
after tossing the camp utensils, bag and baggage about
in all directions. Another beast charged a buggy which
was passing along, but fortunately the driver noticed it
in time, and escaped by laying the whip on the horses
and galloping away. A third beast charged carrier
Westlake s bullock-team, and lamed two of his best bul-
locks, but old George, pluckily going to the rescue of his
pets with a cudgel, knocked the animal down, and, stun-
ning it for a time, he drove away, thus getting out of its
reach.

“ A.G.” : Recently saw a fire started in a
curious way. When lighting my pipe, I dropped
a few lucifers. Then a hen came along, and,
picking amongst ’em, lighted one, which set fire
to some newspapers I’d been reading. Eventual
damage—’bout £25.

Yangan : Re the forensic “ swearing ”of
Chinese. I remember once in Bor-
neo, when a Chinaman was on trial
for murder, the only non-circum-
stantial evidence was that of ac-
oused’s late partner; who duly blew
out a match, and then at prisoner’s request
smashed a plate; but still dissatisfied, accused
asked that the witness should decapi-
tate a white rooster and repeat his
evidence. Fowl procured accord-
tngly, but, as witness refused to

“C.E.”: Be “Flabby
P.’s ” par. (8., 7/10/’99), the
fact that a snake changed
its skin when 7in. long proves
nothing but that the snake
was still growing. Reptiles
and fishes do not cease grow-
ing at maturity, but con-
tinue to increase in size at
irregular rates until death.
I don’t mean to assert that if
an adder lived long enough
he would reach the size of a
carpet-snake, but an adder
will certainly keep on grow-
ing as long as it lives.

How are the mighty fallen ! Queensland,
on account of the falling-off in the quality of its
horses, was unable to supply the War Office with
a few chargers the other day. But you can buy
backblocks weeds and scrubbers at five shillings a
head in the country towns any day. The
übiquitous bike is the alleged cause of the trouble.

“ Eller ” : Many N.S. W. bush “darnces ”
begin with a regular rough-and-tumble. “Gents.”
sit one side of the room ; ladies the other ; and
when the signal is given there is a general stampede
for partners. If you don’t manage to “collar a
tart ” first shot—and no more “ skirt ” turns up —
you may as well go home straight.
Tall yam from “ Waira-
rapa ”: —
Have seen sohnapper speared whole-
sale in Maoriland waters. When the
fish get into the shallows to feed,
their fins, sticking out of the water,
locate them, and if approached quietly
they may be speared either from shore
or a boat. A friend of mine who
is a skilful spearer has been in the
South Sea Islands for years, and has
caught fish by diving for them —hold-
ing a foot of line with hook attached.
Fact! Has also dived and tied three
sleeping tiger-sharks together; then
returned to the surface and made a
splashing with the oars, and the fun
bpgan. True ! Natives swim among
sharks with impunity.

“Ulawarra”: Came upon
M‘Moran, the other day, knock-
ing out stumps with the back of
an axe. He is a long-armed man
and swings the axe wildly and
erratically around his head.
Noticed the blade of a shovel
secured to the back of his head,
and asked what on earth that
meant ? “ Well,” said he, “ some-
toimes whin Oi hits Oi misses,
and thin th’ axe ketches me just
there. If it wurnt for that plate
Oi’d have had me brains knocked
out ivery t’ree minnits ! ” Then
he made a powerful swipe at the
top of the stump, missed —and,
sure enough, the back of the axe
landed on the shovel-blade with a
bang that nearly put Mac. on his
face. “Didn’t I tould ye?” he
spluttered. “ Be the powers, sorr,
it’s dangerous wurrk entoirely
whin a mahn can’t kape his fut-
tin’.” Told him he should bar
the “La Blanche,” but he hadn’t
heard of that artist.

A deputation recently asked
Tas. Govt, that a railway be
made from the Western line to
“ the Promised land.” They’re
great on scripture names in Tas-
mania. En route from Hobart to
Launceston you pass Jerusalem and Jericho, see
the Lake of Tiberius, and get a glimpse of the
Jordan.
* • * *
j jLittle Tom Haynes, aged twelve and a
half, went missing on his father’s Vic. Western
District selection the other day, and Tommy’s
father, after search, found him in the dam. The
small boy was hauled out with difficulty, and sub-
jected to the treatment prescribed for the restora-
tion of the apparently-drowned ; he came to after
about an hour’s hard graft, in which everybody
within call gave a hand. Tommy explained next
day. He had intended to commit suicide, he
said. Having lost everything on the Cup, he felt
it was neither advisable nor customary to live any
longer. His losings, it transpired, consisted of
eigliteenpence and a cocoanut-ring.

Last year, while weighing wool at Bulla-
green station, near Gilgandra, N.S.W., a man
lost his vest, watch and chain. The police were
informed, but no find. The other day the owner
bf Bullagreen got information from an English
wool-manufacturing firm that in a bale of wool
bearing the Bullagreen brand was found a vest,
watch and chain ; and now, after many days, the
owner will get back his property somewhat com-
pressed, and suspicion is raised from many people.

Lightning does strike the eypress-pine even where

Dear Bulletin.—Can any of your readers
name for me the animal herewith described?
Size, nearly that of the brown scrub opossum,
which it also slightly resembles in shape, but at
night, when it wakes and stretches itself round
the cage, it is exactly the shape of an iguana in
body. Head, narrower and longer than the
opossum’s; tongue, about long, and very
thick. Third toe of the forefeet twice as long as
the others. Color, broad black and cream stripes
running from nose along back and sides, and
shading into a light grey towards tail. Tail, 18in.
long, with black stripe to within 4in. of tip, which
is salmon-pink color. It does not coil its tail
around anything as the opossum does, neither
does it carry same over its back, like the squirrel.
Its cry resembles that of the flying-squirrel when
frightened, but is much louder and harsher. Its
favorite foods are milk and honey. Not even
20-year Queensland-bush-residents have been able
to “ place ” the creature for me.—G.C.O.

In a recent London Pelican appears a
short Bulletin story, “ His Second Wife ”
almost, as a matter of course, without acknowledg-
ment. The Pelican has joined the literary vul-
tures.
The errors of a giddy old man are apt to
find him out in his respectable parental character.
Stated that the King of the Belgians was lately
taking a quiet stroll arm-in-arm with his daughter
Clementine, who is the apple of his roving royal
eye, when a profane bystander remarked in a loud
voice—“ What! the King has got a new girl !’’
whereat father and daughter hurried blushingly
along. It made even King Leopold turn red.
For the moment, perhaps, he wished he had lived
a virtuous life for his dear child’s sake. And then,
probably, he began to think of all the glorious
times when he wasn’t good—of the suppers, and
the private ballet shows, and the consignments of
Maiden Tribute from New Babylon, &c , &c.,
and his temporary depression cleared off, no doubt.
Sin has such a wealth of memories to draw upon !

There are many curious ways of dying. At
Bluff (M.L.) Freezing Works a young man was
oarrying the carcase of a sheep up stairs. The
suspensory hook was fixed in one of the hind legs
which hung down in front. Carcase slipped back-
wards, and as it went the hook caught in the
man’s abdomen and disemboweled him. He died
two days later.

K uppers Elberfeld Lagei
else runs about midway between Townsville and Cairns,
und’about 150 miles from the Gulf of Carpentaria, and
the find was made about 12 miles from the mouth. Upon
the banks of the Tally and other rivers in this vicinity
tows a large tree known as the “ Leichhardt tree,
because the lost explorer once subsisted entirely upon its
fruit for weeks. In appearance this tree resembles some-
what the Norfolk Island pine, the branches growing
evenly tier above tier, and the whole tapering to a Point
at top The leaves are dark-green, trisected, and of the
size of a small plate. The fruit is somewhat like “ three-
quarters,” and is verv bitter in flavor, though after being'
soaked several times in wafer it makes nice jam, and will
give an appetite when all else fails. —A.H.

One Tas. cockie rouses his hands out of
bed early in a new way. Bags form their bed-
clothes, and generally before daylight cockie
marches into the hut with, “ Don t want to dis-
turb yez, min, but Mickey is digging in the pad-
dock beyant, and Oi must have a few bags.” And
the subsequent tug at the “ Sydney blankets ”
(slang for potato-bags) would wake the Seven
Sleepers.

“X.” : On opening a rabbit which I re-
cently shot, I found in the stomach, besides some
vegetable stuff, a quantity of live worms, about
fin. long, of the thickness of a medium-sized
needle, and tapering to a point at ends. Have
often found worms in wallabies, but never before
in a rabbit. I “ pass ” bunny now.

A sugar-mill in the Far North is an in-
teresting sight, and presents a few valuable
lessons. In Cairns district you visit a mill and
notice that nearly all hands are little brown men,
garrnentless—but for a pair of trousers. Fireman
—a vigorous stumpy Jap., all smiles and perspira-
tion. Outside —kanakas by the score, with oc-
casionally a surviving white man cropping up on
the outskirts. The planter’s idea of Paradise.

A tale of a man and a dog :
Old Corncob c wned 80 acres of lucerne, and Toby was
his dog—a large, hard-working dog, who got more kicks
than kindness, but did his duty dogfully notwithstand-
ing. He was a conscientious dog, was Toby, and camped
chief!}' on a rise in the paddock which commanded a view
of the fences where neighbor’s cattle could get in. Now,
Toby had never seen his master in a decent suit of
clothes, and didn’t miss h : m when he went to town
to buy a new suit; and on the Sunday, seeing
a gaily-dressed man let down the sliprails, and
jog into the paddock on a horse minus bridle and
saddle, he resolved to Take Steps in the matter. The
man was reaching over, trying to slip home the rail with-
out dismounting, when Toby suddenly put in a volley
from the rear. The horse plunged forward, the man dropped
the rail, grabbed the horse’s mane hard and shouted
“ Lie down yon —— brute ! ” But no horse had been
allowed in that paddock within the recollection of Toby,
and the memory of past kicks nerving him, he heeled
that horse round the paddock. Heeled him and headed
him and twisted him, and never heeded the language of
the rider, and when the church-goers got interested,
Toby warmed up to his work. Maloney’s and Donovan’s
cattle came in at the open slip-rails, but Toby was busy
and didn’t see them. And when, in the centre of the
paddock, in view of a mixed audience, an awful mad man
was shot into a clump of briars, Toby took no notice, but
persevered until the horse was galloping down the road as
if the Devil was after him. Then Toby came back, and
the man being more like his old self by reason of nn’id
and blood and blasphemy, Toby recognised him, helped
to put out the cattle, and went over triumphantly to his
camp on the rise. Now —his bones hallow the spot. He
died, a martyr to duty, of a gunshot wound, and a com-
pound comminuted fracture of the skull.

“ Jim Pan ” ; Be Tommy Tanna’s sense of
humor (“ Ros,” B. 14/10/’99). The handy-man ab
the Canecutters’ Curse Hotel, in Twowharves,
was a kanaka, and, one day, when he had humped
a bagman’s traps from the Tomki to the pub.,
the drummer facetiously remarked, “ Here,
Tommy Tanna, here’s a tanner fa ya trouble.”
“"You makum big mistake, mista,” said the
darkie, “my name not Tommy Tanna—my name
Tommy Two-bob.” He got it!

The English Mechanic thus knocks
on the head a notion which has cost the
life of many an Australian well-sinker :
Candles will burn in air that would instantly
kill any man. Nobody should descend into a
well till a caged-bird has been lowered, and
pulled up again, especially as foul air is to be
found in almost every well.
* • * *
In a Western Q. town lately a
State orphan was flogged with a cat-o’-
nine-tails until his back was raw. The in-
genious flogger then tied the youngster to
a horse’s tail and left him in the yard all
night. The bush is fertile in cruelty.
“ R. 8.”: “ C.H.R.” (B. 14/10/’99) takes
pains to describe the Tasmanian devil when the
“mountain devil” had been asked about. The
Tas. devil is a daysure (Australian “ native cat ”
family), but the mountain devil is a small, horned
lizard, fearsomely ugly but perfectly harmless,
found, as far as I know, only in W.A.

The Little School Marm
For the Bulletin
WACH morning I go riding—
A figure lean and brown—
And pass the school-house hiding
In trees near Coolga Town.
With foolish, fond renewal
Of hope, I pass to see
The little school-marm cruel
Who makes a slave of me.
Her stock of modern knowledge
Ts but a little store,
For in an older college
She learnt her wicked lore;
And she, with gay defiance,
Keeps slyly up her sleeve
The sweetest, oldest science —
The lore of Mother Eve.
Each morn an urchin merry
She kisses, nowise coy,
With lips of reddest cherry—
-0 happy little boy!
She tosses him “ Good-morning."
He shrugs his shoulders thin,
Her sweet caresses scorning—
-0 little whelp of sin!
Were 1 that urchin curly
I could not be so blind;
I would not be so surly
If she would be so kind;
I would not be so stupid
Nor act so poor a part—
But turn into a Cupid
And nestle in her heart
No more would I be riding—
A figui e lean and brown —
My passion vainly hiding—
The talk of Coolga Town.
And she my rose, my jewel,
No more, no more would be
The little school-marm cruel
Who makes a slave of me.
C.R.

NINE OPERATIONS UNSUCCESSFUL-
-14 Years’ Suffering from Hydatids.
Now Thoroughly Cured by “ Webber’s Vitadatio.”
READ !
Mr. S. A. PALMER. Nicholson-street, Woodend, 28/10/ , 99.
Vitadatio Institute, Melbourne.
Dear Sir.—lt affords me the greatest of pleasure in bearing testimony to the genuineness and the
power of your wonderful Herbal preparation, “WEBBER’S VITADATIO.” You may rely on my
doing all that lies in my power to let other poor sufferers know of the benefit I have derived from it.
Briefly, the particulars of my case are as follow: —Some 14 or 15 years ago I was suddenly taken
ill with Pleurisy, and shortly afterwards Hydatids formed on the Lungs. I. was treated by
several of our best doctors, but as they failed to give me any relief, I decided to enter the “ Kyneton
Hospital, ” where I was operated on on nine (9) different occasions, and they wanted me to undergo
the tenth, when one day a Mr. E. Jones, of Kyneton, called on mo and told me that from what he
personally knew of “ Yitadatio ” he was sure it would give me great relief, if not altogether cure me.
As I felt I could rely on this gentleman’s word, and as everything else had failed, I thought I would
give it “a fair trial,” with the result that “I am absolutely cured.” The Hydatids began to come
away very shortly after I commenced the medicine, so you may be sure I stuck to it, till at last every
Hydatid has left me. I can honestly say I AM IN BETTER HEALTH AND SPIRITS TO-
DAY THAN I HAVE BEEN FOR THE PAST FIFTEEN YEARS. For this marvellous
change I have to thank Mr. Jones for his kind advice and “WEBBER’S YITADATIO” FOR
THE COMPLETE CURE. I may tell you that during the time I was under the various medical
men I took innumerable quantities of medicine, without any result whatever. Of Herbalists’ and
Specialists’ medicine I have drunk gallons. One Melbourne Specialist in Hydatids guaranteed to cure
me for £6 ; but my case was beyond him. I can honestly say “ WEBBER’S YITADATIO ” ALONE
CURED ME. I give this testimonial of my own free will for the good of suffering humanity, and
out of a grateful heart. I shall be pleased to answer any inquiries and give full particulars to anyone
interested on receipt of stamped-addressed envelope. Trusting some may be led by my experience
and testimony to give “ Webber’s Yitadatio ” a trial.—Yours gratefully,
MARY ANN COLE.
Witnesses: W. J. Pasook, H. D. Vimpanl

■ ' vas absurd to fine the Age publisher
£lOO for the paper’s alleged contempt of Vic.
Parliament in commenting on the Jackson case,
lhe fine should have been one farthing for the
offence against the Legislature’s dignity, plus
*99 19s. llfd. for impudent misrepresentations.
Subtract the few dozens of big advertising
donations from the sum to date of the N.S.W.
bogus-Patriotic fund and what a beggarly total is
left ! Sydney Evening News has now for many
days been whooping-up shilling subscriptions from
the general public and has raked in .£lO or .£l2
in all.
Wanted, a Parliamentary return showing
the members of N.S.W". Assembly who are also
members of any volunteer regiment; and, where
the member holds the rank of officer, how long he
was in the volunteer service before the rank was
conferred on him, and what examination, if any,
he passed.
In a few days more it is estimated that
Redvers Buller will have 100,000 troops in South
Africa. And the whole adult male Boer popula-
tion, both republics included, isn’t 40,000. It is
suggestive of Germany invading France with
20,000,000 men, or Britain sending 50,000,000 to
attack Russia.
For 20 years Melb. people have talked of
getting the Collingwood railway extended into
the city, and at last the work has begun. The
first shovelful of earth was turned on Bth Nov.
Everything comes some day. Even Sydney’s new
city railway-station, and the North Shore bridge,
and the George-street tram will come some day.
Only three short years ago !
“ To go to war with President Kruger, to enforce upon
him reforms in the internal affairs of his State, in which
Secretaries of State, standing in their places, have repu-
diated all right of interference—that would be a course
of actiou which would be immoral.”— Mr. Chamberlain.
Bth May, 1896.
Only three short years ago !
Brisbane Telegraph: “Had the vote”
(at the Queensland Federal Referendum) “been
numerically, as it teas morally, against the Con-
vention Bill,'” &c. Somebody should" take
“ morally ’ into the back yard and slay it with an
axe. The cry of the fellow who was horribly
licked that he was morally successful makes this
globe tired.
To maintain their grasp of office the Q.
Ministry eat the leek daily. The latest is that,
fearing the angry clamor of disappointed followers
hungry for a job on the contemplated Q. Works
Committee, the Govt. declare it to be a non-party
question. Which is one in the solar plexus for
Forrest, Leahy & Co.—the candid friends of the
lioodlewraith.
Henry Copeland’s sledge-hammer battling
for the Sunday-opening of N.S.W. pubs, is only a
continuation, under changed conditions, of the
rum-and-whisky controversy of Gov. Phillip’s
Botany Bay days. In 1801, the controversy was
for a while silenced by an Order-in-Council de-
claring that all pubs., could open on Sundays after
9 p.m. But they could stay open from Saturday
morning till daybreak on Sunday.
It was persistently rumoured that Premier
Seddon contemplated filling some of the recent
vacancies in Maoriland Legislative Council with
women. There is nothing in the Constitution
against the appointment of lady M.’sL.C., and
nothing elsewhere except old habit. And very
old habit is rather in favor of the idea ; the last
" oman-member didn’t disappear out of the Eng-
lish Parliament till about the time of Henry 111.
N.S.W. ex-Premier Reid’s four Transvaal
Contingent attitudes : (1) That N.S.W. should
send no contingent (as expressed in the minute
which Wise read to the Assembly) ; (2) that it
should send one (asexpressed by cable lateronto the
British authorities); (3) that Lyne (who succeeded
Reid just here) had disgraced N.S.W. by not
getting the Cornstalk forces to S’Africa ahead of
other contingents ; (4) when the N.S.W. men (the
Lancers) did get there first, that Lyne was going
off his head over Contingents and making a fool
of himself. More attitudes daily expected. G.
Reid is opening up his consignment of new spring
attitudes and samples will be on view in a few
days.
“It would never do for Westralia to go
into an immediate and unconditional Federation,”
is the good old parrot-cry of the provincialist mob
in Groperland. But who ever asked these weird
persons to do anything of the kind ? There are
over 200 conditions and sub-conditions enumer-
ated in the Convention Bill; the Federation
offered is just full of conditions, about all of which
Westralia was consulted. Of course it wouldn’t
do for W.A. to form an unconditional Federation.
It wouldn’t do for any country to do it. No
country ever did it, or is likely to. There is no such
thing in the world—except in the imagination of
the old Sandgroper, and in the columns of Perth
Horse Marine Herald.
A number of M.L. papers that have
for long 1 been on the rail now begin to fall
off on the side furthest from Seddon. Pal-
merston Standard declares that the Seddon
party has run to seed, and before there can
be again a healthy and vigorous legislative
growth they must be supplanted by an en-
tirely new set of men who will come fresh to
their work, and not sit like jaded warriors as
the present Ministry are doing. Well, per-
haps Seddon is getting tired—he has done
herculean deeds, and colleagues Hall-Jones
and Jock M'Kenzieand Thomas Thompson
are a heavy load for the old man to carry;
but till the new democratic giants fresh to
their work put in an appearance and give
some earnest of their intentions, M.L. can-
not do better than stick to the old captain
who has done so well. The prospect of
goid-rnnmed Russell, who imports his clothes
iroiT l Lngland, and is the incarnation of
haw, is too dreadful.
Brisbane Telegraph again :
A Frenchman feels a little emotion and displays
nhnw.iim ’ wu Enfe - lishman feels a good deal and
snows little. When injustice was being heaped on
Dreyfus, the raqe of Britons was great but forcibly
expressed and calm. 1
True, the rage of Bull at foreign iniquities
is always something appalling. He howls

to massacre a nation.
The decision of Yic. Attorney-General
Isaacs to carry Melb. Tramway Co.’s appeal to
the .Privy Council argues that the rats are leav-
ing the sinking ship, for if there were no other
reason Isaacs aotio.n would alienate much demo-
cratic support from the Turnerites. The A.G. goes
to bolster up the most privileged and most sweat-
ing monopoly that infests Victoria, in its
shameless attempt to evade its rates and, though
it might be explained until further orders that a
barrister is merely an advocate, the opponents of
the tram Co. would inevitably associate the Govt,
with the boodling scheme.
Carruthers’ effort to coerce Premier Lyne
into urging Britain to take over the German
possessions in Papua was stimulated by a rumour
current m Sydney circles that Germany is already
negotiating with Great Britain for the surrender
ot British possessions there. A similar rumour
caused Mcllwraith to plant the Union Jack there
in the days of his lusty vigor, and it was through a
rumour that Russia was on the move that a N S W
Governor in 1826 planted the flag on Albany
Utterance by Herbert Asquith, British
M.P., dedicated to Premier Lyne and Edmund
Barton—the Australians who say it is not our
business to canvass the right or justice of British
wars—we must follow Britain blindly—
There are those who think that when national affairs
are going wronsr it is the duty of the patriot to be silent
lest he should embarrass the negotiations, and that when
the mischief has been done it is. his duty to acquiesce.
To me that is a perverted patriotism which I entirely re-
pudiate.
And Barton’s “perverted patriot,” is all the
worse, because, whatever the British quarrel with
the Boers, Australia has none.
GRACE AFTER MEAT.
SUNDAY-SCHOOL TEACHER : “ What is it your father says after
each meal !”
SMALL MEMBER OF INFANT CLASS : “ Please m he strokes his
stummick this-a-way and”
TEACHER : “ Never mind what he does ; tell me what he SAYS.”
PUPIL : And he says —‘ Ha! ha! Richard is hisself again !’”
About the tallest lie yet told by the sweat-
ing brigade was put forward by a speaker at the
recent Sydney anti-early closing meeting—that
“ early-closing in Melbourne had resulted in 75
per cent, of the small shops in certain suburbs
being to let.” The clumsy person who told this
whopper was most careful not to specify the
alleged suburbs where 75 per cont. of the small
shops are to let. As a matter of fact, he couldn’t
find one where 20 per cent, are to let, and he
probably couldn’t find one where the number of
shops to let has increased since early-closing came
into vogue.

Latest news is that the Somers Vine New
Guinea Land- Syndicate, which nearly managed
to grab the most valuable part of British Papua
without, as provided by law, getting the assent of
the provinces which stand the expense of adminis-
tration, wants <£sooo from N. S. W. for expenses
incurred in its attempted steal. This is the first
case The Bulletin knows of when the burglar
has publicly demanded that the person whose
house he failed to break into shall pay his cab-fare
to the scene of the intended fraud.
“Lithgow ” to The Bulletin :
Holman is not a blank cartridge. He got his blow in
first, fair and square, and then begged poor old Jingo’s
pardon. “ I did not mean to hurt you quite so much,
and it was the other fellow I meant.” We all know
Holman hit straight from the shoulder, and with all his
heart, and when a friend reads his “explanation” he
puts his tongue in his cheek and winks the other eye,
like any other knowing newspaper-bov. When the great
day of Australia comes, as come it will, may Holman be
there to raise again the red flag, first hoisted at the
Eureka Stockade. Also, may Ashton’s keen brain and
steady hand draw up the Constitution of our Australian
Kepublio. Behold the beginning of the Coming of the
Men 1
It is a very poor argument
—poorer than anything- that
sleeps out in the Domain—that
isn’t good enough for an Anti-
Federalist. As an example The
Bulletin receives from Maori-
land a weighty tome of litera-
ture by that absurd self-adver-
tiser, Dr. Laishley, of Auckland,
with this muddy gem embedded
therein :
True, there is further the lofty bon-
bon idea of belonging to a powerful
neighboring confederation, not only
for defence, but for other purposes,
which has been set forth in such per-
fervid language by the Rev. Canon
MacMuray.
But, notwithstanding illustrations
mentioned by the Canon of success-
ful federations, what of the United
States civil war, and the threatened
revolutions in Norway and Sweden,
Austria-Hungary and Finland, and,
also, what of Ireland and Turkey?
whilst England and Japan—isolated
and small countries—shew that Fed-
eration is not necessary for vigorous
and successful national life.
To all appearance this wonder-
ful parochialist thinks that Fin-
land is federated with Russia ;
that Norway and Sweden are a
Federation instead of two dis-
tinct kingdoms only connected
by possessing the same king,
and that Ireland and Turkey,
of all places in the world, are-
federations. And he isn’t aware
that almost the only spell of
peace and prosperity Austria-
Hungary has known is since the
two kingdoms formed their fed-
eration over 30 years ago.
Apropos N. S. W. Labor
Bureau reports re “ the strong
and willing man, the indolent
and lazy, and the old and incap-
able.” Railway-Commissioner
Oliver stated to a middle-aged
man willing and anxious to
work, that the Commissioners had now a rule
under which no man over 37 years of age would
be taken on as an employ 6.
The weary Continuous Govt, of Bananaland
nearly broke its neck, the other evening, over the
Railway Standing Committee Bill. It was gene-
rally believed that positions on the committee
were to be allotted to Govt, discontents as a com-
pensation for certain little disappointments. For-
rest and Leahy joined hands with the Opposition,
but a Labor pervert saved the Dicksonian bacon
at the eleventh hour by a single vote. The
beginning of the end !
The Bulletin is ready to bet that it can-
nominate a speaker who, on any big gold-mining
field in Australia, granted a fair hearing and a-
representative (i.e., unpacked) meeting of miners,
will obtain a majority in condemnation of Austra-
lia’s aid to England in the Transvaal war. The
Bulletin selects a mining audience because it
regards the gold-miner as the most intelligent and
manly of Australian citizens, and the salt of the
nation.
According to London Chronicle, Cham-
berlain had agreed to submit the wildly-humorous
Transvaal claim for ,£1,000,000 on account of
“moral and intellectual damage” by the Jameson
Raid to arbitration. The damages awarded, if
any, were to have been paid by the Chartered
Co., and Rhodes had promised to refund the co.
the sum, whatever it should be, out of his private
purse. No wonder Rhodes worked for war, see-
ing that the war has sunk this question in
oblivion. At the same time the Boer claim was
absurd. All the “ moral and intellectual damage ’’
suffered in the famous Raid was suffered by the
boodling invaders.
The Maoriland Tories, in proposing to go
back on the principle of State land-ownership and
State leasehold and revert to the old land-sale
policy, are terribly hard-up for an excuse. In
fact, they have no excuse, save their love of land-
lordism, land-jobbing, land-grabbing, and land-
booming. Up to now, every bit of decent land
offered on State least-hold has been applied for
over and over again. If the people wouldn’t take
State leaseholds, then it might be reasonably
alleged that they wanted freeholds instead, but
when they are falling over each other to get the
leaseholds, the cry that the principle is generally
disliked is mere foolishness.
Brisbane Telegraph sticks manfully to its
old lie that the Convention Bill does not provide
for One-man-one-vote in Commonwealth elections,
because though an elector may only vote once in
Queensland there is nothing to prevent him
voting again in another province. It is thin
bunkum, but it is probably the best that the
three-preacher paper can invent. The Bill simply
says (iclauses 8 and 30) that at elections for
either Federal House “each elector shall vote
only once ” —nor that he shall vote only once in
each province, or in each electorate, or each parish,
but that he shall vote “only once.” It Wouldn’t
be more specific than that if it tried.
“A ‘fine old English dish’—the Boar’s
head,’’ writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent, “and John Bull knows this is the very time
for him to sit down and carve it, comfortably.
The Venezuelan difficulty over, Germany squared,
France lame, Russia busy in the far East,
America laurel-blinded ! why, there’s only the
boar’s tusks to guard his head. Tim latest betting
on ’Change is 7 to 5 the war is ended before the
yacht-races, and 3 to 1 ‘ we’ are in Johannesburg
in three months. The old moth-eaten lion has of
late been much camphorated, and is,-no doubt,
making stupendous preparations to impress the
stalls, where Europe sits observant.”
Melb. Argus, for a change—and no doubt
as a political move—takes the side of Public Inte-
rests in opposition to a powerful monopoly :
The Tramway Co. is notoriously before Parliament at
the present moment. Legislation which deeply touches
its pocket' is being pressed forward by one section of the
House. Not only that, but the Tramway Co. is in the
hands of the Ministry, for the Government is entitled any
day to introduce.a bill to revise the fares charged on the
lines. . . . It may be confidently submitted that no mem-
ber of the Ministry ought to lie in anyway allied with the
Tramway Co., and yet we find the Attorney-General ac-
cepting an enormous fee from its coffers, and leaving hie
duties in order to plead (in London) for the company as
against the municipalities.
Premier Lyne wisely decided not to com-
mit himself to the gorgeous proposal to hold a
Great Exhibition in Sydney in 1901. Sydney had
an exhibition in ’79 and the result was a dead loss
of .£258,000, Just at that time N.S.W. suddenly
increased its borrowings from ,£BOO,OOO to about
■£2,800,000 a year, and its revenue increased by
about .£1,500,000 a year, almost the whole of
this sum being derived from the adoption of a
riotous policy of wholesale auctioneering of publio-
lands. The boom created just then by the throw-
ing about in all directions of the new loan and
land-auction funds is commonly set down to the
fact, that “ the exhibition made the world ac-
quainted with our products and opened up new
markets,” but it was a loan and land-sale boom,
pure and simple. In any case N.S.W. made a
Great Exhibition of itself on Circular Quay, the
other day, which is quite enough for the present.

“hope to have an early oppor-

Cured in Ten Days.
THE EDITOR OF THE OLDEST NEWSPAPER
IN VICTORIA EXPRESSES GRATEFUL
APPRECIATION.
W. G. Hearne, Esq. Dear Sir,—Permit me to express
my grateful appreciation of the value of your Bronchitis
Cure. I had, some months ago, a severe attack of
Bronchitis, and took your medicine-, with the result that
at the end of ten days the complaint had completely left
me. We are now never without the medicine in the

With the holiday season coming on, and num-
bers of people either going themselves or sending their
families to the mountains, the enterprising burglars’ har-
vest has begun. Provident people, however, are provid-
ing against his visits by storing their valuables—plate,
jewellery, papers, etc.—at the Sydney Safe Deposit, Ash-
street (back of Palings), and business there is proportion-
ately brisk. We would recommend Bulletin readers to
call and inspect the massive vaults, and ascertain terms
for storage. Safes may now be rented upon reduced
terms, and boxes are received for storage at very tuoder-
at rates.*

“ Asmodeus ”: To a recent pigeon-match at
St. Albans (about 40 miles from Windsor, N.S.W.) a boy,
who has never been away from the village in his life, took
a box of carrier pigeons’ and for every “ bird ” missed he
tossed a carrier in the air, which would entice the other
bewildered pigeon home to the boy’s pigeon-house, the
shoot-birds being strange to the locality. He thus secured
a lot of pigeons. No occasion to teach the young idea,
&c., down that way.

Copies, with testimonials, sent free to any colony
from the depot, 257 George-street, Sydney.*

Last year Bacon beat Deerfoot's record for one
hours running. Now Watkins has eclipsed Bacon’s
record ; also beating Cummings' 10-mile record by a
second during the journey. Cummings’ performance was
done in an actual match against \V. 0. George, whereas
Watkins was running time and was aided by pacers.
* * ♦ *
Black Tom Mitchell and white Tom Foy—each
credited with a long list of battles, of a sort—were
br ught together Saturday night by Golden Gate A.C.
(Sydney). Cognoscenti admired the club’s pluc-k in giv-
ing a fat purse to two such unreliables. Referee cau-
tioned them that there wouldn’t be a cent for either if it
was anything but real fight from the word go 700
people looked on. Foy, short, thick-set, muscular, stood
inches under the lank, lathy-limbed negro, who
about as if on wires. Mitchell sent straight jabs home
unerringly every time Foy led. None of the coon’s
punches were heavy, but they got there often. Now and
again Foy landed and made the dancing darkev wince
but, as a rule, his savage rights—and he relied on the
dexter member most—described parabolas in the air and
brought up against nothing. So matters progressed
very tame on the whole-till round 7, when white let
himself loose, and banging away fiercely with both hands,
gave black a direful time, but couldn’t get the settler
home. Ultimately, midway through the ninth 3mins.,
Foy cried a go, alleging that his arm was maimed. This
may have been correct, but writer thinks that the same
old weak spot asserted itself owing to the failure of the
onslaught two rounds previously.
* * * •
In three fight* at Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney)
last week, white opposed black; former (Watson, Smith
and Foy) were pitted against “ Warrigal,” chocolate God-
frey, ami Tom Mitchell respectfully. To-night, at same
place, black meets white again in the persons of Tom
Easier (“ The Kaffir ”) and Tom Cox, of W.A
* • • •
Monday next, at Golden Gate A.C., Sydney
Maorilander Otto Cribli will be up against what looks the
stilfest task yet set him, in the person of sturdy, deep-
chested, muscular Jack Tuekwell, of Riverstone (N.S.W.)
meat-works. 'Twill be a great night for the butchers,
who are sure to he on their champion to a man. Cribb
listens to all that is said re the man-eating capabilities of
his prospective adversary, and then quietly remarks—
“ I’ll collect the purse and stakes on Tuesday morning.”
Same night at West Wyalong (N.S.W ) iniddleweights
Jack Conlon and mine-manager Jack Goggin meet again.
Latter has been preparing in Melbourne under the crack
Vic. trainer Jack Marshall for a month or more. Conlon
—brimful of confidence in himself as a result of his
previous quick win—kept on laying the odds on pony
oourses and did the Cup instead of getting ready. Up
to -date, he has only put in two weeks’ work—hence
Goggin's chance doesn’t look too bad. There’ll be heaps
of coin for the miner and a good lump for the Sydneyite.
M.L. Tim Murphy (Vic. champion middle-
weight) is in Sydney, “ spoilin’ for a fight.” He has been
trailing his coat round—first among his own division, then
among the heavyweights of all the provinces, but nan-
one picks up the gage. Tut Ryan made a grab, anil
looked like toeing the mark, but he backed down, begging
to be excused. Meanwhile, at his wits' end how to raise
the wind for a trip Americawards, Timothy offers £1 for
every round any man—bar Bill Jennings', Peter Felix,
Bill Doherty and Mick Dunn—will stand up to him,
Murphy taking the “ gate ” if he triumphs.
• - • » •
Champion swimmer Lane continues “down
under.” At Shoreditch Baths (Eng.), 14th ult., he got
away with Thistle club’s I33yds. handicap in 1 min.
4-sths sec. ; but at Otter S.C. gala, when seeming to
have his heat of 100yds. Invitation Handicap well in
hand, lie eased off, allowing someone else to get home.
English swimming scribes say that, judged by the time
of the final, Lane couid have won with seconds to spare.
Later, Southern Swimming Association Council met, and
attention was drawn to the fact of a competitor at Otter
S.C. function not persevering in his heat; delegates de-
cided that offender should be carpeted. If Lane was re-
ferred to. it is any odds the matter was susceptible of
explanation—he was ill, or something else. Betting is
not likely to have been the influence, as very little of it
is done in swimming, and then only when champions
meet for stakes.

» * • *
Sydney’s experiment in sending an 8-oar crew,
rowing on sculling principles, to represent her in the
liiterprovincial race at Melbourne on Saturday last was a
failure, as Victoria at once took the lead and was not
afterwards troubled. It was thought before the race that
for a mile the N.S.W. eisiht might take the lead when the
exhausting style would be sure to bring them back, and
so it proved in their showing with Q. and South Aus-
tralia. After the first dash died out t hese latter drew up
level with N.S.W., and it was only when the latter
changed their style to a long steady stroke that they came
away and left the pair to fight it out. The Victorian
rowing was machine-like. In future, when experiments
in style are to be made, it would lie as well to try them
on other than ail liiterprovincial race.
In J. J. Daley, who won the amateur sculling
championship rowed after the 8-oar race, N.S.W. possesses
the best amateur Of his weight Australia has vet pro-
On Saturday his only opponent was the list. 101 b«
W. r. Slack, of Brisbane. Slack dashed away with the lead
but Daley, who did nob bustle himself in the least, soon
passed him and pot home 15 lengths to the good. Daley,
who is only a handful of a youngster—9st. 101 b.—is an
enthusiast who throws no chance away in his training,
and invariably conies to the post fit. Good judges give
him a great future at the game.

S'raft, and earned only the cheers of the crowd on the
pier.

The luck of the game was shown in bowling-
matches between N.S.W. premier club (City) and Vic-
torian clubs last week. M.C.C. beat the visiting 16 on
Monday by 3, despite the fact that latter secured a long
lead in the early part. Tuesday, Albert Park beat the
Sydneyites by 23, but Wednesday saw latter whip
J-leinington and have 30 to spare. Then came Prahran—
a match between N.S.W. and Vic. champion teams.
N.S.W. won, after a tussle, with 5 to the good.
• * * •
Secretary A. E. Butler, of Sydney champion
bowling club (City) died on Saturday, aged 72. He was
45 years a howler. Few better authorities on card-games
particularly whist and its offshoot, solo—existed. Under
the pen-name of “Old Reciprocity’’he solved and pro-
pounded d.d. problems of the stiffest character with coin-
parattve ease, and enjoyed the work. He was at Ballarat
m the stirring early fifties, and owned mining interests
there. Saw bruisers Harry Sallars, Joe Kitchen, Black
Douglas, Jack Carstairs and Dick Hunt get to it when in
their prime. By and by deceased was a butcher, in a big
way, and later held the post of city-treasurer at Rich-
mond (\ ic.). The land-boom collapse ultimately left him
stranded, with thousands of others.
• * * »
N.S.W.—Q. cricket match aroused little inter-
est, though the same old crowd gazed hour after hour at
the newspaper office scoring-board from the opposite side
of the street. Rain on Friday caused a postponement of
the beginning till next day, when 3000 spectators scat-
tered themselves about the ground—a£9o gate resulting.
Monday saw only 600 (all told) looking on. The cricket
in many instances was good, though it dragged weari-
somely at times. Previous match between these two
provinces was played in Brisbane two years ago, when
N.S.W. won by an innings and 101 runs. Trumper, hero
of present match so far, was the 12th man.
• * * *
There was no stonewalling about the two
century men (Trumper and Dorman) in N.S.W.’s innings
against Queensland on Sydney Cricket Ground last
Saturday. _ After luncheon both men were in the forties,
and the pair rattled up the score, run for run, till Donnan
was 9S to Trumper’s 96. But the latter was first to the
century, and then outpaced Donnan by running to 145,
whilst Donnan scored 113 and lost his wicket. Trumper
continued to score at a rapid pace until 208 stood to his
name, when he was caught by Campbell. Trlunper has
come hack from England a hard hitter—the most pro-
nounced difference in his play being that he now lifts the
hall where he used to send it along the carpet. When
nearing the half-century he gave two chances, but after
that the innings was faultless.
*'* • •
S.A. and Victoria meet in the first Sheffield
Shield match of this season on Adelaide Oval this Friday
(24th). If “paper” form is anything, S.A. should win.
Darling made 259 (not out) in his first club-match after
his return, I.yons has made two centuries, and Reedman
and F. Jarvis one each. Jones is in great bowling form,
hut Giffen is being thumped by club batsmen, and has
much gone off. Melb. batsmen have had such bad
weather that S.A. has a strong chance of catching them
out of form.
* ' • *
Failing a speedy settlement or compromise,
S.A. and N.S.W. will not meet each other at cricket this
season. The first match is invariably plaved at Adelaide
early in December. S.A.C.A. has decided to stand by its
latest offer (one-third of gross takings, less 15 per cent.),
and as both Associations are adamant and declare that
the other body is “ bluffing,’’ an early agreement looks
unlikely. If these two matches are lopped off the season
where are the rising Australian players to get their ex-
perience for next year’s battles with England ?
* • • *
Wardill returns to Australia determined.that
the next cricket-team doing England must have better
terms. Marylehone Club cleared £6OOO from the first
test-match—an eye opener. Many of the English county
clubs almost depend on the Australian matches for their
existence.
* • • •
Jim Phillips, the evergreen Melb. Club prof,
bowler and Australian umpire, has abandoned cricket
and is studying mining. He acted as scorer for the just-
returned Australian team.

Favorites for the big wheel-event of the year—
the Austral—are thick as Geo. Reid even now. Parsons
(90yds.) is among the best-fancied—a fair measure of his
old dash would almost see him through. Italian Boidi,
lately hack from W.A., improves on last season’s form,
arid with 80yds. should be dangerous. McCombe (N.S.W.),
140yds., will give trouble. R. W. Lewis, one of the first
batch (20yds. in front of the scratch division), is said to
be travelling at a greater rate and with more stamina
than ever. P. R. Beauchamp on 150yds. has prospects
second to none. Sutherland (the M.L. rider alongside
Lewis), plucky, speedy and level-headed, must be con-
sidered. Farley (120), Dawson (150), and Doyle (180),
look like leading the back-makers a merry dance.
“Piugger” Bill Martin (scr.) may work himself to the
front—but it will be a big task.
* * • «
Handicapper Dakin wrote to|V.R.C. commit-
tee, last week, asking for an inquiry into the running of
April Fool at the Cup meeting. On the Thursday (Oaks
Day), the handsome “Fool” finished nowhere‘in the
Carnival Handicap (1J mile), hut, two days later, he
made a holy show of a big field in the Final Handicap,
over the same distance. Mr. Dakin complains that “such
performances make the work of handicapping on equit-
able lines an impossibility,” hut the V.R.C. committee
is not likely to do anything to soothe his feelings. April
Fool’s sudden improvement was only a little more startling
than Parthian’s. In the case of Mr. Long’s colt, the
jockey who bestrode him when he was badly beaten on
Cup Day attributed Parthian’s failure to the greasy
going. The course was also somewhat greasy on the
Thursday, when Parthian won. Yet the explanation
was accepted. Nobody could deny that the greasness
had diminished in the meantime. April Fool’s jockey
trainer, and owner can plead much the same excuse!
The erratic animal was unable to do well on the rather
muddy course, wheieas he could travel at a great hat
when the ground had hardened a little, it may be said.
If the slight difference in the “ going” between Tuesday
and Saturday was considered sufficient to account for a
great difference in Parthian's form, the further slight
change which happened between Thursday and Saturday
must be accepted as an explanation of April Fool’s eccen-
tricities.
• • * •
A Melb. insurance man, who was expecting an
event, dreamt that he saw by his bedside a cradle of
twins, and he set to work to interpret the dream. He
finally decided, over a list of possible starters for Derby
and Cup, that Providence had given him the straight
wire, and he backed' Merriwee for the double, and
pocketed £5OO over the deal.
* * * *
The most cocksure man who has ever fluttered
on the Australian turf turned up at Remington on Derby
Day with a large yellow handbag, strapped over his
shoulder, in which to carry home his winnings. When
Caulfield Cup was run this sanguine young man was
clerking in a riverside establishment.. lie went to Caul-
field with no knowledge of horses or of the mysteries of
the ring, and backed every winner, bar One, converting
30s. into 450 in a few hours. Then the favorite of fortune
went incontinently balmy on betting, believing himself
inspired with an unearthly power of picking winners.
He took his £5O to Flemington, and his bag for the spoil,
and lost every cent he possessed on the first two races.
* * * *

his patriarchal whiskers, a monument of woe. Then an

“ The * Water Lily,’ as Mrs. Langtry De Bathe
is now called,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent, “lost the Csesarewitch of’99 assisted by Merman.
She intended to win with ihe old Melh. Cupper’s (Grand
Flaneur’s) progeny, and far and wide laid on all her
friends—they are now ‘laid out.’ Nightly, on the con-
clusion of ‘The Degenerates,’ has the fair owner sped by
special train to Newmarket to press a kiss of grease-paint
on Merman’s brow as he slept coiled in his manger with
lus head beneath his wing. At Kempton Park, in the
IJ4-mile October Plate, at 3 to 1 on, he led to the last
400yds., only to be beaten by St. Ja, and to fall from 9’s
to 25 sin the Caesarewiteh betting. But the money went
on, as the ‘Lily’ was confident still. Practically at Ihe
last moment Robinson, the trainer’s relative, was ordered
off the pigskin by the owner, who proposed that Charley
Wood should mount. Then the trainer otitspake : ‘lf
you do, out go your horses from my stable,’ and Lily
became the ‘ woman who didn’t ’—after a battle—and
Merman sauntered home third last, while his backers
took oaths re women-owners—and whiskies. The handi-
capping of the race was shown to he grandiloquently
idiotic, the winner (Scintillant, 7st.) and runner-up
(Ereildoune, 7st. 91b.) carrying 91b. and. 101 b. respectively
over their original weights. Mrs. Langtry has decided
not to write her‘Life.’ It is well —for if she did, 6he
wouldn’t.”

own race-meetings.
* * * *
Mao., though a councillor and secretary of the
local racing-club, has not “ had the advantages.” So
when a local horse-owner decided to call his neddy
“ Spondulix,” Mac. waxed indignant. “Spondulix be
hanged !” he said. “ Call him something that a man can
spelt.” “Well, how would Carbine do?” “That’s
better,” said Mac., and he serenely jotted down “ Kar-
byne.”
•‘• • •
At present about half the Adelaide population
is laid up with influenza, the other half rides a bike;
what is left devotes its time to tote-betting. According
to a local divine, little girls going to Sunday-school,
instead of discussing the iniquities of Sodom and Gomor-
rah, dilate at length on the respective chances of horses
for the next race. And pennies that should convert the
heathen float away to Ikey Mo or the machine.
* * * *
At a recent M.L. trot-meeting the driver of the
certainty in the big race wore a spectacle-looking
arrangement which stuck out about a foot in front of his
nose, said glasses being represented by two little mirrors
faced backwards so that the driver could see other com-
petitors behind him. He came in second.
* * * *
Van Bruggins’ Gravy Beef, heavily backed for
the chief race at a recent Smellbourne pony-meet, dead-
heated with de Smith’s Ginger, who was “ dead hut
hard to hold. Everyone knew that Ginger was “stiff,’’
and as he went out for the run-off, with his owner walking
beside him, the “books” gathered round and bawled to
the jock, “Lay yer five yer don’t win,” “a tenner yer
go down,” etc. “D’ye think they’ve livened him'?’,
enquired Van Bruggins, anxiously, when de Smith
returned. “Dunno!” said de Smith. “Don’t matter
much ; I’ve just sneaked a hunk of lead out of his hag.
Nothin’ like luttkin’ a cert ot it.” The hooks loudly
snorted applause when Ginger won, hard-held, but they
tore their hair and groaned when he was declared “short
weight,” and the race given to Gravy Beef. “ Nothing
like making a cert of it.”
* * • *
One Sydney tote-runner has, in seven years,
paid £ll6O in fines and court costs (to say nothing of his
personal legal expenses, &e.) for breaches of the anti-
gauibling law.
* * * *
While the world’s premiership fight between
Fitzsimmons and .Jeffries was progressing in New York,
the various blows anil situations were recorded on
screens in front of ’Frisco newspaper offices thousands of
miles away, and ’Frisco Examiner had two top-notch
bruisers in the office being photo’d in probable positions
when any particular punch was delivered. London,
Glasgow, and N.Y. journals went one better over re- •
cent Goliimbia-Shamrock drifting-matches. All worked
together; 50 experts flashed every movement of vessels to
various offices. A swift yacht was chartered to follow
the “race,” paying out loose cable connected with At-
lantic submarine wires. On eminences of Coney Island
and New Jersey, reporters stood with powerful telescopes.
London, Glasgow, and N.Y. papers, concerned in the
compact, gave shifting illustrations (termed “ Cincyach-
tographe”) of the contest as it proceeded. Evening
News screen on Thames Embankment; was viewed by
40,000 at one time. N. Y. sea-board and a stretch of the
Atlantic were depicted, yachts travelled to the stake-
boat and back in grooves cut on the screen; Shamrock
had green, and Columbia red lights.
The Glory of Man.
The crown and glory of manhood is strength—
power. All human conquests have been won by
it; individual and collective. The earliest sign of
decay in man is not usually pain, but weakness of
some kind—of mind, or body, or both. This may
be the inevitable sequence of advanced years, or
it may, as we all know, show itself at any period
of life. But it always means that the sources of
strength are failing, as the lessening of the water
in a river means a drying up of the springs and
streams which feed it.
I said “sources” of strength, using the plural
word. I should have said “ source ” of strength,
for in human beings there is but one. What is it ?
Perhaps the experience of Mr. David Jones will
help us to an answer.
“ In the summer of 18S1,” he says, “ I began to
feel ill and out of sorts. I was weak and tired
and quite worn out with little exertion. I had a
poor appetite, and after eating had pain and
weight at the chest. My secretions were scanty
and thick. My back felt stiff and gave me much
pain as I moved about. For three years I suf-
fered like this, being at times better and then
worse. I consulted a doctor, who gave me medi-
cines and recommended Turkish baths ; but I got
no better for any kind of treatment.
“ My brother then told me about Mother Sei-
gel’s Curative Syrup, but I had no faith in adver-
tised medicines, lie, however, urged me to take
it, and after taking a few doses 1 experienced so
much relief that I continued with it,and was soon
strong and well as ever. Since that time I take a
dose of this remedy when I feel at all ailing, and
it sets me right. 1 have praised Mother Seigel's
Syrup to all my friends, many of whom have
tried it and been benefited by it. You can pub-
lish this statement if you like. (Signed) David
Jones, 1 Dunlace-street, Walton, Liverpool, July
31, 189 G.”
We have room for one more short letter before
we try to come at the answer to the question with
which we set out. It is exactly in the same line,
and ought to make the results of our enquiry all
the more clear and plain.
“ In the spring of 1888,” proceeds the writer,
“my health began to fail me. I felt languid and
heavy, as if something had come over me. I had
a bad taste in the mouth, my appetite was poor,
and the little food I took gave me pain across the
chest and between the shoulders. My food lay
like lead on my stomach. I was constantly
spitting up a thick phlegm, and when in bed
heavy sweats came over me. I got weaker and
weaker, and from time to time was confined to
bed. Often I was so bad I could not dress or un-
dress myself.
“Now better, now worse, but never well, I
suffered for over five years. I took different kinds
of medicines, and had a doctor, but got no real
benefit from anything.
“ In July, 1893, a hook was left at my house in
which I read of cases like mine having been cured
by Mother Siegel’s Syrup. I got a bottle from
the International Tea Company, Yeovil, and be-
gan taking it. In a short time-1 felt much benefit,
and, by continuing with it, gained strength. I
could eat better, food agreed with me, and by-and-
by I was strong and well. But for Mother Seigel's
Syrup I believe 1 should not now be alive. You
are at liberty to publish this statement. (Signed)
(Mrs.) Jane Cronel, 82 Huish, Yeovil, November
29th, 1895.
What, then, do these cases show to be the source
of strength? The answer is, Digested Food. That
and nothing else. All strength, of body or of
mind, comes from that, and that only. What,
then, is the great enemy of strength, the fountain
of weakness? Indigestion—dyspepsia. You see
why and how. A child could not miss the argu-
ment. What, then, restores strength ? Mother
Seigel’s Syrup. In what way? By setting the
digestive machinery in healthy operation. That
is what it did for our correspondents and does
daily for multitudes. Use it if you need it, and
pass the news to other weak ones.

A Sydney milkman was recently fined for
vending milk not up to Board of Health stan-
dard, the magistrate remarking that “ he was
perfectly satisfied that the milk was supplied
just as it came from the cow, hut as it did not
carry the prescribed butter-fat contents he had
no option but to inflict a penalty.” This
decision has now been upset by the unanimous
ruling of the Full Court. Chief Justice Daki.ky
said that “ he was of opinion on the whole case
that the magistrate was wrong in holding that
when a man simply asked for milk and got
milk as it came from the cow the vendor was
liable to be convicted of any offence. If a pur-
chaser asked for milk of the standard fixed by
the Board of Health and did not get it, that
might be a very different matter.” And yet to
the simple lay mind it would appear that any
milk below the Board standard is not milk
within the meaning of the Act, and vendor
should therefore be punishable. If a standard is
fixed, which no cow can be expected to main-
tain, then it should be lowered ; but if it is fair
and reasonable then vendors should be kept
right up to the mark and so compelled to dis-
card worthless cattle. Anyhow, the public
wants a law which will work that way.

Melb. Age, on the precious Jackson case
report: —
As we anticipated from the evidence, the report com-
pletely exonerates Mr. Rf.st from the charges brought
against him A Parliamentary tribunal was ap-
pointed to inquire into Mr. Graves's charges, and he
himself was made a member of it. . . From that enquiry
Mr. Best has emerged w it.hout a stain on his character.
Melb. Argus on the same report :
But the minor charge that the brother-in-law was
favored in the department, remains. ..... There was
something both reprehensible and extraordinary In the
swift promotion of a telegraph operator to a position in
the hands Office requiring much experience in the details
-of administration. . . . How-far was the Minister respon-
sible fur this “ blunder,” or, as some would put it, act of
favoritism? Mr. Outtrim's draft report declared that
the Minister’s retention of Mr. Jackson in t he position for
nearly three years, despite protest and discontent, was
“an act of bail administration.” Had Mr. Graves voted,
as he was legally entitled to do, the vote would have been
three against three, and then the vote of the Chairman
(Mr. Outtrim ) in support of his opinion would have made
it four against three. So the Minister has been saved
from an adverse finding by the self-imposed restraint of
his accuser.

William Wilkinson Wardell, architect of
the two cathedrals of St. Patrick’s, Melbourne,
and St. Mary’s, Sydney, died the other day at the
age of 70. His talent' and services were referred
to in a recent BULLETIN article.. Coming to Aus-
tralia as a young man, his life despaired of by
leading London doctors, be found bis life-work
before him. For twenty years he was bead
of Vic. Public Works department, and he
.designed many of the chief buildings in that
province. Fur forty years he watched the progress
of his two cathedrals to their stage of temporary
completion, and he has left behind him all the
plans for consummating his designs. In virtue of
achievement, Mr. Wardell was by far the most
eminent architect who has lived in Australia, and
his professional zeal and genius would have given
him high rank anywhere.

ANOTHER RADICAL CURE.
To Convince Sceptical Readers or Unbelievers.
IMITATORS CLA-IN" COPY BUT BIYAL.
Dyspepsia and Neuralgia Promptly Cured.
MRS,
Th® G&®© of
. ELIZA!
{Reported for “ The Redfern Times.”)
Within gunshot of Redfern Railway
terminus our reporter found the residence
of Mrs. Elizabeth Phipps, at 418£ Elizabeth-
street, Surry Hills. This lady was at home,
and, in answer to enquiry, freely gave
an account of her recovery from a serious
case of anaemia and distressing neuralgia.
My general health has not been good
since I was a child,” said Mrs. Phipps. “ I
was not strong, and some months ago I got
’•'to an exceptionally low state. My diges-
tion became impaired ; I lost my appelate,
and had no taste for food I had, at times,
a sharp pain between the shoulders and in
the left side, but my most distressing
symptoms were neuralgic pains that, coming
up my neck, spread all over my head and
face with unbearable pain. At last this
pain became so serious that it affected my
eyes ; they became weak and dazed. This
was followed by a nervous, trembling pain
in the arms, especially the right arm, so
that I thought 1 was going to be paralysed.
-My husband tried to laugh me out of my
fears. I noticed that the neuralgia pains
were worse in hot weather, and always came
on towards sundown, and, continuing in the
night, robbed me of sleep. Towards
morning t hese pains would ease, but only to
return again the next night. Sometimes,
when I tried to lie down, suffering from this
terrible neuralgia, my throat would seem to
swell, my breathing became difficult, and I
leib as though someone was trying to
smother me ihis feeling was so distressing
that even if I could have gone to sleep I
telb my breathing would have ceased.
-Naturally, I was distressed, and in very
low spirits.” J
“ What remedies did you try, Mrs.
■1 mpps ? enquired the reporter.
I took a mixture from the chemist’s
and advertised preparations, but they were
far from improving my general health, and
very soon my neuralgic pains returned as
badly as before.”
“ 'V bat did you try next ?”
“By accident I came across one of the
pamphlets of Clements Tonic, and, on read-
ing the testimonials, I found the names of
several persons I knew This gave me
confidence. I knew what was said was
genuine, and I became convinced that
Clements Tonic would suit my case and
cure me. This is how I came to purchase
the first bottle. ”
“ And vvliat was the result, Mrs. Phipps ? ”
“ After taking the fourth dose, that is,
on the second day—for I took Clements
Tonic three times a day—l noticed a decided
change for the better, and as I continued
to take it the improvement was perceptible
and constant I know it was Clements
Tonic that did me good, for, owing to our
changing fiouse and being Very busy, I
neglected for a short time to take Clements
Tonic, and I found that during that time
my cure was retarded The first and most
welcome sign I had that Clements Tonic
was doing me good was the return of my
appetite. I could eat my meals ; and, after
that, all my other pains went away ; or,
rather, they did not come again, as they
used to do, particularly the terrible pains in
my face and head that had come every night
so regularly and made sleep impossible.
Only last week I recommended a friend of
mine, who is in a very low state of health
from overwork, to take Clements Tonic,
and already it is doing her good.”
‘ Have you any objection, Mrs. Phipps,
to your experience being made public ?”
“ Not at all. You can freely add my
name to the numbers of those I know who
cannot speak too well of Clements Tonic.
It is a grand medicine.”

Statutory
Declaration.

street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows: —
Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.
Whereas I, as a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rass-
mcssen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,
which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COM-
PLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and although
this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
THOMAS MUDIMAN.

Government Notices.
Proposed Sites for the Federal
Capital.
TSERSOXS desirous of bringing under the notice of the
Commissioner any area as a suitable site to be
ceded to the Commonwealth for the Seat of Government,
are invited to forward their suggestions, in writing, ad-
dressed to the Registrar of the Land Appeal Court. Syd-
ney, if possible before 31st December proximo.
Such communications should afford as full and accurate
Information on the subjects hereunder set forth as can
be obtained.
I. CLIMATIC CONDITIONS.
(a) Range and mean of temperature during each quarter
of the year, beginning with January.
(f>) Altitude above sea level, and mean altitude of the
area as a whole.
(c) Rainfall.
11. ACCESSIBILITY.
(a) By railway (existing or to be constructed).
(I>) By road.
(c) By water.
The conditions under this head should deal with the
approaches to any proposed site as from the several con-
stituent States of the Commonwealth, and distances, in
miles, from approximate centre of area to the Capital
Cities of such States should be given.
111. PHYSICAL CONDITIONS.
(a) Nature of soil.
it) Water supply and catchment.
c) The possession of, or proximity to, stone, timber, aid
other building material.
(d) Drainage.
(e) Other physical features.
IV. OWNERSHIP AND VALUE.
(a) Area of alienated or private lands.
(6) Area of Crown Lands (including Church and Schoa
Lands, reserves, &o.)
(c) Estimated value of private lands (unimproved).
(d) Estimated value of private lands (with existing im-
provements).
V. MISCELLANEOUS CONDITIONS, &o.
(a) Character of neighboring country, having regard to
1. Facilities for food supply.
2. Mineral products, especially coal.
3. Capacity to support a considerable population.
4. Conditions favorable to commercial and industrial
development.
Upon the receipt of such suggestions, the Commis-
sioner will as soon as possible appoint limes and places for
the holding of public inquiries, at which evidence will be
taken, and due notice by letter will be given to persons
who may intimate their wish to give evidence, as well as
by advertisement in the local newspapers. Attention is
invited to Clause 125 of the Commonwealth Bill.
125. The seat of Government of the Commonwealth
shall be determined by the Parliament and shall be
within territory which shall have been granted or
acquired by the Commonwealth and shall be vested
in and belong to the Commonwealth and if New
South Wales be an original State shall be in that
State and be distant not less than one hundred miles
from Sydney. Such territory shall contain an area
of not less than one hundred square miles, and such
portion thereof as shall consist of Crown lands shall
be granted to the Commonwealth without any pay-
ment therefor.

The working-jewellers of Melbourne are agi-
tating fora good hall-mark on precious-metal goods. The
same thing is badly needed in N.S.W., where goods
marked by the makers, 9, 15 and 18-carat, often contain
hardlv any gold. Writer saw a ring tested recently.
It was marked 15c., hut the test showed it to contain
about one-fourth gold.

returns over 0 per cent, on capital cost of the houses, and
they were built in boom-time.

“Frank R.”: An utterly neglected question,
affecting the public health, is the trade done at Melbourne
in connection with “slinks” —the technical name for die
bodies of very young calves. The calves are knocked on
the head as soon as born, and sold to the lowest class of
butchers—or “scavengerswho turn them into “Ger-
mans,” “poloneys,” Frankforts, and soon. All is fish that
comes to the nets of these wretches, who snap tip
even unborn calves taken from dead mothers, and
who haunt the various saleyards for this purpose. Owing
to the growth of the dairying industry this nefarious
trade is spreading, and 1 saw some slinks the other day
put on hoard a train 100 miles from Melbourne, and con-
signed to a “ butcher.” I am going to Melbourne myself
shortly—hence these tears.
It is now a trick of well-dressed dead-beats to
make pretty certain of two square meals a day by calling
at boarding-houses about accommodation just as the hell
rings for a meal. The inquirer is, of course, satisfied with
the place and terms, and the landlady can hardly do less,
in the circumstances, than invite the prospective new
lodger to “ join them.”

“ Tas.” : Worked in Launceston (Tas.) once for
a publican who used to “break down” his rum with
honey and water. He was locally renowned for rum ;
and when, ou one occasion, the excise officers took a
sample for analysis the analyst’s report made no mention
of honey. But there are many kinds of analyst.

“ Ben.” : Some phases of Vie. railway manage-
ment are wonderful examples of business capacity.
Tenders are called for firewood for stations in Jots of 800
tons or so, with the result that a sweating contractor
si metimes trucks wood from a sparsely-timbered locality
into a forest. If orders were given locally where wood
was available there would be a saving anil a crumb for
local splitters.

“ C.F.” : Is this a brand-new fake? The other
night in George-street I struck an apparent sea-faring
man, looking half-seas over, who asked me the way to
Balmain ferry. Started to show him, and presently he
stooped and picked up an envelope, which turned out to
contain a 3-stone gold ring wrapped in well-known jewel-
ler's receipted hill for 355. The “sailor-man ” was unfor-
tunately sailing next day, he said, and the reward would
be at least 10 s. Consequence was we parted at the ferry;
he with my half-sov. Next clay 1 made inquiries, and
now for a single boh that simple mariner can have his ring
back !

“Stylus”: Was recently asked hy a friend to
buy him a long cane. Called at first coy-and-lolly-shop.
“ Don't keep canes,” replied the woman, quickly, in
answer lo request; “if I did, children wouldn’t come
and buy of me.” Is boycotting a developed trait of the
juvenile mind, dating from the famous Dish episode, or
is it a natural form of protection, practised unconsciously
and instinctively ?

“ C.TI.” : “ Old Stoney’s” statement (B. 26/8/’99)
that town-employers are forced to attend to sanitary
arrangements vvili be news to many. Writer, when broke,
once worked in a Sydney restaurant, and slept with
others in a room 18x10ft., lift, high, and below the street-
level. The kitchen was adjoining, and the fragrant pig-
tub at the door. Imagine it in summer ! Wages, 10s.
a-week and tucker, for 14 hours' work a-day. Also, a
night in the “glory-hole” of some of the coasting
steamers would he more than any pig could stand. The
bushtuau is no worse off, after all, than the town or sea-
grafter. » * »

“ E.D. ” : Rubbish-sorting doesn’t seem too
bad a game. Know four men who pick over the Moore

“Wooli”: Re “Sheoak” on Tweed River
illicit stills. The industry also flourishes on the Clarence.
Some time ago a visitor to the wild part of the district
accidentally dropped across three stills in one day while
searching for a friend, and no attempt at secrecy was
made hy those in charge. In same locality the police
failed to find even one, allhough they searched a house
in which a “ worm ” was packed in a box. Two years ago
the police, “acting on information,” discovered a still in
working order, with all accessories, in a canefield adjoin-
ing the town of Maclean, within 200yds. of the main road
and less than a mile from the police-station. At a later
date a local white lady sued her spouse at a district
police-court for assault, and deposed that hubby’s quarrel-
someness was the result of chronic indulgence in spirit
made by him in his house in town.

As Germans have of late taken to figuring
largely on the hooks of the Australian Patents Hepart-
ments, it is worth noting that the Govt, of the Fatherland
grants no patent unless a guarantee he given that the
articles concerned shall he made there. Further, if there
he no local workmen aide to manufacture the articles tlie
patentee must import workmen who will thus spend the
wages in Ihe country and teach the new industry to
Teutons besides.

“Jack Shay” ; To detect sixpenny kerosene
try tfiis racket, one often worked on the pirate Flossie
who sub-rents a private bar. Most men have a particular
fancy in kerosene ; some like Johson's Special, others
M'Malt’s V. 0., or u’llarley’s iievil's Own, and so on. Hut
judges, as a rule, fight shy of Uur Own Special Very Old
Matured in the Wood served out of a cut-glass decaliter,
If you favor Johson’s Special, when you catch hold of Ihe
bottle scratch the label with your nail, and take a note of
the mark. Next day, or next week, or tlie ensuing half-
year, if you're having Johson’s Special again, notice the
label. Have seen a marked bottle doing duty for a week
in a house that claimed to sell in that time 10 to 12 dozen
of that particular brand. Gave that house best.

“Straight Griffin”: The guinea-pig director
not being quite so frequent ns of yore, investors have a
new trouble— that of guinea-pig secretaries. Some of
these with only the mental equipment of the average
clerk, have secured many companies' secretaryships, and
with the secretaryships an absolutely fictitious import-
aiice. The actual legal management is done by a staff of
clerks, and the apparent secretary lias little knowledge
of the working of his companies beyond the fact
that he gels the earliest information from the
mines, and uses that information in transactions on
’Change. Also, in many cases, directors are not eon-
suited aliout printing-orders, and, as a general rule, no
tenders for printing are called, and i fie favorite printer of
anv particular office gets the work at his own price.
Thus money is thrown away in exorbitant legal nianage-
ment charges, while the mine-manager is cutting to
the bone actual mining expenses. A friend of mine—a
one-time legal manager—told me recently that when
very young and green to the business lie was ap-
proaelied by a printer who offered him taeafy-
five per cent, commission on the gross amount of
all printing accounts ! The young secretary accepted (his
am! for some months was paid the proportion quoted.
Finding the charges became hot as mustard the seere-
tary shut down on the game, and although throat-
ened by the printer, was mail enough thereafter to submit
his printing orders to competition. Directors should in-
sist on printing being let by annual tenders on a fixed
price for scrip, balance sheets, reports, circulars, &c.,
oth envise in nine cases out of ten they are tacitly agree-
ing to a fraud on shareholders. Yet another swindle is
solicitor’s costs for “drawing up” and printing articles of
association. Tiiere is plenty of swindling in mining by
St only 1 th^begi in nhnj of' a

A young fellow entitled to a bequest of .£5OO
under his father’s will, came of age a few years ago, hut
on consulting the trustee co. managing his affaire was in-
formed that the estate would not stand the
oNvniir to the depreciation of values. A conference witn
the rest of the family led to their guaranteeing him a loan
of £5OO, which the trustee 00. advanced at 8 percent.
Years passed, and t’other day the girls got an auditor to
go through everything to ascertain their position. The
man of figures was received affably by the trustee co.,
anil on miming though llie paper* be discovered that
the lost £r>oo lay in the local iiai ings Bank, untouched
ami forgotten. At t lie lime of the banking collapse the
trustee co. had carefully drawn it from us regular in-
stitution and lodged it in the Sa» iugs Bank for safety,
and lud then proceeded to forget it. And it was just
because the forgotten boon was taken out of the estate
that there wasn't sufficient estate left lo meet the claim.
Meanwhile, Hie young man has been paying s per cent, for
the use of Ins own money.
