“ Bar.” : lie crust struggles. Once, when beam-
ended, answered Sydney advi.—“ Clerical vacancy, £2 a
week ; apply,” &c. Ki\e minutes before the time found
Ou applicants wailing; gave my last shilling lo the jani-
tor, slipped into the front office (vacant), and, taking off
iny bat. explained to the crowd that a gentleman bad
been engaged. Two minutes later employer arrived,
found me the only applicant, gave uie the job, and talked
for weeks afterwards about ‘‘how well things must be
going with clerks when only one turned up for a £2 job.”
Another lime applied for clerkship—no wages specified;
85 applicants. Passed in at fronl door, through manager’s
room, and out into side street. Told to come hack three
hours later. Did so, found 22. Drafted through again,
reduced to 12; last draft reduced to four, including
myself. Having the best references, 1 was asked the
lowest I would lake. Said 80s. “ Unit, you're mad!”
said ibe manager. “ Here is a man who will come for
AT." I left, without charging anything for being drafted
like a prize merino.

Sydney pub. Sunday-trading isn’t all gilt. A

Swamp up a drink each, and refuse to pay. The publican

one Bung was the victim of about the smartest Sunday

for the lot—lB drinks —and got a bottle of three-star 10
take out. Altogether 1 Is., for which Bung cashed a fiver.
Sharp lingered a bit, shouted again, and then was let

not one of your mean men, that would get one pinched
for a threepenny drink. With a few more of hi# sort I
could stand a line.” But, on Monday -iiing, the liver
came back from the bank branded “ Forgery."

Many people spoke highly of President Kruger. lie
believed lie was a good man, a son of gqodOld Testament
man.
A good Old Testament man, mark you ! All very
well in his way, of course, but not the new, up-to-
date, quick-firing religion which discharges (JGO
shots a minute while you turn a handle. Rev.
Allen further remarked that—
lie believed that England was called to take up th»

native races.
Britain has uplifted a good many nativo races,
too. Some of them that were uplifted as much

must have been uplifted very high. And since
the invention of the lyddite shell Britain can
elevate native races faster than ever.

Tlie After Effects of
INFLUENZA AND TYPHOID
Can be Permanently Removed by
Dr. Williams’ Pii)k Pills
which will also ward off these diseases altogether IF TAKEN IN TIME. Influenza, like
typhoid, is an epidemic fever. When you experience a severe cold in the head and chest, heavy
headaches, rheumatic pains in back and limbs, digestive disorder—influenza is coming. Symptoms
of approaching typhoid are pains in limbs and back, increasing weakness, headaches and fever at
night, loss of appetite. In either case Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills used NOW will purify your
blood, banish the headaches and feverishness, remove digestive troubles and pains, and make you
STRONG AND WELL in place of being a prostrate victim to these dangerous and lowering
diseases. The cases below (two out of many) prove it.
Influenza’s Dangerous Effects
Banished.
Miss Jamieson, of Autumn Street, Ashby, Geelong,
Vic., is a young lady who has thoroughly proved the
value of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills in curing the after-
effects of influenza. Miss Jamieson’s case was par-
ticularly severe. “ Although,” she says, “ I generally
experienced robust health, I became a victim to in-
fluenza. A severe cold in the head and chest first
attacked me, then
Miss Jamieson.
feverishness came
on, I began to feel
depressed, severe
headaohes attacked
me, and aoh i n g
pains in the back
and limbs were also
preseut. My diges-
tion was disturbed,
and I became
weaker as the

When at last I was
able to leave my
room I was so thin
and debilitated that
my friends hardly
knew me. I never
got strong again,
and sometime
afterwards pains in my right side became chronic. I
always felt weary, and too weak and depressed to
take pleasure in exercise and in those duties which I
had always performed light-heartedly before. A
physician whom I called in said it was the after-
effects of influenza, and I was not to worry ; but the
severe pains continued, and I did worry. I even
went to Melbourne to consult a specialist. He said
it was partial paralysis, and put me under treatment
which did not cure me. A week afterwards erysipelas
set in on my face, which became tense and painful,
and for several months I suffered the greatest agony,
becoming weaker day by day. At last a friend recom-
mended Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills to me. A few days
after using them the pains began to lessen. I con-
tinued with them until quite cured, and am now

Saved from the Scourge of
Typhoid.
West Australia, of all the oolonies, may be said to

The Rovers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Some born of homely parents
For ages settled down—
The steady generations
Of village, farm and town ;
And some of dusky fathers
Who wandered since the flood—
The fairest skin or darkest
Might hold the roving blood.
Some born of brutish peasants,
And some of dainty peers,
In poverty or plenty
They pass their early years ;
But, born in pride of purple,
Or straw and squalid sin,
In all the far world corners
The wanderers are kin.
A rover or a rebel,
Conceived and born to roam,
As babies they will toddle
With faces turned from home ;
They’ve fought beyond the vanguard
Wherever storm has raged,
And home is but a prison
They pace like lions caged.
They smile and are not happy ;
They sing and are not gay;
They weary, yet they wander;
They love, and cannot stay ;
They marry, and are single
Who watch the roving star,
For, by the family fireside,
Oh, lonely men they are 1
They die of peaoe and quiet—
The deadly ease of life ;
They die of home and comfort;
They live in storm and strife;
No poverty can tie them,
Nor wealth nor place restrain —
Girl, wife, or child might draw them,
But they’ll be gone again 1
Across the glowing desert;
Through naked trees and snow ;
Across the rolling prairies
The skies have seen them go.
They fought to where the ocean
Receives the setting sun ;
And, where shall fight the rovers
When all the lands are won ?
They thirst on Greenland snowfields,
On Never-Never sands;
Where man is not to conquer
They conquer barren lands ;
They feel that most are cowards,
That all depends on “ nerve,”
They lead who cannot follow,
They rule who cannot serve.
Across the plains and ranges,
Away across the seas,
On blue and green horizons
They camp by twos and threes ;
They hold on stormy borders
Of states that trouble earth
The honor of the country
That only gave them birth.
Unlisted, uncommissioned,
Untaught of any school,
In far-away world corners
Uneonquered tribes they rule j
The lone hand and revolver—
Sad eyes that never quail—
The lone hand and the rifle
That win where armies fail.
They slumber sound where murder
And treachery are bare—
The pluck of self-reliance,
The pluck of past despair.
Thin brown men in pyjamas—
The thin brown wiry men 1
The helmet and revolver
That lie beside the pen.
Through drought and desolation
They won the way Out Back ;
The commonplace and selfish
Have followed on their track ;
They conquer lands for others,
For others find the gold,
And where shall go the rovers
When all the lands are old ?
A rover and a rebel —
And so the worlds commence 1
Their hearts shall beat as wildly
Ten generations hence ;
And when the world is crowded—
’Tis signed and sealed by Fate —
The roving blood will rise to make
The countries desolate.
H. LAWSON.

Q .eensland at the end of ’9B was 8485. The
number introduced was 1181 for the year, and the
number returned 847. Deaths, 202. The

form of clothing, lamps, axes, etc. When will Q.

and half-caste question ?

The Lash.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A MORNING of cloud-ripping breezes and tri”mph-
ant sunlight; a morning to open the heart and let
its pent-up joyance fly in the face of convention ;
a new, untarnished folio of a day.
Passing out from the nerve-bound city, we
paused to admire the expanding unhedged country,
and then turned with a sigh to the gates of the
prison—funereal and forbidding by contrast. The
gum-trees growing on the roadside had life in
them ; they flung their hardy glistening foliage
gaily to the marching wind ; but on the bleak
immobile wall-face, the sunlight turned to a sickly
gray. Around the wall’s abrupt angles and
breaks the wind lost its happy tone and moaned
like a lost spirit. The ugly, stubborn bulk of
masonry rose suddenly before us like a rubbled
and buttressed materialisation of that whioh
always comes between man and ideality.
They passed us through with a convincing
parade of guards, carbines, padlocks and brogue,
and we saw between the files of our blue-coated
Hibernian escort two grass-plots set with military
exactitude in the centre of the hard-paved yard.
Here, two thin, sour-faced convicts, presided over
by a warder with a gun, were shaving off the
scanty verdure with scythes. One of them was in
irons, and the clank of his leg-chains when he
stepped upon the pavement was a grim and
ominous sound.
Preceded by a large, imposing official, we
passed through a wicket, which was opened for
us with a lever by the sentinel above—keeping all
the time his rifle in his disengaged hand and his
eye on the universe. Crossing a bare, gravelled
yard, we entered a long bluestone building, and
were conducted to the prison-library to wait. It
was a small, stuffy room, surrounded by unplea-
sant religious books—the table littered with tracts
and missionary literature. Not an enlivening
place 1
For a time there was an eloquently dungeon-
like quietude ; then footsteps sounded in the cor-
ridor—the jangle of keys and the turning of a
lock. “ That,” said my companion, “is the man
who is to be flogged in private. He has been on
the triangles before ; they say the lash cuts fright-
fully the second time. Besides ”
He was interrupted by a long, mufflled wail,
which oozed in through the half-dosed door, and
set my hair a-bristling. Others followed in quick
succession. I longed to close the door, but some-
how didn’t like to move. My friend also kept his
seat, and there we remained grinning painfully at
eaoh other till the awful echoing monotone ceased
and a warder came to lead us to the torture-
chamber.
I felt a growing abhorrence as we passed into
the corridor, with its austere walls of painted
stone, and grim, grated cell-doors, and when we
entered the flogging-room this feeling intensified
to a sickening degree. I trembled at the
thought of what we were about to wit-
ness. My every sense was nervously quick-
ened, and I actually smelt the pain-wrung
sweat of the victim who had just been removed.
It was a small room with whitewashed walls and
board-floor; its only furniture a table with an
electric battery on it and the “ triangles ” —a
wooden tripod with cross-bars on one side. Pre-
sently two men, wearing false beards (the hang-
man and his assistant), led in a man with a
hideous canvas-mask over his head. This they
removed and revealed a low-browed, brutish face.
He was a young man, and his crime had been a
loathsome one—cruel, motiveless and unaccount-
able. Yet something in the helplessness of his
condition appealed strongly for pity. He stood,
while his sentence was being read, glancing
shiftily at one and another of his custodians, and
anon hopelessly at the grim torture-frame and the
half-dozen “ cats ” placed on the floor conveni-
ently near the flagellator’s hand.
As they bared the prisoner’s back the officials
spoke in half whispers, but as soon as the subject
was strapped to the frame with arms and feet
spread wide, one called out with startling loud-
ness “Fifteen lashes!” Immediately the flagel-
lator stepped forward, whirled the knotted thongs
once around his head and brought them whistling
down across the white shoulders. The stroke was
dealt with the precision of long practioe, and the
victim, taken by surprise, caught his breath with a
gasp and strained desperately at the unreleasing
bonds, the muscles of his shoulders and arms
quivering convulsively in the effort to free his
limbs and get one solacing writhe under the
sudden, unavoidable, tormenting sting. Fail-
ing this, the wretch threw back his head and
screeched forth the pent and raging resenfc-
ment of his body, with an intonation hideous,
heart-piercing and unforgettable. The sound was
comparable to nothing else in nature : it ex-
pressed all that is meant by despair and mortal
other strokes followed in orderly mechani-
cal sequence, and I watched them every one. At
each stripe the tortured wretch howled anew, but
above his screams could be heard the shouts of the
man whose duty it was to count the strokes, and
—between —the vicious “sing” of lash and
its gruesome “ splash ” upon the wealed flesh. At
the ninth stroke the doctor ordered the hangman
to vary the direction of his blows. He did so,
after staying to run his fingers through the clotted
lashes and to flick the gouted blood from them
upon the floor. The five last strokes were punc-
tuated only by deep, rhythmic sobs from the victim,
who now seemed to be numbed and stupefied by
pain.
When it was finished, they covered up his face
and body, and took him quickly away to the
hospital.

Cables in Australian papers told us that
the Cape Government were helping the Boers to
arm, and that a heavy oase of goods, consigned
over the Cape railways to the 1' ree State, was
found to contain rifles. But they didn’t tell us
that, on further inquiry, this case was found to
contain only private letter-boxes for the new post-
office at Lucahoff. Also, they told us that shots
were fired at some Hussars before war was de-
clared. But they didn't tell us that, on further
inquiry, it was ascertained that the supposed rifle
shots were only the report of some blasts in a
Natal Govt, quarry. These corrections arrived
gradually and very obscurely by mail
“ Piripi” on tree-planting in the streets :
It should be made a criminal offenoe to plant dark-
foliaged trees in the environs of Sydney. The mono-
tonously-dark tone of the natural bush wants relieving
with lighter tints, but the tendency has been to go on
putting in firs, and pines, and Moreton Bay figs, until the
sameness of sombre color almost incites to suicide. Then
the aldermen who have been to Paris bring away with
them a mind-picture of the Boulevards, in which the
plane-tree predominated—a tree charming enough in
France, but not too suitable to the Australian climate,
and, at any rate, being deciduous, an eyesore and a
nuisance for nearly half the year. So, simian imitative-
ness is threatening us with an epidemic of plane-trees.
The best tree for street-planting, in present writer's
opinion, is the camphor laurel —in leaf all the year round,
showing the loveliest shades of living green, delightful
in perfume, unequalled as a shade tree, and having soft,
accommodating roots that will not upheave footways and
disintegrate walls.
